THE RED PAGE.
A. Doctor’s Confessions*
Some three years ago a Russian doctor of medicin
Y. Veresaeff, published a remarkable book (now tran
lated into English as The Confessions of a
He pictured a doctor as he may see himself, divested o
mysterious “etiquette” and “the dignity of th
profession.” His sincerity is manifest. He tells, fo
example, of hospitals and dissecting-rooms.
We, the representatives of the most humane o:
the sciences, are compelled to trample the mos
elementary humanity under foot. Availin
itself of the inability of the poor to pay fo
medical aid, our school takes .advantage of the;
need and converts the sick into dummies to b
used for the purpose of study, ruthlessly settin
at naught woman’s shame, adding to the sorrow
of the bereaved mother by “desecrating” he
dead child.
I am quite at a loss to suggest any practicab
way out of this dilemma ; all I know is, that w
cannot do without medicine, and that no othe
way of acquiring knowledge is possible. At th
same time I must confess that if want were t
force my wife, or sister, into the position of th
unhappy patient who consulted the professor o
syphilodology, I would cry out that I did no
care a fig for all the schools of medicine in th
world and that no one was given the right t
degrade the feelings of his fellow-creatures
merely because the latter chanced to be poor
. Dr. Veresaeff relates how he was compelled to hid
inevitable doubt and ignorance at the outset of h
practice.
I took lodgings, put up my brass plate with th
legend : ‘ ‘ Dr. , ” and began to wait fo
patients. I awaited them, and at the same tim
dreaded their advent. Every pull at the be!
made my heart beat in a panic ; each time that i
turned out that no patient had called, I sighec
with a feeling of relief. Would Ibe able to
diagnose, would I be capable of prescribing th
right treatment? My store of information wa
neither so large, nor was I so well at home in
what I possessed, that I could feel certain of beinj
able to apply it impromptu. All well and gooc
if the patient’s case was not too urgent and he
could be allowed to wait: after prescribing some
thing neutral, I would look up my text-books a
home and ascertain the right thing to be done
But what if I were called to an emergency case'
It is just this sort of case that beginners are gene
rally summoned to.... What would I do then ?
There is a book by a certain Dr. L. Blau, called
Diagnostics and Therapeutics in cases showing
Alarming Symptoms. I got it and transcribed it
main contents into my pocket memoranda, sup
plementing this conspectus from my text-books
I grouped the different diseases in accordance witl
their symptoms; for instance, dyspepsia: (1
croup ; (2) pseudo croup; (3) oedema of the glottis
(4) spasm of the larnyx ; (5) bronchial asthma ; (6
congestion of the lungs ; (7) croupous pneumonia
(8) uremic asthma; (9) pleurisy; (10) pneumo
thorax. Each disease in turn was accompaniec
by an enumeration of its symptoms and the cor
responding treatment. This note-book did me
yeoman service and I could not dispense with i
for a long time afterwards—about two years
When summoned to the bedside of a patient, ]
would unclasp my trusty friend under the pretex
of taking notes, seek out the disease most resem
bling the patient’s, and prescribe accordingly.
Some of his cases, by good luck, did well; others, bv
bad luok, did ill; but presently he made a mistake in
treatment, and caused blood-poisoning in a patient
washerwoman’s son. The boy died.
Half an hour later, as I was on the point o
taking my departure, the washerwoman openec
her box and offered me a greasy three-rouble
note.
“Take it, sir...for your trouble, she said. “Oh
how you tried to save him, may our Mother ir
Heaven bless you ! ”
I declined the fee. We stood together in the
semi-dark passage.
“It was God’s will!” I murmured, looking
away.
“His holy will. He knows best,” she answered
and once more her lips began to tremble and her
sobs broke forth afresh. “Oh, thank you, thank
you, sir, for having been so good to my boy ! ”
And she tried to kiss my hand, still crying,
thanking me for my kindness and consideration..
Remorse drove Veresaeff back to study, and he was
misled by a “new” cure for croupous pneumonia. An
article in a medical paper of repute had recommendec
digitalis in enormous—and, in normal circumstances
poisonous—doses. Dr. Veresaeff tried the remedy, and
the patient died ; whether from the illness or the digitalis
he could not say.
I registered a vow in future only to employ
those remedies upon my patients which had stood
ample test and left no room for doubt as to their
effects. The closer I became acquainted with
current medical literature, the firmer did this
decision become. An appalling state of affairs
was _ revealed to me. Every number of every
medical journal contained communications on
dozens of new remedies; and thus it went on,
from week to week, from month to month ; a
gigantic, insane and endless torrent, which be-
wildered the eyes: new doses, new medicines,
new methods of introducing them, new operations
—and dozens, nay—hundreds of human lives lost
and healths ruined. Some of these innovations,
like bubbles on the frothing surface of a torrent,
leaped up and immediately burst, leaving behind
one or two corpses.
J*
Veresaeff stresses the importance of “ keeping up con-
fidence ” in patients, and points out how at every step
the doctor has to fight patients’ ignorance.
The exaggerated demands made of medicine,
founded upon an ignorant belief in its omnipo-
tence, are the physician’s bane, and fetter him
hand and foot. A patient down with typhoid
suffers from high fever, splitting headaches, and
night sweats ; he is oppressed by delirium ; these
must be fought against with great precautions,
natural remedies being chiefly recoursed to; but
only try to tell the patient that he must suffer,
bathed in perspiration and tormented by hallu-
cinations. He will turn from you and apply to a
medical man, who will not stint him in quinine,
phenaeebin, and chloral, for he says: “What
doctors this who does not alleviate the patient’s
P ain p matter that this assuagement is
nought at the price of the patient’s strength : let
the permanent ruin of his constitution be the
result, let him lose for all days the power to com-
disease independently; his pain was soothed,
and. that sufficed in itself. The most miserable
patients in this respect are various “exalted per-
sonages impatient and spoilt, who blame the
doctor for the slightest malaise, even when it is •
impossible to remove it. This explains why many
doctors, of whom their colleagues speak with con-
tempt, and to whom no medical man in his right
senses would ever dream of applying, enjoy the
greatest popularity with a certain section of the
public.
Jt
„X - w dtes of the inevitable illnesses bred of modern life,
and suffered by the workers—
A suffering from eczema of the
hands comes to me, or else a drayman with hernia,
or a consumptive weaver; I prescribe salves, pills,
and powders all round, and, myself ashamed at
the farce I am keeping up, tell them in depre-
catory tones that the chief Conditions necessary
for their recovery are as follows : that the washer-
woman should not wet her hands, that the dray-
man should not strain himself by lifting weights,
and that the weaver should keep out of dusty
places.
And he concludes—
Great suffering humanity hangs upon the cross;
his hands and feet are transfixed by cruel nails;'
and medicine bathes the ensanguined wounds
with arnica, and applies aromatic poultices.
On the other side, take an example of the beneficent
work of mechc.ne given in Our Hospitals and Charities
(Liond.) for March.
A patient when he first arrived at the London
Hospital was a man without a face. It was eaten
away by lupus. There were eyes and there was
a chin, little more. The picture is not one good
to think of. The disease was cured. The patient
passed from one department to another. In one
he received an artificial nose; in another a coun-
terfeit moustache and beard. He was refitted to
take his place among his fellow-men in the battle
of life. He had no palate; it was eaten away by
disease. His food had to be poured down his
throat. The surgeons gave him an artificial
palate. Had the man come to consult these
various specialists as any other than a hospital
patient, his treatment would have cost him hun-
dreds of pounds. As it was, it never cost him a
penny.
This, of course, was surgery ; and surgery now-a-dayg
is a science. Medicine, on the other hand, except for
born healers or with the simplest cases, is almost as much
a, matter of guesswork and quackery as in th« days of
Gaien. Says Veresaeff—
Even when my diagnosis appeared to mo to bo
clearly indicated, actuality constantly showed me
to be in the wrong; and, oftener stiff, I stood
before the patient completely nonplussed from
the outset. How was I to draw any conclusion
from the misty and obscure indications with
which his case furnished me! And for whole
nights I would pace up and down my room, pon-
dering upon the data, piecing them together,
and never arriving at apy definite conclusion.
When ultimately I did diagnose, I could never
shake off the haunting thought : “What if my
guess be wrong ? How am Ito verify its correct-
ness ? And to think that I had to live my life
through, beneath the weight of such onstant
uncertainty !
But assuming that I had diagnosed the disease
aright, I now had to treat it. What guarantee
does science give me that the remedies it recom-
mends are really efficacious and to the point ?
The action of most of them is in the highest
degree obscure to us, and the indications to their
application are most empirically set down through
clinical observation. We already know, however,
how unstable and deceiving clinical observation is.
A given remedy acts excellently according to the
unanimous testimony of the profession, and in a
year or two it is thrown overboard as useless, or
even injurious. Koch’s tuberculine lorded it for
two years they saw with their own eyes what’a
brilliant ’ effect it had in combating tubercu-
losis !
A certain young doctor asked the great
Sydenham, the “English Hippocrates,” what
books it was necessary to read to become a good
physician.
Read ‘Don Quixote,’ my friend,’’ answered
Sydenham. “Itis a very good book, and I often
peruse it.”
But this is awful! This means that there oan
be no tradition, no pre-emption of observation.
“Learn to study life without any prejudice, and
eaoh of you learn from the beginning ”
d*
His reflections apply to Australia and the world no less
than to Russia. “ Had I not,” V. asks,
Had I not examined my patient who died of
aneurism most conscientiously, applying all that
science demanded from me, to fall into gross error
nevertheless ? Had a born physician been in my
place, he would have diagnosed rightly: his
peculiar creative faculty would have seized upon
a mass of impalpable symptoms which had escaped
me; through sub-conscious inspiration he would
hav© thus filled the void left by the absence of
clearer indications, guessing what I was incapable
of divining. Only a man thus gifted can answer to
the requirements of the ideal doctor, for talent is
as necessary here as it is to produce the true poet,
artist, or musician. Whereas I had imagined,
when entering myself at the Faculty of Medicine,
that it was possible to learn that art. . . I fondly
believed that a certain level of knowledge and a
certain degree of mental development were all
that I required ; that these qualifications would
enable me to master medicine in the same way as
any applied science, such, for example, as chemi-
cal analysis.
When medicine shall have become a science in
the completest sense of the word, single, universal,
its acquisition by the average man
will be possible. But at present it is as far
beyond our powers to learn medicine, i.e., the
healing art, as to become poets or actors at will.
There are shoals of excellent theorists, genuinely
scientific medical men, who are not worth a
brass farthing in actual practice.
The Passing Scribe.
Gov.-Gen. Northcote or bis secretary has been
whetting wit on that useful word appreciate,
and the Labour Prime Minister is supposed to he
transfixed by a subtle shaft of satire. Watson
telegraphed “ hearty congratulations ” that North-
cote had been dubbed G.C.M.G. —“hearty con-
gratulations upon your receipt of a further mark
of his Majesty’s appreciation of your valuable
services to the Empire.” He went on: “I
assure you that the news will be warmly received,
throughout the Commonwealth a sentence that
may represent either official dullness or personal
irony. N. replied: “Very many thanks for
your telegram of congratulation ” (and he might
have stopped there) “which I fully appreciate ”
(and lo! the venomed dart). For, look you,
the word appreciate has two chief uses,
the good or scholar’s use=“to estimate justly ” ;
and the less good or vulgar use= “ to estimate
highly.” The confusion is so familiar that every-
body who keeps nails and language clean
is accustomed to add highly or greatly for a
quality mark when he fits the word to the popu-
lar understanding. Lord Northcote added fully.
Ordinary, readers (and, doubtless, a Labour
'Premier is an ordinary reader from the Govern-
ment House point of view) would interpret the
telegram to mean “I thank you for your good
wishes, which I esteem highly.” What it really
says is “Very many thanks ” (ironical common-
place) “ for your telegram of congratulation ”
(stressing the better use of congratulation)
“ whioh I rate precisely at its worth.”
Jt
The stroke is too easy to be fine. A more in-
genious passado, for example, is reported by
Frankfort Moore in They Call it Love.
“ I have the honour to wish you good afternoon, sir,”
said the colonel...
“ Nay, sir,” said Mr. Conway, drawing upon his memo-
ries of the eighteenth century. “ Nay, sir, the honour is
on my side.”
That, now, is really pretty. We like it almost
as well as “Sappho Smith’s ” remark when an
eminent citizen lingered over his dying. His
life-innings had been good, and it was clearly “up
to him ” to give his relatives a chance to score the
assets. So “Sappho” reported to the effect that
“This week Mr. is again very ill, and
grave hopes are entertained of his recovery.”
Quod appretiet Etona.
«*
John Pollock, who writes a panegyric on Lord
Acton in April Independent Review, has this
especially interesting passage :
Lord Acton said: “ I was once with two eminent
men, Bishop Stubbs and Bishop Creighton. On another
occasion I was with two far more eminent men, the two
most learned men in the world—l need hardly tell you
their names—they were Mommsen and Harnack. On
each occasion the question arose : who was the greatest
historian the world had ever produced ? On each occa-
sion the name first mentioned, and on each occasion the
name finally agreed upon, was that of Macaulay.” Burke
and Macaulay Lord Acton held to be the two greatest of
English writers, and Burke at his best to be our wisest
political thinker j but, at a time when Macaulay’s name is
more often than not held up for rebuke by historians, it
may not be unprofitable to remember so remarkable a
consensus of opinion of five men, whose varied accom-
plishments at least cannot be accused of lightness or
want of breadth.
You might explain in this way. All history is
false, or at best is but a distant approximation to
the truth. The more one knows of his-
torians, their materials and their prejudices,
the more one realises this. Since then, con-
sciously or unconsciously every historian bears
false witness, the best is he who can make
the historical lie most picturesque and vital, can
most vividly create his own honest conception of
past people and past times. Thus the liest is
Macaulay. Often he is wrong, often he is tire-
some ; but on the whole one cannot be sure
that the duller men are truer. Seeing that all is
error, his imagination is more probably accurate
than others’ representation of what are euphemis
tically called “facts.”

N.S.VV. Birth-rate Commissioners, who for
want of imagination and capacity for generalisa-
tion recently presented us with a report so woe-
fully inept, might read and endeavour to compre-

concludes that a falling birth-rate is an essential
phenomenon of rising civilisation.

Lord Acton’s preference for Middlemarvh is a
crucial trait revealed in his newly-published
Letters. A division of the wbrld into people who
prefer Adam Bede and people who prefer Middle-
march would make a good working division of
literary sheep from those very wise animals the
goats. Perhaps Middlernarch fails in dramatic
impression ; but it is the wittiest, tjie most
thoughtful, and the most suggestive of all “ George
Eliot’s” books. Dorothea called here only last
week She is a good creature—that fine girl—
but a little too earnest. It is troublesome to talk
to such women. They are always wanting reasons,
yet _ they are top ignorant to understand the
merits of any question, and Usually fall back on
their moral sense to settle things after their own
taste.” Women’s moral sense, of course, repre-
sents the race’s legacy of accumulated instincts,
and is doubtless a necessary brake on the wheels
of intellectual progress. But it is obnoxious in
the field of art.
Hector Maclean, F.R.P.S. (Lond.), vouches
that a practical and commercial method of photo-
graphy in colours has been invented. The inven-
tor is the clever young Galician Zepanik (whose
name is transliterated into English as Szczepanik.
with a crowd of useless consonants).
A sheet of the colour sensitive paper, being placed
under a suitable transparency, may, under favourable cir-
cumstances, within ten minutes be made to yield a’’
coloured photograph reproducing the whole range of
primary, secondary, and tertiary colours and their mix-
tures. The resultant print, moreover, calls for neither
development nor toning. Indeed, it might iln. v,
without “fixing," but in order that it may not chm,
when exposed to light it is subjected to some simple
4mT ICa ' °P era^'ons "fell within the power of aiiy ordinary
• j 0 near attire there is good reason to expect that
instead of—as at present—rbeing merely a printing pro!
cess, it will be possible to so increase the sensitiveness of
the prepared paper that, just as tintypes in black and
white are taken in a camera, so will it be possible to taka
coloured photographs by imprinting on this paper the
coloured image of a scene or subject as focussed by a lens
m a camera. More than this, inasmuch as with this
strange, eventful '* colour process a positive colour
transparency prints out on to the prepared paper as a
positive, and not, as in all other photographic‘procedures
as a negative, once a coloured picture has been obtained
is a camera it can be printed from times out of number
each print reproducing whatever has been photographed
by the exposure in the camera.
In ordinary photographic procedure the rule has
hitherto been that wherever light acted the sensitised
surface darkened. Zepanik’s printing process, however
depends upon the principle that where light falls a propor!
tional bleaching action ensues. So far the primuline, and
certain other obscure processes, have acted as pioneers
But in the Zepanik process two other steps forward have
been taken. In the first place, the particular dyes are so
sensitised that each dye used is respectively protected
from decolouration by red, yellow, and blue. The
second step is that three collodion (or gelatine) films
dyed red, yellow, and blue, and sensitised by a treatment
with anithol, are successively coated on a sheet of paper.
On placing a coloured positive transparency over sucli a
sheet of paper, and allowing sunlight to act, the red is
entirely bleached except where protected by any red on
the positive used for printing. Similarly the blue and
the yellow films are bleached pro rata according to the
absence of yellow or blue in the transparency. A short
’treatment designed to dissolve out the anithol and to fix
the dyes by means of a “mordant” completes the
operation.
J*
The last story of all-embracing Mormondoru.
declares that President Smith was recently walk-
ing down a street in Salt Lake City when he met
a boy smoking a cigarette. The “prophet”
stopped to remonstrate with him. “ Little boy,”
he said, “don’t you know that it is very wicked
to smoke those nasty cigarettes? What would
your father and mother say if they saw you now ?”
The boy looked up in the speaker’s face, and;
said, “ Don’t you know me, pa ? ”
J*
This reminds of the much-married clergyman
who was placed in charge of an extensive parish,
and came on his pastoral round to a pub.'
in the wilderness. He signified hi 3 inten-
tion of stopping for dinner, and the publican,
took the horses out of the buggy, looking
curiously at him the while. Presently he
said, “ Don’t you know me, father ? ” The
minister started. “Bless me ! Why, no—it can’t
be Joseph ? ” “ Joseph it is, father—there’s the
name up ! ” “ Dear me ! Joseph, and how are
you?” “ Fust-rate, father ; have n’t seen you for
twenty year.” “As long as that!—how time
flies ! I suppose you know I’ve married again.”
“Well, we guessed as much, father —you’re that
sort, you know.” “Dear me! dear me!—and
how is Mary?” “Mary, father?” “Yes, your
sister Mary ?—she had red hair, I remember.”
“No sister of that name, father—there’s Alice
and Jane and Henrietta—no Mary ; no red hair
in our family. We ’re by your second, you know.”
“Dear me, to be sure! Mary was Emily’s
daughter; I remember now. But wasn’t there
a Clara? ” “She was by your third, father; she’s
housekeeper fifteen mile frdm here; we found
her out by the name.’’ “ Dear me ! And William,
and Thomas, and Eustace?” “We don’t know
them, father; they must have come since our
time.’’ “ Dear me ! I wonder where they are ! ”
And the patriarch, who had dropped progeny all
over the length and breadth of the country, went
ponderingly in to dinner. J >

A recent visitor to George Meredith (now 76) is
especially awed by his mouth. The head is good too—
“ a grand head” —“a Greek head ” ; but the mouth!
“ The srreat month opens almost foursquare. It is an
Attic mask, a magician’s cave. . . . We feel that, as Men-
delssohn said of Goethe, he could shout like a hundred
warriors.” v>
An Academy reviewer says very sensibly of Yeats’s
plays, written for “an Irish thea re,” that they can do
nothing to help the Irish .stage because, first and last,
they are not dramatic—they contain much good poetry
and little characterisation. What is wanted ? Living
hearts, not dead bones ; human beings, not abstractions ;
—dramas of Irish life of to-day.
New sixpenny books worth buying are Brownell’s The
Heart of Japan and (possibly) Hath. Thurston's The
Circle,
The Strand Magazine quotes Phil May as praising the
incomparable Gillray. “ Look at his sweep of line and
his astonishing mastery over the grotesque and ridicu-
lous. There are pictures so extraordinarily funny that
you can’t laugh—too funny for words.”
“Little Miss Bauermeister,” one of the old-time
operatic singers, now retiring from the stage, says of
“our Nellie” that “Madame Melba I always call a
diamond in the rough. If she likes you she is as true as
steel, and if she does not it takes a very short time for
you to discover the fact.”
The Athenceum mentions that Sidney Lee, in a pre-
face to the collection of Elizabethan sonnets in the new
edition of Arber’s Garner, shows that a mass of them,
“ hitherto regarded as original, are literal translations
from French or Italian.” One of these days somebody
may generalise regarding the whole Elizabethan illumina-
tion that the fire was kindled in Europe, and the fuel
was supplied, and that England’s part was with charac-
teristic energy to blow the fire to flame.
Regarding 0. Rossetti, the same review remarks that
“ It is curious—curious and regrettable—how few poets
retain the white heat, the thorough-blast of their inspira-
tion, when they dedicate themselves to direct religious
themes. Since the days of Crashaw, Vaughan, and
Herbert many have felt themselves called, but few have
been chosen. It seems to demonstrate the inferiority of
holy-water to the Pierian spring as a source of in-
spiration.”

“ Sigma ” is Paget Toynbee, author and critic, known for
his work on Dante.
Holbrook Blinn, an actor, relates this Amer. experi-
ence. The play was As You Like It, and the place was
Detroit, Michigan. On the morning after the first
performance one newspaper appeared with no mention
whatever of the theatre or the company. Its criticism,
headed “The Baconian Theory,” ran as follows “The
believers in the Baconian theory will noV have an
opportunity of proving the truth of their opinions. If
to-day the graves of Shakespear and Bacon be opened,
She body of that one which turned over last night will
establish the authorship of As You Like It."

BUSHMAN and BUCCANEER

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Ballad of Regret.
The Bushland tells her stories
In whispers on the breeze ;
The morn unfolds its glories
In golden ecstasies.
The sun was never purer,
Nor half so brightly shone;
The Bush, my love and lurer,
Has called sweet fancies on.
The skies to me are clouded,
The earth is wrapped in grey,
And Life itself is shrouded
In dismal dark dismay.
Bush songsters sing in gladness,
Where leaves are wet with dew;
The old earth knows no sadness
Her wide dominions through.
The soft mists rise to meet me
From sleeping river-bends —■
The whole bush wakes to greet me
For we are more than friends.
The breeze?s song a dirge is “
To my unmindful ears,
It sobs and sighs and surges
To wake my sleeping fears.
The rising sun has crowned me
A fairy king of old ;
The mountains tall around me
Are tipped with gleaming gold.
Through fern and frond and creepers,
That each with each entwine,
He wakes a thousand sleepers
To cheer this path of mine.
In spite of sunlight streaming ,
The Bushland?s glories pall;
She is (in raptures seeming)
A woeful sight withal.
No morn was ever brighter,
No path was half so gay;
No heart was ever lighter
Than mine might be to-day. . . .
We praise sweet Nature highly,
As she, my lover, knows,
And yet we chide her slyly,
Because of human woes.
This world of jubilation
Finds me for murder ripe;
Fm ten miles from the station.
And Fve been and lost my pipe.
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
Cuttin’ Wood.
You’re sure to meet when you are poor and badly
needing food
A Christian-hearted lady who will let you cut some
wood;
She smiles and finds a blunted saw, some wedges
and a maul.
And points you out the curly logs she wants cut
“ nice an’ small.”
You doff your coat and get to work and wonder
what’s the pay,
And as you stack the billets up the kids bear them
away;
And soon the lady comes and says “her heart
bleeds for the pore,”
And urges you to hurry up and cut a little more.
Abashed you graft away again until the sun is
down,
Then meekly hint you’ve not engaged your shake-
down in the town;
And when you’re paid—a battered “piece”—a
tract on “ Faith is All,”
The kids take care you reach the gate without the
saw and maul.
So you depart, their parting words a-ringing in
your brain:
“Mum sez yer good at cuttin’ wood, an’ p’raps
yer’ll call again ! ”
vio.
FEANK BELLMAN.
Our Spell.
We’ve got the Blue Tank paddock cleared,
We finished up to-day ;
We grafted till we almost feared
We’d graft our souls away.
The crowbar and the pick we drove,
In heat and dust and sweat,
And heard the old man spouting of
The spell we were to get.
’Twas just the same when first we came
To live at Wangeroo;
The looked-for rest was sure to come
When harvest work was through.
Since then we’ve passed through weary years,
Oft prosperous, but yet
Still in the future dim appears
The spell that we’re to get.
Dad reckoned once we’d get the spell
When Bunyip Creek came down ;
A month, he thought, would see us well
Upon the road to town.
But when the flood came rushing past,
The roads became so wet,
Dad reckoned that we’d best put off
The spell we were to get.
It’s been our guiding star for years
(A far-off star, indeed !)
It always glows its brightest when
The land is clothed with “feed.”
For wealth and gold we rarely yearn,
But oh ! with deep regret,
Our hearts keep sadly longing for
The spell that we’re to get.
Ah, well! perhaps the life in town
Would sicken such as we,
And in the contemplation of
The spell the joy may be.
So let us strive to be content,
It’s little use to fret;
For in the grave we’re sure to have
The spell we are to get.
N.S.W.

A Six Years’ Martyr
To Neuralgic Torture.
A PERMANENT CURE EIGHT YEARS AGO.
The Case of Mrs. K. WARREN.
(FOR THE “NEWTOWN INDEPENDENT.”)
The first and most obvious duty before us
is the adoption of a policy of rigidly shap-
ing our actions according to our judgment.
W hat these actions should be would remain
a matter of doubt were it not for such state-
ments as those from the lips of Mrs. Katie
Warren, of No. 106 Wilsoa-street, New-
town. Interviewed by our reporter, this
lady remarked :
“ For nearly six years I was a great
sufferer from neuralgia, and although I
visited plenty of chemists’ shops, and pur-
chased the medicines which the chemists
said were remedies for my complaint, I
could not get ease from any of them. The
pains in my head were terribly severe, and
to think that I had to bear them all my life
was too dreadful a reflection for any woman
to resign herself to patiently. Better a
thousand times to be out of the world than
endure such torture, I used to think, but
what was I to do when no relief could be
obtained ? ”
“Had you tried many things?” queried
the reporter.
“ All that I could think of, and the only
return I got was to find myself sinking
lower and lower, for it is an awful drain on
the system not to be able to sleep and take
proper nourishment. I certainly thought I
would never be cured, and became a most
unhappy mortal; but one day a relative put
the idea into my head to try some Clements
Tonic, and when I got some I felt great
relief in a few days. The stiffness and sore-
ness vanished from my jaws, and with the
hungry feeling which Clements Tonic gave
me I started to eat well, and picked up
strength rapidly. I was so astounded at
the disappearance of my afflictions that I
thought it was only just a temporary affair,
and that my neuralgic pains would come
back again, but for all that I took a few
more bottles of Clements Tonic, and I have
not been troubled with my unmerciful com-
plaint during the past eight years.”
“And I ho te you have been free from
other malad'tii.'.”
“ Unfortunately, I have not, as I took ill
again twelve months ago last February. I
remember the very day, which was an ex-
ceedingly hot one, and in the evening I
took a cold bath, and caught a severe chill.
Shortly afterwards I was taken with shivers
and frightful pains in my right side, while
in my back and chest the agonies were in
the extreme. * I could hardly breathe for
the pains, as when each breath was drawn
an acute cutting sensation ran right through
my chest and gave me terrible suffering.
During the night a more alarming symptom
than ever set in, for I began spitting blood,
and, as if my fate were not already suffi-
ciently cruel, the pains in my chest became
still more violent. A little relief was ob-
tained through applying hot fomentations,
but I am sorry to say that the ease was only
of a temporary nature. All that week I
was confined to my bed, although I was
taking medicines in the hope of beinghelped
out ef my distressful predicament; but I
derived no relief. I could not tolerate
lying in bed any longer, so I got up, being
barely able to drag myself about the house,
I was so weak and thoroughly run down.
This calamity came shortly after a domestic
misfortune which had previously befallen
me, so the lowness of my spirits can be
better imagined than described. Although
I would not stay in bed, I was nevertheless
conscious that my health was drifting away,
as I was kept awake at nights by a nasty
hacking cough, and during the day I was
languid, and could not eat. Because I
vomited clots of blood I imagined some-
thing was wrong with my lungs, and I grew
quite alarmed, while by my sallow com-
plexion and the emaciated condition of my
body I really thought I was going into
consumption I could not digest even the
lightest food procurable, as everything gave
me an uneasy feeling in the stomach, and
my shoulders had sharp pains between
them. Three weary months went on in
this way, all of wltich time my eyes were
dim and swollen and my limbs ached from
head to foot, the pains across my loins being
so bad that I could scarcely walk for them.
Sickened as I was by a vile taste in the
mouth, there seemed no prospect of ever
enjoying my food again, and as my nervous
system was quite unstrung I was a misery
to myself when left alone, for I was always
imagining some frightful thing was going to
happen, and if I heard a knock or anything
of the sort my nerves trembled excessively.
I do not like going to doctors if I can avoid
it, and I followed my previous practice by
taking advertised medicines ; but when my
condition did not mend I thought seriously
of seeing a medical expert. One lady in
particular was very angry with me for not
calling in a doctor, and she said : l lf you
will not do so, why not try some Clements
Tonic. You say it cured you before.’ ‘ But
that was for neuralgia,’ I replied. ‘ Never
mind ; it may do you good again,’ my friend
answered. It was not improbable, I thought,
so I got a couple of bottles. The first one
affected me but very little, and it was not
until I had emptied my second bottle of
Clements Tonic that I knew whether to
continue with it or not.”
“ Was there much difference in your con-
dition then ? ”
“ Not a great deal, but enough to make
me think I ought to give Clements Tonic a
further trial, and I am heartily glad I did
so, for shortly afterwards I felt so much
revived that I ventured to hope I was saved.
The three months’ blood-spitting trouble,
which had held me in a state of awful
suspense from the time it started, was now
becoming less frequent, and the appetising
influence of Clements Tonic enabled me to
take some nourishment. The indigestion
symptoms which had made me loathe the
thought of eating had lost a great amount
of their force already under the treatment
of Clements Tonic, and as the pains died
away so could I sleep in proportion. I used
to prop myself up with pillows because of
fearing suffocation, but that was not neces-
sary now, as Clements Tonic had entirely
relieved my chest, and in a little while my
cough was better. I used to start up
suddenly from my sleep and feel as if I
were choking, but three or four weeks’ use
of Clements Tonic saw me arising with a
cheerful heart and an invigorated system.
The pains left my body one by one, and my
nervousness was quite overcome by tho
toning qualities of Clements Tonic, which
had a regulating and strengthening power
over me which surprised everybody. My
complexion became bright and clear, an l
the fears of going into consumption were
dispelled by the robust appearance I again
assumed, for I took my Clements Tonic ti l
I looked and felt as well as I ever did in
my life. My restoration to an enjoyable
existence should be a lesson to other poor
sufferers, so you may publish these par-
ticulars in any way you consider most
useful.”

Wonderful Home Treatment that Cures
Without Pain or Loss of Time.
Dr. W. 6. Eice (Dept. 1317), 8 & 9, Stonecutter
Street, London, E.C., has invented a method that
cures rupture without pain, danger, operation, or
an hour’s loss of time from the day’s work. To
avoid all questions of doubt, he
sends free to every sufferer a
free trial of his method, and
there can be no earthly reason
why anyone, rich or poor, should
not avail themselves of this
generous offer. As an instance
of this remarkable method,
the cure of Mr. J. J. Hogan,
Cobar, N.S.W., is well worth
noticing. He was cured at the
age of 70 years, after suffering
10 years. He made an affidavit
to this effect, of his own free
Mb. A. Dochebty. w iu ) before two J.P’s.
Mr. J. Eubank, a farmer of Yarloop, Western
Australia, was cured after suffering 48 years from
a scrotal rupture. Mrs. F. Medley, 177, Sturt
Street, Ballarat, Victoria, Australia, was cured
after suffering 5 years. Mr. A. Docherty, 23, V ar-
wick Street, Birkenhead, Cheshire, England, was
cured of an irreducible scrotal rupture—one of the
worst kinds known. No treatment he had used

THURSDAY, MAY 19, 1904.
THE BENT ELECTION.
Victoria holds a State general election next
month, and it presents the curious spectacle of a
Government with no policy, and hardly any politi-
cal assets save a curae and a howl, going to the
country with some slight prospect of success.
There are many things wrong with Victoria just
now, but what is most wrong is the want of a
statesman large enough to realise what is wrong.
The gross and grotesque figure of Bent, of course,
fills the foreground. Bent is a relic of the old
nefarious boom days, which led to the State’s
downfall, and he is so gruesome a relic that even
the bad old gang of the boom and the burst and
the halfpenny in the £ compositions, dropped him
overboard for its own safety in the long ago.
But he came to the surface again like the
remains that have been in the Yarra for
many days, and now floats temporarily on
the top of the political waters. He is a grotesque,
dribbling person with a crumpled shirt-front ;
lie is curiously unlettered, and can’t pronounce
“ consolidation ” —he talks of the “ conciliation of
tlje statutes” ; he thinks there are 88,000,0)0
square miles in Victoria ■ and he has got some
wonderful and dizzy idea in the way of apple-stall
finance whereby, per medium of some ‘ ‘ fake ” or
dodge with a company, the £350,000 a year which
Victoria has to pay in pensions is to be liquidated
for £175,000 or so annually. In fact, he has a
scheme for getting a quart out of a pint pot by
floating the pot into a company. Thomas Bent,
in short, is about the most amazing figure that has
loomed up as a ruler since a certain nigger left off
driving his mule to become Emperor of Hayti,
and immediately ate a large pot of jam on the throne.
Bent’s whole existence is a squalid shriek of humor.
And this quaint object has a policy. It consists
mostly of a yell against Socialism, which Bent
apparently, by his descriptions of it, mixes up
with Communism, or else thinks is a new variety
of lettuce. Otherwise it is made up of a habit of
promising every farmers’ deputation all kinds of
socialistic assistance ; of a threat to disfranchise
every civil servant who doesn’t vote for the Bent
candidate ; of a further threat to repudiate the
State’s liability to its civil service pensioners; and
of a general waddling up and down on the face of
the earth, and eating largely, and spluttering
foolishly.
« -ft • ft
The Bent Government has a record apart from
the record of its leader. It has been a strictly
class Administration, whose policy has consisted in
granting concessions to farmers and rich land-
owners, and allowing them to repudiate their
obligations to the State. It partly inherited from
its predecessor, Peacock, and partly manufac-
tured itself, a deficit of £400,000 for the year ended
June, 1902, and didn’t pay it off; all the statements
that it did are unadulterated falsehood. It just
avoided having a deficit in its first complete year
by annexing as revenue certain sinking funds and
other moneys which the careful Turner left
behind him. It repudiated the law whereby the
small surplus which it manufactured out of the
sinking funds must go in reduction of old debts.
It claims to have a prospective surplus of £500,000
for June, 1904, but refuses flatly to publish expen-
diture accounts, as most other States do, whereby
the figures can be verified. It cut down the old
age pensions, and drops hints about abolishing
them altogether. It has starved the charities and
the hospitals; in Melbourne poor and aged in-
valids are dying in .gaol and in the streets for
want of hospital accommodation, and in such
hospitals as exist there are dreadful cases of
two sufferers in one bed, and even worse horrors.
Victoria, under Bent and his friends,
has become a disgrace to civilisation, a
smudge upon the fair face of the earth.
Though its manufactures are prospering its
people are leaving it for want of land to settle
on, while its big landlords are allowed to lock up
the land and block all progress. And through all
this chaos and political debauchery lumbers the
surprising apparition of Bent, struggling round in
search of a policy, promising everything to-day
and cursing everything to-morrow, stumbling over
the small words and laboriously spelling out the
big ones, perspiring in a manner that isn’t agree-
able to witness, dropping off platforms and break-
ing furniture when he sits down upon it, rushing
to and fro on railways that are tied up with
string through Bent’s management, and in the
tottering vehicle which is called in derision
“Bent’s train”—a. wretched, pathetic, human
spectacle of blither, ignorance and violent futility.
• • • •
If the Opposition had the courage to promul-
gate a big policy it would almost certainly wipe
Bent off the face of the globe. It has got rid of
the mentally shambling Peacock, and its new
leader, Mackinnon, is a man of some promise, and
was never accused of taking £5O from any Canadian
assessment assurance office, or suspected of accept-
ing favors from any Red Indian. But while the
Mackinnon policy is honest and honorable, it is
only half a policy at best. There is a great future
in Victoria for the leader who will promise to
break up the big estates with a heavy graduated
land-tax and thus provide land for the people on
a scale which will set the tide of immigration once
more flowing towards the misgoverned State ; who
will promise an absentee-tax, a fair old age pension
system, decent treatment of hospitals and chari-
ties, complete cessation of borrowing, the establish-
ment of a sinking fund, an honest Arbitration Act,
and such encouragement to local industries as
a mere State Government can give. For a Whole
Hog party, whose programme of reform is obviously
for the; good of all classes, there is always an
opening in politics ; while for the colorless party,
which is moderate, neutral and half-hearted—the
party of Respectable Drift—there is no permanent
abiding place. The strength of Kyabram lies in
the fact that it is Whole Hog—in its meanness, in
its resolution to rob everybody for the sake of the
farming class and the rich traders and landlords
who are its main support, in its brute selfishness,
its tyranny, its lying, its complete callousness to
human suffering, and its pandering to the worst
passions of human nature. Being Whole Hog,
and haying a leader who answers to the same
description, it is a power that has to be con-
sidered. It may not have a legislative pro-
gramme, but every member of the Hog party
rallies round it confidently, knowing that,
however hazy its outlook, it can be relied upon to
do the worst thing when the moment arrives. On
the other hand, Opposition-leader Mackinnon has
no record in office. The party which is not Hog
doesn’t know by experience that he will manfully
do the best thing when the moment comes, and his
programme leaves the impression that he is liable
only to do the second-best thing.
* • * *
The State Labor party suffers from the same
trouble as Mackinnon. It lost its chance through
its failure to come out in strong protest against
the spectral Peacock’s policy of financial drift.
Mackinnon has an indefinite financial policy ; the
State Labor party has actually issued its manifesto
with no financial policy in it at all. The downfall
of the remnant of the progressive party once led by
Turner was brought about mainly by Peacock’s
incapacity as Treasurer. The finances wanted
squaring, and Kyabram and 00. promised to do
it—by starving the poor and the sick, by robbing
the public servants, by stealing the sinking funds,
and in general by lying, fraud, brutality and other
sins. To some extent, too, though only to some
extent, it kept its promise. Its opponents now
want, above all things, to come forward witb a
definite and cast-iron policy for making the
finances square and keeping them so without
robbery and ruffianism. Kyabram’s lie is that its
methods are the only ones which will stop the
financial drift, and until that lie is refuted
Kyabram will have an advantage. The Vic-
torian Labor party has everything except the
thing which, just at this crisis, it most needs.
It has a splendid programme so far as it goes, and
that is all that can be said for it.
• * * *
All the same, no Victorian can go wrong by
giving his vote in any direction which will rid the
State of the political leprosy of the Bent Govern-
ment and the crowd of old Tories, dull incapables,
boodlers, hoodlums and back-numbers by whom it
is surrounded. The Bent Government is making
Victoria a world’s jest, and Bent’s legislative
schemes threaten to crowd up the world’s ash-
barrel. Strangers visit Melbourne and look at the
deafening curiosity that is supposed to conduct
the affairs of the country, and then go out in a
dazed fashion to the Zoo to see if the rest of the
Ministry has also escaped from its natural place
of residence. They consider the starved hospitals,
and the decayed charities, and the old people who
are sentenced—sometimes by a J.P. holding an
impromptu court on the pavement—to 12 months
in gaol for being sick, because gaol is the only
available accommodation ; and they reckon Vic-
torians a callous and brutal people, whose more
natural habitat would be somewhere in the Dark
Continent. This is a time when all the decent
elements of the community should make up their
differences, and combine to clean up and disinfect
their native land. It was never more in need of
cleaning—not even in the days when Gillies the
wife-deserter was the temporary god of Melbourne
Toryism.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Political Position.
The situation in Federal politics, at the time The
Bulletin went to press, was mainly made up of
Yes-No Reid pathetically whining for a situation
of some sort and industriously explaining that
nothing mattered to him except office. This
situation has been developed in some mysterious
place by the sea-shore of Victoria; and most
stringent precautions were taken to ensure the
secrecy of the “ caucus.” So it is not possible to
say what the Deakin response to the pitiful appeal
of a stout man in his agony has been. The curse of
Deakin’s nature as a politician is that he is such a
good-hearted fellow, and it is among the imagin-
able possibilities that he will throw away his
future in Federal politics now, out of charity for
the forlorn Reid. It is among the possibilities;
The Bulletin is beginning to hope that it were
among the probabilities, for with every day it is
becoming clearer that a Reid-Deakin coalition,
whilst dispossessing Watson from office to-day,
would make his accession to a long term of power
in the near future, with a reliable majority of his
own, a practical certainty. Already there are
several defections from the proposed following of
the suggested Deakin-Reid coalition, and with
Parliament in session those defections would
increase with every false step made by the two-
headed monstrosity whose construction was
undertaken in the cave. As for the con-
stituencies, no Reid-Deakin coalition could
appeal to them with the faintest hope of suc-
cess. The only plea such a body could put
forward would be that Premier Watson was
a dangerous anarchist, whose existence in office
made it necessary for Reidites ahd Deakinites to
sink all else to turn him out; and there is no hope
of convincing the electors of that. A Deakin-
Reid combination would be such a glaring example
of selling principle for office that it would pass all
explanation. It is known that Deakin found the
Labor party so safe and desirable a party that he
worked with it for three years ; and that Reid
found it so useful, patriotic, and progressive
that he worked with it in State politics
for five years, and offered to work with
it in Federal politics for an indefinite
Eeriod, provided he was allowed to hold office.
f these two men informed the country
at election time, as an explanation of their
new attitude, that the Labor party was so
dreadful that they couldn’t conscientiously touch
it with a big pole, and that they had to sacrifice
their life-long principles to get rid of it, it wouldn’t
be a convincing shudder. People would naturally
ask why this feeling only came upon them when it
was calculated to lead to office. Also, why they
didn’t, by way of evidence, leave Watson in
power long enough to let the malignity of his
character develop, and thus prove that his removal
was really for the country’s good.
♦
The Federal Capital.
The Federal capital comes within sight. The
Watson Ministry will make the settlement of the
locality one of the first duties of office. The sites
in the running have changed somewhat as the
result of recent Parliamentary visits. The chances
of Lyndhurst have not appreciated; nor, on the
other hand, have they suffered. The support
given to that site is not even pretended to be on
account of its suitability, but because it serves the
aspirations of the narrow Sydney provincialists,
just as Albury served the aspirations of the same
class in Melbourne. Tumut seems to have dropped
altogether out of the race. There is still some
talk, however, of Batlow, a site near Tumut,
and on a higher elevation. But the choice
really seems to rest between Bombala and Dalgety
—two towns close to one another on the Southern
Monaro tableland. Each possesses a capital cli-
mate, a good soil, and an easily accessible position.
Dalgety has the advantages of a better water
supply and a better opportunity of utilising the
Snowy River cataracts for electric power. Bom-
bala, on the other hand, is much nearer to Twofold
Bay, and a Southern Monaro site which did not
stretch down to the coast would fail in some im-
portant essentials. With the choice of Bombala
the territory necessary to be resumed for the in-
clusion of Twofold Bay would not be nearly so
great as if Dalgety were the site. If it is stipula-
lated that the Federal territory shall be sufficiently
large to extend, with a reasonable width, from Dal-
gety to Boydtown, The Bulletin would be inclined
to favor Dalgety as being at least equal to Bombala.
But since a territory including Bombala and Boyd-
town would need to be of at least 2000 square miles,
and a territory including Dalgety and Boydtown
at least 3000 square miles, and there is already a
cry that Sydney “ has been betrayed ” whenever a
suggestion is made that the Federal territory shall
exceed the minimum mentioned in the Constitu-
tion Act, the selection of Dalgety might create a
situation which would lead to the Federal territory
being left without a port. Of course, an area of
3000 square miles of Federal territory would be
better for the Commonwealth, and for N.S. Wales,
than an area of 2000 square miles, and an area of
5000 would be better than either; but the posi-
tion has to be faced with its practical difficulties
as well as its ideal possibilities. Still, the
Constitution gives to the Federal Government
power to resume any area so long as it is greater
than 100 square miles. There is a minimum
provided but no maximum—a clear inference that
no maximum was thought desirable. The Sydney
Stak the other day talked ignorantly of—
Some novel proposals in relation to the capital site.
The most startling of these was that, instead of the
hundred square miles specified in the Federal Act as what
should constitute the Federal area, a thousand square
miles should be taken from New South Wales for that
purpose. The idea of this wholesale appropriation, and
in complete defiance of what was set down in the bond, is
known to have found great favor with many of the
forwards among the Labor party.
That is a foolish perversion of the truth. The
Constitution Act does not specify 100 square
miles as the area of the Federal territory: it
specifies that the area must be at least that, and,
inferentially, as much more as is thought desirable.
—
The Independent Order of Fakirs.
Nobody who knows much about the Red Indian
Order of Forester" the impudent cheap-jack con-
cern which masquerades as an insurance society,
will be surprised that Justice Hood, in Victoria,
has found it guilty of bribery and corruption.
How could a society which offers these disad-
vantages hope to get along otherwise
(1) It gives no guarantee as to the amount of premiums
that will be called for. They may be increased at any
time in the shape of assessments.
(2) Its policies have no surrender value. If the insurer
fails but once to pay a premium (and he never knows
what the size of the next premium is to be), the policy is
forfeited, and he loses everything he has paid.
(3) Its funds amount to 'only 6d. in the £ of the total
alleged to be assured ; so that it is morally certain
premiums will have to be increased. If they are not
increased it will only be because of the vast number of
policy-holders who are crowded out and forfeit all that
they have paid.
The only chance for such a bounding society to do
business is to do it with those who have no
capacity for figures or business themselves
unfortunates, mostly, who have very little put by,
and who are least likely to be able to pay increased
premiums. To such people results are nothing ;
names are everything. And the fact that the
1.0. F. offers bribes in order to get these names
seems to prove that those who know it best know
that they can’t get the names by fair means.
A society which has merits of its own has no
need to corruptly buy support; and when an
attempt is made to buy it in that way, the society
stands self-branded as worthless. That, in fact,
is the most valuable result of the Victorian Com-
mission’s inquiry. Whether Dr. Montague is a
corrupt person or not is of little consequence to
Australia. The thing that is of consequence here
is that his society, which apparently lives to some
extent by bribery and is therefore self-condemned,
is continuing to collect premiums from Austra-
lians, and Australia lets it go on doing so. The
Governments demani from it no guarantees what-
ever. They submit its affairs to no sort of official
inspection. The society, which a Supreme Court
J udge has found to be capable of attempting to
bribe and corrupt a State Premier and member of
a State Parliament, may issue statements of
accounts which are utterly misleading; and the
Governments do not interfere. It was shown
lately that the society spends enormously more in
“expenses” than the accounts supplied to in-
surers show. It is fairly obvious now why it
cannot afford to be precise on such a point; but
have the Governments no duty to protect the
public, especially in such a matter as that of life
assurance? The Commission will be so much
wasted effort and money if the revelations which
it has made are not followed by laws that offer
some protection to the people against adventurers
in a field where, above all others, cheap-jack and
purse-trick methods are dangerous. If -the cheap-
jack must live, it is an obvious duty to see that he
doesn’t live at the expense of the provision which
struggling men make for their families after their
death.
A Superfluous ‘Worm with a Popgun.
The superfluous worm Jenkins, of S.A., still
wriggles. The Australian Government refused a
1 Hiding at Port Darwin to nine Chinese. Then
the worm Jenkins ordered the police to land
them, in defiance of the Customs, which repre-
sented Australia in the matter. The foolish
J enkins thought he had some shred of authority
for his action ; he relied upon some State certifi-
cates that he supposed these friends of his held.
As it happened, they did not hold anything but a
handful of forgeries. Therefore Jenkins had not
even the bad authority he thought he had. He
declared war, and marched out (strictly by proxy),
and then found he had only blank cartridge to
fight with, and that his gun was broken and also
obsolete; likewise that he had never possessed
any real gun at all. Of course, that doesn’t
improve Jenkins’ position ; it only proves him a
more foolish Jenkins than he seemed to be before.
It doesn’t alter the fact that he declared war; it
only proves to S. Australia that it has a Premier
who leads it into a row first, and finds out after-
wards that he has only a popgun to carry the row
through with. Now the Popgun Premier wants
to know why the Australian Government didn’t
prevent him making a fool and an exhibition of
himself. He whines that it did not intimate to
him that the certificates were forgeries. Why
should it? Jenkins had nothing to do with the
case. The charge against these Chinese was that
they were prohibited immigrants. Australia was
the prosecutor; nine Chinese the defendants.
Who was Jenkins? Where did he come in? All
that he was doing was making a fool and a laugh-
ing-stock of himself outside the courthouse. Why
should the Australian Government come outside
the court to tell the mountebank who was per-
forming in the street that he was an ass? Mr.
J enkins hasn’t found his level even yet. He can
be even a worse sort of an ass than this without
becoming of any Australian consequence.

Fiscal-Sinking and Locomotives.
If the N.S. W. Labor party would devote a tenth
of the energy to bringing new work into the
country that it does to deciding whether some
scrap of work already in the country shall be done
at Fitzroy Dock or some other place, it would be
better for the party and for N.S.W. also. For
quite a long time past there has been a wild
clawing-match as to where some few engines,
which it is proposed to build in the State, shall be
constructed. So desperate has been the clawing
that one might imagine this was the last job left
on earth, and that when it had gone the sun would
set for ever on civilisation, and the universe
hand in its resignation. The Government estab-
lishment at Cockatoo wants the contract to keep
things going ; the Clyde Engineering Works want
it to keep their hands employed and prevent the
bottom falling out of the local bread and butter
supply. The fate of the Government almost came
to depend on where this job went to—yet, compared
to the State’s demand for ironwork in general, it is a
small job, spread over a number of years, and
during the time it has been wrangled over, foreign
hardware to about three times the value of the
whole affair has been dumped on N.S.W.’s shores,
and as much work as represents the entire cause
of the row three times over has been fooled away
unnoticed. If the N.S.W. Labor party hadn’t
“sunk the fiscal issue,” and had shut out
imported ironwork, there would have been so
much ironwork to be manufactured in N.S.W.—
the ironwork that is noiv imported that the

hungry wolves after this one lone contract, would
have been unable to overtake orders. If the
Labor party in its Federal capacity intends to
continue sinking the fiscal issue, then in its State
capacity it should let this contract go wherever it
darn well pleases. To demand that the importa-

all this sort of work to foreigners, and hasn’t
definitely repented of that policy even yet. The
party’s present locomotive policy and its fiscal-
sinking policy are two things that won’t hang
together under any circumstances.

The Farmer—and His Man.
There was a wild and hairy gathering of farmers
at Bendigo (Vic.), the other Saturday afternoon,
when “it was decided to form Farmers’ Leagues
throughout the district.” All the farmers present
were fairly indignant at something not clearly
specified, but an agriculturist named Read was
the worst. He—
Did not believe in class legislation. It was contrary to
an Englishman’s idea of legislation. (Hear, hear.) He
had been in Australia fifty years, and he felt that the
country was becoming worse and worse every year so
much so that he felt inclined to leave it.
And, curiously enough, just a few hours pre-
viously, a farm labourer named Thomas Upton
had come to something like the same conclusion.
“ a Wr to the Trades Hall Council, he asked
that body to take some action to ameliorate the
condition of farm laborers :
The accommodation provided for such men was dis-
graceful; only about one in a hundred had a proper place
to sleep in Men were put in places that would not be
given to a dog, and it would be a blessing if these abodes
were placed under the Health Act, so that the workers
might be treated like human beings. Further, the un-
specified hour system left the men without an hour to call
their own from dark to dawn seven days a week. The
farmer had more consideration for his dogs and horses
than his men.
Upton did not state that he intended to clear out
of the country, but it is evident that, like farmer
Read, he “feels inclined to leave it.” With the
peat majority of farmers the objection to pay a
fair wage and accord decent conditions to their
laborers amounts really to a disease ; and when it
fs suggested that the roof of the men’s quarters
should not leak in more than ten places, the
farmer screams about socialistic legislation, and
rushes out to form a league. The root of the
whole matter is the mental inability of the farmer
to grasp the fact that the farm laborer is a human
being and a citizen like himself. In the eyes of
his employer the farm hand is something like his
cow or his plough.

Legal Bluff.
Waxcellency Madden, Chief Justice of Victoria,
is engaged in what appears to be a game of legal
bluff. A while ago Madden solemnly announced
his intention of prolonging his ordinary court sit-
tings until. 5.30 p.m., whereat the learned counsel
were unanimously shocked and politely enraged.
Madden, C.J., speaking as one of the brethren
who had changed his line but not his sphere of
usefulness, gave the counsel to understand that
their horror and disgust did them great credit.
He was intensely sympathetic. No Judge, he
said, who considered his duty to the Bar—-with a
Hig - , big B—would prolong the Bar’s harassing
duties until 5.30 p.m. But there had been an
accusation of laziness levelled against their Honors ;
an ignorant public and a viperous press had
imputed a lack of enthusiasm to the judges,
and therefore he (Madden) had made up his mind
to get more work out of the court by way of con-
vincing its detractors that the thing can't be done.
He agreed, with the Bar that his awful innovation
was not wise, nevertheless he thought it right—
which conclusion, by the way, seemed perfectly in
accord with many of Madden’s judgments as
others see them. They are not wise, though he
thinks them right. This unwise policy of rightness
is to be abandoned as soon as the public recognise
the impossibility of continuing it, and then
Madden will be—
only too willing' to do what he conceived to be in the
interests of the public business, and which would involve
going back to the method that would give the Bench an
opportunity to get that sufficient condition of mind in
which it could direct its best powers to its business ; that
would give the Bar an opportunity of properly doing its
work, which could not be neglected and could not be
omitted if the work of the court was to be well done, and
which the Bar could not do unless it had reasonable time
in which to do it.
Madden’s latest utterance on the subject of late
sittings indicates his intention to assume that the
public have had enough of his “experiment.” He
smiles a bland smile, and says “You see, it
couldn’t be done.” The world sees nothing of the
sort. The new long sitting lasted an hour and
a-half longer than the old short sitting. The
extension went to an unnecessary length, in order
that Madden and co. might be publicly over-
worked. There was no reason why the Court
should not have changed its time from 4 p.m. to
4.45. Madden piled on the agony with the ob-
vious intention of taking it off again. Bluff, gen-
tlemen of the jury ; bluff !
The Timid Whine of B. Smith.
Mr. Brdce Smith, whose diseased self-esteem
allows him to style himself “ a thoughtful Imperial
statesman,” whined to a Sydney meeting last
week:—•
If the Japanese came out of the Russian war victorious,
he should expect the Federal Parliament to modify our
existing legislation, which grouped them with other less
progressive peoples, and to place any arrangement limit,
mg their introduction to our country on treaty terms,
instead of effecting it offensively on statutory exclusion.
Which is typical of the low political morals and of
the cowardly attitude generally of the party to
which B. Smith belongs—the foreign-trade, foreign-
borrowing, cheap labor, anti-Australian party—
the set of people who, from timidity or worse, are
ever arguing that the Australian is a poor and
Uriah HEEP-ish object, who must crawl on his
belly before the world, must be very ’umble and
refuse to dare to manufacture anything, or to
strive to get out of debt, and must be always
ready to bow to the opinions of foreigners, Euro-
pean or Asiatic, as to the management of his own
affairs. Bruce Smith says, in effect, that if Japan
comes victorious out of the present war in the
Far North, Australia should tremblingly climb
down on its policy of excluding the Japanese
Rahab and her complaisant husband, and should
allow the Japanese Government to decide what
form of restriction, if any, Australia should enforce
against Japanese immigration. In other words,
Australia is to surrender self-governing rights in one
important respect to an Asiatic power. The man
who advocates such abject cowardice should give up
calling himself Anglo-Saxon—“Mangled-Oater-
pillar ” would be a vast deal more expressive. If—
as is not probable, in spite of the seeming trend of
affairs at present—Japan is finally victorious in
the struggle with Russia, there is not the least
doubt but that the bumptiousness of the Monkey
will be almost equal to the abjectness of our local
B. Smith, and that Japan will be more than
willing to push an intrusive paw into Australian
affairs. But does B. Smith —an Imperial as well
as a “thoughtful” statesman on his own showing—
wish to suggest that 1 ‘ the Imperial connection ”
is not an obstacle to the success of such an ambi-
tion on the Monkey’s part? It is, of course,
useless to ask B. Smith whether, as an Australian,
he was not willing, anyhow, Imperial help or no
Imperial help, to put up a fight for his indepen-
dence against the brown man, for B. Smith, is
not in any sense an Australian, and probably has
no notion of the value of independence. He could
live just as comfortably, and pose just as beauti-
fully, as the political slave of the Asiatic. But
The Bulletin believes that there are still in the
Commonwealth, in spite of such cankers as the
borrowing policy, a majority who would, no matter
what the consequences, elect to fight for their
rights as a.self-governing people rather than yield
them up without a struggle to the Japanese, for
to yield on one point would be to invite further
aggression. Demands grow with granting.
Switzerland, with a smaller population than Aus-
tralia, has kept back the mighty foes who cluster
round her borders. Australia, with a decent
defence organisation (which is unhappily as yet
lacking), would find it at least worth its while to try
to do the same. If not, and if, as Mr. B. Smith
seems to suggest, Britain is too weak and cowardly
to be of any assistance, then presumably Australia
will go down when attacked. But better to go
down fighting than to surrender liberty without a
blow ; and that’s what the B. Smith proposal
amounts to—the acknowledgment of a sort of
Japanese suzerainty over Australian domestic
legislation.

The Benevolent Variety of Tramway.
“Galvo” writes on tramway matters:—
Re A. E. Morgans’ Essendon (Vic.) trams (B. 7/4/’O4).
The bait held out to councils that, in 30 years, the tram-
way systems for which the Morgans class gets concessions,
will become their property, is a common one. If they
only knew it, it means that, instead of only having to pay
first cost for a new system of their own, and pocket the
profits thereon, they will, in 30 years, have to pay in
addition, cartage on a quantity of scrap iron and broken
down obsolete machinery in order to get it out of
the road. And yet the bait has so far been a very successful
one. Perth got its system that way ; Melbourne the same ;
Brisbane ditto. The British-owned Auckland tramway
co., not satisfied with getting a highly profitable con-
cession on these terms, has actually the cheek to ask for
another two years to be added, so that the concession
may date from the time the trams started to run, and not
from the time the agreement was made. Why these
concessions were ever given is a mystery. Given a
town of any size, an electric tramway system,
if economically installed and decently managed,
is almost bound to pay. But municipalities seem
to have a fatal hesitation in taking up any undertaking
that might prove profitable and relieve the rates.
They are ready enough to do the work which capitalists
won’t touch, but to rob dear old “ private enterprise ” of
anything with money in it is seldom thought of. Take
the Australasian systems in detail: Perth citizens pay
enormous fares to support a foreign company, run by
foreign engineers. One has only to look at Melbourne
conductors and gripmen to see that the company’s
money is not lavished on them, although the fares
are ridiculously high. Brisbane’s tramway competes
most seriously with the railways and sends thousands a
year out of the country. Auckland tramways have prac-
tically ruined the railway line to Onehunga, and the co.
now proposes to run baggage cars—it has power in its
concession to do so—in order to grab freight as well as
passenger business. After such examples it is a pleasure
to turn to the socialistic horrors of Christchurch, Welling-
ton and Dunedin. Dunedin already has the first part of
its system in successful operation, and has laid in a
splendid plant, which compares favorably with anything
in Australia, the cars and overhead lines in particular
coming easily first. Christchurch has just let a contract
for its electrical plant, and will have its cars in operation
within 12 months. Wellington will probably start to
collect fares in less than a month.
Sydney system, as a State-owned concern, stands alone.
If it was better managed it could easily be the best-paying
system of the lot. Unfortunately red tape, frill, and
Owe’Sullivanism get in the road. Still it is 1000 miles
ahead of the Melbourne system.
FAR.and away the silliest document issued in con-
nection with the Victorian elections is the Minis-
terial manifesto. _ Except that the country has had
lately so much evidence of the stupidity of Bent,
Gillott, Sachse, renegade Murray and decaying
old Davies it would be impossible to believe that
any body of men could write such twaddle. There
is talk in high-faluting terms of the Ministry
having “restored Responsible Government,”
“ restored the equilibrium of finance,” etc. Then
there are fine rounded periods like the following :
The Labor party, whose aims are avowedly socialistic,
assisted by Mr. Maceinnon and his party, are making
strenuous and determined efforts to wrest from the
majority of the people, and to transfer to the Trades-hall,
the power which will enable it to exploit the affairs of the
State in order to aggrandise the minority at the expense
of the majority.
If you are indifferent, if you fail us, then all that has
been accomplished will be lost, and it will not be long
before you feel the squeeze of the socialists, and their
allies, the Opposition, are waiting to advance merely class
interests, regardless of the general welfare of the country
as a whole.
Electors ! do not be side-tracked into apathy ! Erect a
bulwark of votes strong enough to resist the surging rush
of socialism that threatens to devastate the best interests
of Victoria.
Socialism has its best definition in the law which
the Lord gave to Moses when He provided,
among other things, that every man should
have his own bit of land, and that no
mortgagee, boodler, land-boomer or person who
constructed bogus banks and then paid 2d. in
the £ should deprive him of it permanently under
any circumstances whatever. The “anti-socialist”
howl is mostly the revolt of Bent against the
Lord, and the case of Sammy Gillott versus the
Book of Leviticus.
The confidence yarn circulated by the Australian
dailies as to how their several uncles from Fiji
were dissuaded from investing their cowries in the
Commonwealth by the socialistic trend of this un-
happy country, etc., having failed to excite any-
thing but ridicule, it has been found necessary to
devise other means to vilify democracy. It was
left to the hundred eyes of the Argus to see that
“ the Woman who Did” could be dragged into the
case. In a recent criminal assault case tried at
Shepparton (Vic.) the jury refused to obey the
direction of Chief-Justice Madden to bring the
accused in guilty. Whereupon his Waxcellency
bristled up and said things. Inspired thereby,
Granny published a leading article dealing with
the disagreements of juries, in which occurred the
following gem of deductive reasoning :
One begins to wonder whether some subtle spirit of
revolt against law and order has not been undermining
to some extent the wholesome common-sense of the
people. In the political arena there has long been
preached a doctrine of defiance against existing institu-
tions, against society as at present established. Have we,
in the self-willed lawlessness of certain jurymen, an after-
math, so to speak, of the political sowing ?
The doctrine that the Democratic party is respon-
sible for the refusal of juries to believe the gentle
little modern maiden, what time that guileless
damsel swears that she was chased all night by a
man in maroon through a dense forest opposite
the Y.W.C.A. rooms, is original if somewhat
startling.
The Maoriland Government will meet Parliament
under difficulties when the session opens in June.
Seddon will almost certainly be a cypher, if not
an absentee, for it now seems that the big man’s
career is about ended. Then Joseph Ward is in
less than his usual robust health, and there is only
a team of weak political scrummers to look to for
support. Hall-Jones (who by reason of his
lately-annexed hyphen will henceforth be known
as Half-Jones) will be back from England, but
his forte is departmental work and not debate.
Timi Kara is popular with all sides of the House,
but any aid from him will necessarily be small
when the Cold Tea Goths hammer at the gates
for Jimmy’s unuttered answer to everything
requiring sustained effort is “What’s the
good!” As for Tam Duncan, of the “Lands,”
he is a replica of N.S.W. Pa Brunker— with
the saving clause that he occasionally drinks
whisky. And the remaining member of the
Govt., Charlie Mills, will be quite useless with-
out his prompter, Seddon, whose practice was to
tell Charlie what to say, and to audibly criticise
Charlie s method of saying it. There is much in
doubt as to what changes the coming session will
result in—but that Mills and Duncan will be
jettisoned in the sea of party exigencies is con-
sidered certain. Hogg and Millar are the like-
liest successors, but M‘Nab, of Mataura, who
favors a liberal land policy and a conservative
liquor law, has also a chance.
“O.B.” writes:—
The Papuan slaughter, whatever its dimensions
calls lor an emphatic expression of Commonwealth
opinion on the morality of buttressing missionary
exploitation with the secular power and the carnal gun
This latest shooting down of Papuan natives was
admittedly the outcome of secular revenge for the killing
of missionary Chalmers. But Chalmers had gone into
the barbarous region of Goaribari, not for any State
purpose, nor for any purpose the State undertakes to
advance, but solely to propagate his own spiritual
opinions. He knew he had prejudices and passions
to meet; he knew he carried his life in his hands; that
knowledge was his pride and boast. He volunteered
for the work and was prepared for its possible consequences
That is the theory that vests missionary enterprise with
any character of merit it possesses. But -that theory
takes it out of the sphere of secular government. It does
not matter what other countries do or have done The
Commonwealth should make it clear that it has no
alliance with zealots who leave the protection of the State
and cast themselves, for purely religious purposes into
the arms of savages whose gods they denounce, whose
family life they assail, and whose habits and customs thev
anathematise. The man who goes among the Goaribari
for secular ends is an agent of the Commonwealth
and the Commonwealth must stand by him- w
he who goes among those or other savages to’force
an exchange of religions should be left to stand, as some
say they do, on the rock of his faith. When a missionary
is paid for his work and demands the protection of the
law as well, then he only differs from the trader or the
gold-digger in the fact that his remuneration is more
regular, and he should go out of the halo business and
cease to pose in the limelight.

The person who says that the country is ruined
and that capital has been driven out, is an amusing
individual at times. For instance, at the recent
employers’ shivoo in Sydney, old Mr. Forsyth
was very severe about the exit of capital and the
wreckage of all kinds of industry by socialistic
legislation. He was so convincing that his audi-
tors, if they had taken him seriously, would have
decided to give the old man’s firm no more credit,
and would have rushed home in alarm to see how
much he owed them. But they didn’t, for they
knew that the respectable old gentleman was talk-
ing mainly from force of habit. And then young
Forsyth rose up to explain enthusiastically that
the Forsyth firm was prospering under the ruin-
ous and disastrous system his father had just
described, as it never prospered before:—
The firm had imported the finest machinery in the
world from America. Within the last year we started
this new factory, in consequence of which 40 additional
hands had been given employment. (Cheers.) The firm
had now the best and most up-to-date machinery in the
world.
Now whose capital is it that is being driven out of
the country? Nobody seems to know. The bur-
den of the lamentation of each individual speaker
is : “ Everybody’s ruined by socialistic legislation
except me. lam just building a new four-storey
factory and struggling so keep pace with the
enormous rush of orders.”
