The events of the past two or three weeks cannot possi-
bly be said to have strengthened Mr. It kid's position. The
tactical move by which he voted with the Deakin Govern-
ment, while his followers voted against them, though suc-
cessful in wrecking the Ministerial craft, left Mr. Reid
helplessly amongst the political breakers. Nor has his
openly-expressed dissatisfaction with the action of Mr.
Deakin, in recommending his Excellency the Governor-
General to send for Mr. Watson, and the acceptance of
this advice, done aught else than further damage his
reputation. The verdict of the lobbies plainly is that, in
pursuing the course which Reid and his followers mostly
did, they all, in vulgar parlance, fell in badly. The
division-list was transparent enough, but, when coupled
with the loquacious indiscretions of some members, who
gave away the whole show, and plainly indicated the
manoeuvre that it was hoped would result in turning out
Deakin in the interest of Reid, no one but the self-deluded
plotters expected any other outcome of the Ministerial
defeat but the sending for Mr. Watson to form the first
Labor Ministry of the Commonwealth. To-day members
in the lobbies find a sarcastic pleasure in comparing the
present situation with that created a few years ago by
Mr. Reid when he announced his intention of voting for
the Convention Bill, and left his followers to vote against
it. It is a commonly-expressed opinion amongst members
that the “No-Yes !” incident of the near past finds a
remarkable parallel in the “ Yes-No 1” of last month.
Anyhow .it.fs a very generally expressed opinion that Mr.
Reid has shot his bolt, missed the target of oflice, and is
now much in the position of “ odd man out.” To-day,
therefore, (“Reid and his Wreckers,” as the squad are
called, are not greatly considered amongst members of
the Federal Parliament, except as to how best to dish
them.
“One of the Race’’writes from a North Island
(Maoriland) town :
As one of the “ garish Asiatic tribe ” alluded to in your
leader of the 7th ult., permit me to make a few observa-
tions and comments.
The writer of the article in question is wilfully mis-
leading his readers by insinuating, as he does, that
Wernher, Beit and Co., and the other Rand capitalists
(many of whom, by the way, are not Jews at all) in any
way represent the views of the majority of their race.
They do nothing of the kind. They speak and act as
capitalists, and capitalists only; and to fill their own
pockets they are as ready to ignore and flout the opinions
of their brethren, as they are those of the world at large.
You trot their names out, and make much of their racial
origin, but conveniently forget to record that at a recent
discussion on the subject of the introduction of Chinese
labor into the Transvaal in the House of Commons, it was
Mr. Herbert Samuel, the Conservative member for Lime-
house, who moved the amendment of protest. There are
two or three Jewish members of the British Parliament
who are Radicals, and I have yet to learn that they have
given their vote for the mine owners.
The writer’s allusion to the Jews being an unwarlike
race may be lightly passed by. I should like, however, to
remind him that there are over two thousand in the
British army alone, that they are more than propor-
tionately represented in the armies of Europe, including
even Russia. A member of the “ garish Asiatic tribe,” at
which he sneers, is also Minister of War for Italy.
The three words I have quoted indicate, I think, what
was passing in the writer’s mind. He is a fanatical
“ White Australian,” to whom everyone not of European
blood is a “ nigger,” and therefore inferior. The Jews are
of Asiatic origin, and as, in his opinion, Asiatics (even
when white) are, without exception, inferior to, and unfit
to associate with, Europeans, he is, of course, quite con-
sistent. If the Chinaman, the Japanese, the Hindu, the
Afghan, the Arab and Syrian are a menace to European
society, and should be rigorously excluded from Australia,
why make an exception in favor of the Jew, who is also an
Asiatic, and closely akin to the last-named on my list ?
I am, like many other Jews, an advanced democrat, and
am rather astonished at the attitude taken up by The
Bulletin in regard to my race, as the article complained
of is only a sample in extenso of what I frequently see in
its pages. If a prominent public man, who happens to be
a Jew, adopts a policy of which The Bulletin does not
approve, the fact that lie is a Jew is enlarged upon ; but
nothing is said about his Asiatic origin if his views should
coincide with those of The Bulletin, which itself has no
objection to making use of the talent of members of the
race it sees fit to decry, and which race, notwithstanding
its many faults, has given more leaders in the van of human
progress, in proportion to its numbers, than any other.
This is no idle boast, and, if the writer takes' the trouble
to investigate, he will find that what I say is correct. I
say nothing about its contributions to science and art,
especially musical and dramatic.

There is probably no characteristic of the Tory
mind so constant as its habit of viewing all sug-
gested reforms from the stand-point of what they
will cost. Witness this extract from the debate
on the Navigation Bill:
Senator Pearce : Another matter, suggested to me by
Senator Gutiirib, has brought before my mind scenes
l i* ave often witnessed in travelling* between
Westralia and the eastern States. I refer to the over-
crowding of cattle on cargo steamships. I venture to say
that the Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals
might well turn its attention to the subject, and inquire
into the way in which cattle are crowded on to these
boats, so that in rough weather 25 per cent, of them are
often lost. This Bill does not deal with that question,
but we might insert provisions which would prevent such
cruelty. r
Senator Fraser : That would increase the freight upon
store cattle. ‘
The small soul of Simon Fraser was quite unaf-
fected by the fact that cattle are so disgracefully
ultreated in these ships that sometimes one out of
every four dies on the short trip; but it
instantly discerned that any improvement in
the conditions might mean a rise in freights,
nf. • fc y.? lcal Tory has been so busily engaged
all his life getting fat, that his alleged heart has
apparently become one compact mass of profits,
and when the cold wind of extra expense blows on
him he complains with great bitterness. Yet it is
quite possible—in fact, very probable—that Simon
x! baser himself belongs to the Society for the Pre-
vention of Cruelty to Animals; it is certain that
he is the High Chief Flag-waver of an institu-
tion which is continually shrieking about an
Open Bible—a Bible that teaches compassion

That tough group consisting of Prof.
Moriarty and his three merry men, assisted by
the gentleman blackleg, James Larrabee, and the
bandy-legged safe-breaker, Prince, are back at
Her Majesty’s, _ Sydney, pitting their merely
human wits against the superhuman intelligence
of Sherlock Holmes. The public must love the
investigator thoughit knowsall his tricks and clues,
for it turned up in swarms on Saturday night
to watch the hunt, the double and the ambush,
■with as much zest as if the manoeuvres were over
new ground. Cuyler Hastings handles his con-
tract with the same old assurance of victory,
inspired, by the fact that the dramatist has fixed
things right for him beforehand and Moriarty is
consequently running stiff. But there are a few
very minor changes in the piece. Hastings’ face is
made up red, which is a mistake. Women in the
audience will no longer think he requires comfort-
ing. if he looks healthy, and the hypodermic
syringe will have to give way to lager beer and a
large solid meal of boiled meat. Then Miss
Chevalier, as Alice Faulkner, doesn’t propose quite
so plainly to the detective when it’s time for the
curtain to fall and leave all the good people to
wallow in solid happiness, though whether this is
an improvement or not it is hard to say. Also
Mabel Lane, in a glittering black gown and with
a startling exposition of creamy shoulders, is back
in her old part of the heartless Madge Larrabee.
On Saturday next “Raffles,” an amateur cracks-
man, will make his first appearance at Her
Majesty’s. . Hastings will assume the disguise of
the chief criminal.
** & *
At Sydney Royal the “ Great Millionaire ”
will be withdrawn after Friday night, and on
Saturday “The Golden Ladder” will be staged.
Bland Holt’s choice of pieces suggests a desire to
cheer this hard-up land with visions of consider-
able wealth.
** * *
The difference between “polite vaude-
ville ” and plain variety show is that polite vaude-
ville covers its legs with something more than silk
stockings. When the Bells sing “Come Back to
Me, Barney,” in Castlereagh-street, Sydney, they
wear a frock of green to rhyme with the brogue,
and have the skirt cut well above the garter to
rhyme with the bald old heads in the stalls. But
when Miss Dorothy Drew sings it at the Palace,
as a member of Tom Nawn’s company, she wears
a trailing gown of black spangled net—and seems
to wish she didn’t. The whole Nawn programme
consists of seven turns and a train robbery. The
seven turns are all good, and some of them better,
but the robbery apparently had a wet time coming
across from America and is afflicted with the
jumps worse than usual. Mr. and Mrs. Nawn’s
own contribution consists of a slight, amusing
sketch which opens as a melodrama, develops into
a tragedy, and ends in a pair of roller-skates. On
Saturday, the opening night of the season, a densely-
packed house welcomed it with real enthusiasm,
and when Nawn had no more breath for fancy
tumbles he gasped a speech mainly to the effect
that his heart was too full for words. Pete Baker,
the veteran of the ancient Baker and Farren. co.,
which used to play “ Struck Oil ” in the dim
distance of last century, probably began to have a
different opinion of the house. He opened with
a monologue in the Dutch dialect, which he is said
to have invented, and the gallery got restive. The
Palace isn’t a good place to monologue in ; it has
great height and little depth, so that when the
monologuer patters confidentially to the stalls the
gallery only sees the crown of his head, and asks
him to “chuck it off his chest.” Later on, when
he got to know better what was wanted, and, with
the help of a remarkably mobile face, represented
14 different characters m 10 minutes, the gallery
reckoned Baker was all right, after all. And he
was—it was a very clever and artistic enter-
tainment. Three Johnstons hammered music
out, of a quantity of rosewood, including the
overture from “ William Tell,” certainly the most
ambitious thing ever attempted here by xylopho-
nists, and admirably performed. The “tossing
Austins,” neat jugglers, one of them taking his
tossing seriously and the other mostly engaged in
burlesque ; Katherine Dahl, who sang the quaint
“Hiawatha” craze, heard previously in the same
house when the Stine co. held it, but without the
present picturesque moonlight effects ; and illus-
trated ballads by a lady who hasn’t a voice strong
enough, nor an enunciation clear enough—these
completed the bill, except for the flickering
felony previously alluded to. The new co., especi-
ally on the male side, is a well-assorted lot, which
gives an artistic show.

For his last week at Sydney Criterion
W. F. Hawtrey dipped into the stock-pot, and
drew out ‘ ‘ Little Lord Fauntleroy ” for Saturday
and an afternoon snack on Wednesday ; “ Tom,
Dick, and Harry,” with “A Highland Legacy”
to eke it out, on Monday and Tuesday ; and when
this was written “ The Lady of Ostend ” was being
war-med up for Wednesday and Thursday.
The co. goes north, after a season which will be
best remembered for Hawtrey’s tramp in “Mars”
and Miss Lane’s “Dearest” in “Fauntleroy.”

will begin to be a, married lady at the Criterion
under J. C. Williamson’s management.
* * Kf *
Since last week the Jap. Consul-General
has given his benediction impartially to the Bear
and the other portions of Fitzgeralds’ Circus.
The Osaka wrestlers have had fierce bouts
to determine the championship, which still remains
with a gentleman named Tomato Can as near as
The Bulletin can get it. This is the same Can who
has worn the belt before —the “belt” in the Jap
case consisting of a silk rope tied behind in a huge
true-lovers’ knot. Gordon is still turning himself
into a swingle-bar and dragging 5 tons of wheat
behind him, and breaking chains, and explaining
the simplicity of it all; also he does a clever bur-
lesque of Yes-No Reid. Zaph has reappeared
with his tiger-and-elephant act, and is providing
numerous extra thrills nightly at the old tariff of
4,3, 2, and 1.
* * * *
Jack Carkeek, the first imported repre-
sentative of the English music-hall boom in
wrestling, arrived at Sydney Tivoli on Saturday,
and proved a first-rate platform speaker. The
“ turn ” is something like this (Monday night’s pro-
gramme) :
Hush—the Tivoli’s weird expectancy. Crash-brrr from
the orchestra. Enter Carkeek—a clean-shaven fellow,
rather pleasant-faced, looking not more than 30 or
35 perhaps, close-cropped, in evening clothes. In-
troduces himself (3min.). Says Mr. Rickards (up in
the manager’s box) has engaged Harry Pearce as his
wrestling companion for the tour. French gentle-
man in a box interrupts. Understood to say (2min.)
that he has challenged Carkeek through the papers.
Somebody says the French gentleman represents Marzouk,
the Algerian who wears the crazy-quilt. Difference of
opinion (3min.) as to the style that shall be adopted
when the French gentleman’s champion comes along.
French gentleman is with difficulty understood (3min.)
to insist upon Grseco-Roman. Carkeek says he insists
upon the right to name the style, whether catch-as-catch-
can, Grseco-Roman, or Cornish. Will lay £lO to nothing
(“ which are pretty good odds even in Australia ”) that he
will throw the man in 15 minutes. “ Right ” from the
box. More speech from Carkeek, saying they may reckon
the match fixed for next night. (3min.) Voices from all
parts wanting to know what’s wrong with' to-night. The
manager could tell, only he’s in the office counting up the
money. Introductions all round: “ Dr.” Gordon, referee ;
stage-manager Glynn, time-keeper ; Harry Pearce, the
other party. Carkeek explains some more—what catch-
as-catch-can is. “ You’re not much to look at,” irrele-
vantly and untruly from the gallery. Carkeek annoyed:
“ You’re not any l(/,000-dollar beauty yourself.” (3min.)
Disappears and disrobes—smartest thing of the evening.
(15sec.)
At this point there is a short interval for wrestling,
mostly on the mat. Pearce, swarthy; Carkeek, fair and
smooth, and apparently giving away some weight.
Pearce defensive ; winded and panting in 3min. Carkeek
cool and deliberate ; no bustling. Pearce pretty obviously
outclassed. Shoulders down. Shake hands. (4min. Bsec.)
Re-appears in a dressing-gown. Another speech ; tells
a story (4min). London, music hall; Carkeek performing.
Old lady asked to see him. Apologised for interrup-
tions ; said her imbecile son mqde them, so Carkeek’s
back-answers had hurt her. Said he~then resolved never
again to notice interruptions, or answer back, for fear of
again hurting the unfortunate mother of an imbecile son.
And he never had. Evidently forgot the “ no 10,000
dollar beauty” episode earlier. More remarks (lmin.)
from the French gentleman. More remarks from Carkeek
(2min). More crash-brrr from the orchestra. The
wrestling is over.
Nat. Clifford continues to do the best eccentric
dancing seen since the earliest days of Johnny
Coleman; and the Bells and the Six Empire
Girls, and the rest of the strong second-part bill
continue as previously chronicled.
* * * *
Melbourne Argus had a wailful leader
lately on the apparent impossibility of making a
permanent success of orchestral music in the city
by the Yarra. That, too, despite the efforts of
Marshall Hall to forward the cause. Signor Hazon
may now stroke himself down with his own baton.
For 14 years he has been the heart and brain of
Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society, and the con-
certs which he directs would not be second-class in
the Cold Capital itself. The society opened its
season at the Town Hall last week with great
glitter and brilliance. That original pianiste, Miss
Florence Taylor, and Mr. J. W. Aitken, who
sang a love song, deserve flattering mention for
their work, and the undertaking deserves to be
patted generally.
* * *
The Sydney Chamber Music Society will
start another season of its attractive concerts on
June Ist. Herr Gerard Yollmar, of the waving
locks, is surrounding himself with the best
musicians, and something mighty is expected.
* * * #
At Melbourne Princess’s the event on
Saturday was the first Australian appearance of
the touring “Gaiety” co. from England, by way of
America. The word ‘ ‘ Gaiety ” applied to a visiting
co. has not quite outlived its original significance,
and doubtlero most of the audience on Saturday ex-
pected a wild, leggy burlesque—a variety enter-
tainment such as Fred Leslie and Nellie Farren
headed in the long ago. But that was very long
ago. The people who brought “Three Little Maids”
to light are from the same school as those who in-
troduced “ A Gaiety Girl” and “In Town” nine
years back. More artistic as comedians, they are
decidedly weaker in song and dance. The early
musical comedies, being haunted by old burlesque
traditions, depended largely for success upon their
“variety” items. “Three Little Maids” is
quieter toned, and the strength of the perform-
ance lies in its unstrained assumption of natural-
ness, and its continuity of intorcst. A thin 3-act
story is made the most of by the careful “ produc-
tion,” or staging, whilst the leading man of the
co., who neither dances nor sings, presents his
own original caricature of a true British peer.
* * * *
The Maids are the daughters of a hard-up
vicar of a parish that includes the local golf links.
The usual society Lady of the district (Miss Maud
Hobson), who is attended by the usual society
girl proteges, engages the vicar’s fair young brood
to go to London and earn their daily scone and
perquisites as waitresses at her fashionable tea-
room. She is a wise, commercial-minded Lady,
and she reckons that the rosy rural maidens will
treble the takings at her shop. But before the
girls leave the dear old home in a donkey cart
they achieve the individual mash of three golfers
from town —a long-pedigreed lord, a vivacious
Frenchman, and a seriously sentimental lover,
contrasted like the trio of intruding bachelors
in Pinero’s “Amazons.” Next act shows
the Maids repeating their mash in their
disguise as Dutch waitresses at the London shop,
where an eighteenpenny cup of tea possesses
strange medicinal qualities, and the broken edge
of a sponge cake is hard enough to cut glass.
Finally the Maids reappear as the vicar’s
daughters, and triumphantly drag their adorers
from the clutches of the Society girls. Just here
it may be remarked that the vicar amounts to
a weak spot in the landscape.
* * * *
Misses Madge Crichton, Delia Mason and
Elsa Ryan are three pretty little actresses with
sympathetic little singing voices—the first a com-
paratively pert maid; the second, demure and
soulful; the third, remarkable for the expressive
smiling of her eyes. Most clever girls can look
sweetly wicked, but Miss Ryan has a larger assort-
ment of wicked glances than the average angel.
These three name parts are filled to admiration, if
not to perfection, whereas the society spinsters
bring no particular charms into the shows. Miss
Maud Hobson (the only link between this
‘ ‘ Gaiety ” co. and the previous one) is the same
handsome Maud as before, with an improved
manner, but her presence on the stage is more
important than her acting opportunities. And
there is a casual low-comedy lady in a bucolic bon-
net. She dances a duet with George Carroll,
a very diminutive comedian who follows in
the well-remembered footsteps of Fred Kaye,
and is moderately amusing. Maurice Farkoa
scores freely as a captivating Frenchman, although
a long odd lock of white curled amidst his black
hair, doesn’t impress one favorably at first sight.
Perhaps the snaky white coil is an acquired taste,
or perhaps one must be a woman to appreciate its
loveliness. Maurice Farkoa possesses no voice
worth mentioning, yet he fetches sincere encores
for his expressive French-colored warble, and he
has a way with him—a Rudinoff way. J. E.
Fraser commends himself as a pleasing young
man and a minor baritone. His duet with
Miss Mason in the first act is gracefully
done. The remaining half of the company
consists of G. P. Huntley in the part of Lord
Cheyne, and there we have a performance that
looks inimitable. His lordship is a slight exaggera-
tion of a lamentable truth. His eye-glass, his in-
human deportment, his method of speech, his air
of being half-conscious of his own absurdity, and
quite satisfied with the result, make him an edu-
cation for less intelligent actors of similar char-
acters. Lord Tweenwayes, in. “The Amazons,”
was a doddering, unamiable young idiot who
drivelled haughtily of his inherited diseases, but
Lord Cheyne is a cheerful creature of cir-
cumstances, a Johnnie who brags of having
“ led a fearful life,” an ass by instinct and
by the influence of environment, rather than
from utter mental incapacity. Huntley’s char-
acter study abounds in convincing touches.
The Lord Dundreary played by American actor
Sothern in the ’6o’s and ’7o’s was a less subtle
satire on British aristocracy than is this up-to-date
presentment of an egregious type of “swell.”
Dundreary is dead ; his ghost still walks the
Italian and French stage when a comic English
lord is required, but the bones of the languid,
lisping, stuttering man with the prolonged whis-
kers are now laid upon the shelf of many a noble
family vault. Lord Cheyne flourishes in his stead,
and the House of Peers is still there.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal the lurid wife of many
husbands is concluding “ Her Second Time On
Earth,” and the place where she buried the luck-
less organ grinder will be occupied by “ Tommy
Atkins ”on Saturday. ‘ ‘ Atkins ”is a more or less
military drama, first played in Melbourne in the
way back time when Kipling wrote the Hempire’s
songs and Australia helped to sing ’em.
** * *
One or two “turns” are being retired
from the Opera House this week and another one
or two will fill the gap. The programme is strong
and long, and one of the later items that looked
weak on its first night appears less flabby than it
was.
The early Roman Christians eternally talk-
ing of their Faith in the language of Harmy
preachers, and the ancient Roman pagans who
talked of nothing but Marcus Superbus, also the
lions round the corner who only roar at the first
sight of Stephanus and are profoundly silent when
Marcus and Mercia go to meet them —all these
matters will be discontinued at Her Majesty’s
after Friday. These are the last two nights of M.
Superbus’s earnest appeal to Caesar for the life of
just one girl (which shows M.S. at his best), and
the final repetitions of Poppea’s emphatic “No.”
On Saturday, there is to be a revival of “Silver
King,” with Miss Jeffries as Nellie Denver and
Julius Knight in the big boots of Wilfred, the
converted punter.
** * *
Johnny Wallace, probably the oldest and
best known of Australia’s representative stage
managers—veteran John who, in days of yore,
revolved around the late Harwood in “ Tambour
Major,” and placed this country under a deep
debt of gratitude for merriment received—John
is going to enjoy a benefit at Her Majesty’s,
Melbourne, on the afternoon of May 27th. The
whole strength of the theatrical profession just
now in Melbourne will be drawn upon for this
occasion.
*# * *
Adelaide Tivoli has been crammed during
Cup week by people wanting to laugh at “Mama’s
New Husband.” Stine and company are out after
laughs, and get them—American fashion—by any
means that comes handiest. Olive Evans is a
desirable proposition whether dressed as 17 or as
7, and the Bicknells’ dancing is of the Darto or
whirlwind nature. The competition has been bad
for the Pollard co. at the Royal. “DjinDjin”
brought with it a pleasant flavor of panto-
mime-in-lavender, with the songs and jokes of
yesteryear, when Japan was considered merely a
humorous kind of country. A nation that does a
lot of hard and successful fighting somehow suffers
as a background for decorative effect. All the
same, the big earthquake scored heavily as of old.
Nellie Wilson, an Adelaide girl, makes a first
grown-up appearance as the Prince in this pro-
duction. This week, one Bronson of New York, is
engaged with an Anti-Cigarette Society and a
Polite Lunatic and other mirthful trifles.
The Wild Cat Column.
Apropos of the Independent Order of Foresters
life assurance stench, it may be remembered that some
time ago certain questions were asked in the Victorian
Assembly about the soundness of the miserable and
bumptious concern. Then Premier Peacock (who, it now
transpires, had received £5O from the society under very
remarkable circumstances) rose like a man and a
brother (he was a brother, and the fat poten-
tate of the Order who came here from
Canada is understood to allege that he was a very
cheap brother whose first year’s premium was paid
for him, though the statement isn’t at all borne out by
evidence), and delivered a vague and eulogistic reply,
tending to show th£t the 1.0. P. was so sound and repu-
table that any inquiry into its solvency was quite need-
less. It now leaks out that the reply was not Peacock’s,
but was supplied ready made from Canada, presumably
by arrangement with Dr. Montague, the fat potentate
of the society above mentioned (the same man who handed
Peacock the £5O), and apparently the cable communica-
tions with Canada over this matter were charged to the
people of Victoria. Also it transpires that the questions
were drafted by Dr. Montague —that the whole thing
was a put up job to advertise the institution which gave
the foolish Peacock £5O. Under these squalid circum-
stances the assurance of Brother Peacock about the
honorable record of the 1.0. P. is quite useless, and the
need for a rigid inquiry into the solvency of that dreadful-
looking concern is more urgent than ever. The whole
business only tends to make the 1.0. F. official look like a
person who was very anxious to avoid inquiry, and who
started a bogus inquiry to get ahead of any real one ; and
Peacock looks like his humble servant and obsequious
hanger-on. The fact that this insolent concern deli-
berately pasted its advertisement on the Victorian
Parliament by getting a member to ask readied-up
questions about the 1.0. P. and getting the Premier to
deliver a readied-up reply, gives it an extra claim to be
investigated. Australia has a right to know fully what
kind of institution has been hanging its placard on our
Parliamentary institutions. Also it has a right to know
some more about the fat and corrupt bungler from
Canada w ho engineered all this silly business.

From the evidence of the first financial secre-
tary of the Melbourne branch of the 1.0. P. “ assurance ”
office :
We were allowed to keep members financial. There
was a fund we could draw on for the purpose.
Is there any other case where you had a cheque
from the funds of the society to pay anybody’s pre-
miums ? —Must I answer that, your Honor 2
Yes.—There were others.
_ You need not mention their names. Was it a
usual practice ? There were only a few prominent
names.
When the new insurance legislation comes along it ought
to contain a clause absolutely prohibiting any society
issuing or maintaining a policy out of the funds
tit the society (this is, of course, quite different from keep-
ing it in force out of the policy’s own surrender value).
Even if guinea-pigs and decoy ducks can’t be barred out
of the ordinary financial wild cat, they ought to be barred
out of the life assurance business, anyway ; for the assur-
ance business is invested in by thousands who are not
professional investors, and who don’t even know that there
are such things as guinea-pigs.

now more than enough to pay 100 per cent, per annum on
the total amount actually put into this remarkably
successful concern.

It cannot be said definitely what the amount of one year’s
final dividend is until the next year’s balance-sheet has
been issued, so in the foregoing the amount of dividends
shown are those paid during the year. If the ordinary
process were possible, and the amount of dividends paid
in respect of each year’s operations were set against that
year, and if the other appropriations made in respect of
the year were taken into account, the last line would
show very differently; for the 5 per cent, bonus which
usually accompanies the final dividend (paid in March for
the preceding year) has been dropped, and £35,828 has
also been transferred from revenue account to suspense
account, in order to provide for the slump in American,
British, Australian, and other securities in which the
funds are invested. Approximately, if the balance-sheet
showed all the year’s operations, things would work out
in this way:—
Income. Profit. Dividends.
£1,130,516 .. £12,919 .. 15 per cent.^£24,446
And the reserves, after the final appropriations made in
respect of the year, would be £657,634. With an improve-
ment in securities the amount now in suspense (£50,000
altogether) would, of course, be retransferable to reserves.
The recent shrinkage in ordinary trading profits—in 1902,
in fact, there was a heavy loss—was due to the co. going
heavily into the employers’ liability business before it
knew anything about it; before, in fact, anybody knew
anything about it. Gradually, however, rates have been re-
vised as experience dictated, and a considerable amount
of business has been dropped, with the result that profits
seem to be mending again, though they have not yet
mended to anything like an extent that justifies the
present extravagant dividends.
* » * *
Paunch M‘Kinley, now putting up for Victoria’s
House of Rather Fat Landlords, has a wondrous patent
scheme for relieving the State of the pension incubus
that Bent perpetually fumes about. It seems to be
the same scheme as Bent’s. At present the pensions
amount to about £350,000 a year, which M'Kinley
“has discovered, after great difficulty, represents a
capital liability of £3,500,000.” He proposes that the
Government shall make arrangements with one of the
big insurance companies to take over this liability on
consideration of an immediate cash payment of three and
a half millions, to be obtained by issuing bonds at 3* per
cent. This interest, with 1* per cent, added for a sinking
fund, would mean, he reckons, a reduction by one-half of
the annual pension payments and a consequent saving of
£175,000 a year. It is a beautifully simple scheme, by
which some fool of a company is to agree to pay £350,000
a year for an indefinite period in return for £3,500,000 —in
other words, it is to pay 10 per cent, on the money. The
humor of the situation lies in the fact that a respectable
concern would probably want nearly £3,000,000 to insure
the men already on the pension list; to insure also the
multitude who have still to come on the list when they
leave the service wouldmost likely require about £7,000,000.
Anyhow, if the Paunch man’s scheme is sound, why drag a
company into it at all. Why not let the Government
finance the matter itself and keep the profits ? The only
profit that an insurance office could make in the matter—-
the profit out of which any real saving of £175,000 a
year could be effected—would have to be made out of the
fact that the Government would borrow the £3,500,000 at
3* per cent, and the insurance office would invest it (if it
Sad luck) at 4* per cent. Therefore the thing is quite
simple. Let the State buy a large box and imagine it has
-put into it its own securities for £3,500,000. Let it put
into the box the £157,500 a year which the securities
would yield at the rate of 4* per cent., that being some-
thing like what the best insurance offices realise on their
investments. And by the success it meets with in
paying out of the box £350,000 a year for pensions it can
decide what size of an ass Bent is and what dimension
of a mule is Paunch M’Kinley. If the £157,500 which
went into the box wouldn’t pay the £350,000 which is
supposed to come out, then the scheme falls through as a
saving, though it may be a postponement. M’Kinley, by
the way, doesn’t explain why he hasn’t tried the thing on
his own business first. Say Paunch’s wages sheet amounts
to£2ooo a year; this represents for that item alone on
AI Kmley’s reasoning a capital liability of £20,000. Why
doesn’t M’Kinley borrow £20,000 at 3* per cent., put by
1* per cent, for a sinking fund, “ arrange with a big insur-
ance co.” to pay the wages and so save £lOOO a year ? For
such a genius of a financier M'Kinley seems a painfully
thriftless, casual sort of person.

This doesn’t look as healthy as it might; but a good deal
of the unhealthy appearance can be fairly exp amecl
away. During the previous two years there had been a
boom in most American securities, as a result mainly of
the Pierpont Morgan operations. The securities of the
American life assurance offices are valued by Government
officials annually, and as a result of these valuations the
assets were, in those years, written up considerably. n
1901 this appreciation of assets added £964,509 to the in-
come, and in 1902, £247,951. Now, however, Pierpont
Morgan has lost his Pierpont and is just plain Morgan, a
sort of head-clerk in Rockefeller's office ; the booin is
dead ; and the Government valuation knocks £1,915,712
off the value of the society’s securities. This comes off
the income as so much loss, and affects the amount added
to funds correspondingly. If no heed were taken of these
market movements the past three years’ record would be .

Which puts a different complexion on the Mutual’s face.
Even then, of course, the rate is high—about twice as
high as the most cheaply managed of Australian offices.
But, to a great extent, that is because wages and salaries
are high in Protectionist America, where the co. does by
far the greatest part of its business. Even the insurance
canvasser can command good wages where the business
isn’t overrun by unfortunates who can’t earn a crust at
anything else.
Interest earnings continue good. The funds at
the beginning of the year amounted to £78,089,284; at
end of the year to £81,981,491. On the year’s average,
the return was at the rate of £4 ss. 4d. The A.M.P-’s
rate for the same year was £4 Bs. lOd ; on the other hand,
the British offices usually earned considerably less than
the American office—the Scottish Provident, for example,
£3 16s. 5d., and the Provident Life £3 19s. sd.
As was expected, South Australia did not get an
offer for the building of the overland line. The Arltunga
wild cat killed whatever chances there ever were of push-
ing the business through. The yarn of the “ mountains
of gold ” in the interior, all waiting to be tapped by this
line (the syndicate was offered all mineral rights), was
overdone. Bull-Cohen too easily recognised the voice as
the voice of an irresponsible book-fiend standing in his
doorway and talking wild advertisement ; and as soon as
Arltunga fell in and exploded the wild yarn of the
canvasser, Bull-Cohen slammed the door and refused to
listen to anything that might be said on the subject.
The moral seems to be that the methods which will sell
a pen’north of peanuts or even a book on the instalment
plan aren’t to be relied upon when it comes to hawking a
huge railway concession.

The co. will, from present appearances, have to recon-
struct before ordinary shareholders can hope to get any-

in the Maitland district. The present paid capital is

which £6061 is still callable. As usual, the pref. shares
are the bugbear. Even in good times 12£ per cent, is
enormous, but in bad times it is impossible ; and, with
the keen competition among producers, these are . bad
times for N.S.W. coal co.’s. The old Wickham mine is
now idle, for, though there is a little coal left, it doesn’t
pay to work at present prices ; and, though the new mine
could probably be worked more cheaply, the difference
wouldn’t be enough to provide for any 12J per cent,
dividends.

Mr. A. C. Rowlandson, who trades under the

The Australasian Sheep Dip Co. (Sydney):—

1903 .. 891 .. Nil .. Nil .. 2344
This record shows the hard row which is hoed by the co.
that tries to set an industry on its legs in Australia. The
co. can apparently make very good sheep and cattle dips
(it has good references as to its character, anyhow) ; but
it has no Protection, and so it bumps up against tremend-
ous opposition. The importing crowd is constantly
crying about capital being driven out of the country,
and here is a pretty convincing proof that the importing
crowd is the crowd that is doing the driving. An industry
which, with a little Protection, might easily employ 50 or
100 workmen, and profitably employ also £lOO,OOO of
capital, gives without Protection precarious employment
to perhaps half a dozen men, and the £15,488 sunk in the
co. hasn’t yet earned a dividend, And last year over
£5OOO capital was driven out of N.S.W. to pay for im-
ported dips alone. Last year’s loss was, of course, mainly
due to the drought, which reduced the turnover nearly
50 per cent. The co. appears to allow a rebate to share-
holders who use the dip, but the amount isn’t shown in
the accounts. The visible deficit as shown above has been
increased from time to time, apparently, by expenses in-
curred in extending the business to other States and
South Africa.

The Ballarat Woollen and Worsted Go.
(Ballarat, Vic.): —
Profit. Dividends. Reserves.
Sept., 1902 £17,389

Sept., 1903 .. 2215 15 „ „ 2249 17,376
March, 1904 .. 2308 15 ~ ~ 2249 17,434
The most remarkable thing about this little co., one of
the most successful of Australian industrial enterprises,
is that it maintains its excessive dividend in the face of a
heavy overdraft; its interest bill is usually about £6OO a
year, and at present is still more. As to the national
value of even such a comparatively small manufacturing
concern as this, it is worth noting that last half-year it
paid for wool, stores, fuel, &c., £12,752, and in wages and
other charges £9638. # #
Most important announcement made by Broken
Hill Junction directors in their half-yearly report is that,
“ from time to time, as conditions (of the metal markets)
varied, estimates of working results have been made, but
the calculations have shown a probable loss. To resume
operations with profit there must be a rise in the prices of
lead and silver, or a better recovery of metals.” Metal
markets are even less favorable now than they were a few
weeks back. All the same, the co. hardly knows what it
can do, as the remodelled plant has never been tested.
The ore lately developed is as good as any recently found
at Broken Hill, and if the mine could be economically
worked (which is the trouble with all these little northern
mines, that ought to be amalgamated) a fair profit should
show. The co. has now a surplus of £3688 of liquid assets
over current liabilities.
* » * *
Broken Hill Block 10, which made a loss of
£1777 in the half-year ended Sept., 1903, made a profit of
£12,680 in the half-year to March, 1904. This is by far the
best result since March, 1901; in fact it is double the net
profit made in the intervening 2£ years. Of course this
does not include payments for the new plant or even for
the new main shaft; on the former £13,618 has been
spent in the six months, and on the latter £4934. These
things have been provided for mainly out of the reserve
fund, which stood at £50,000 when the remodelling of
mine and works started but is now down to £8772,
represented by Victorian 3 per cents, at face value ; and as
Victorian 3 per cents, are about 87, over £lOOO must be
written off that. As there is still a great deal to be done
and paid for, and as the directors are apparently averse to
making calls (there is £35,000 uncalled] notwithstanding
that the overdraft at the end of the half-year was £13,591,
dividends are hardly likely to be seen yet awhile. The
old mill, which was built on the side of the hill, and
almost over the lode, has been affected by movements for
years past; and, though these movements have given a
lot of trouble lately, and affected the running of the mill,
only three or four half-years have shown a larger output
of crude ore treated. The value has, however, been a
good deal less than formerly; in the half-year ended
March, 1900, it was 15T40z. silver and 16T6 per cent,
lead ; last half-year, 13’8oz. silver and 15 "2 per cent. lead.
During part of 1902 the grade was still higher ; but at
that time, with the metal markets very weak, and the
mine and mill generally under a heavy cloud, the poorer
faces were not worked. That means that if the mine
had been worked during the past half-year as it was worked
during a good part of the previous two years, when only
the richer ores were taken, the profit would have been a
good deal greater than £12,680. Portions of the new
plant are now getting to work ; and with this and other
economies (including cheaper coal and freights) going in
its favor, and with the lead market still 12s. per ton"
higher than last half-year’s average, the co. ought to do
still better in the current six months.
* * * *
Such a poor apology for ore-treatment is the
ordinary concentration process at Broken Hill that Block
10 has only saved about 36.8 per cent, of the silver in the
ore during the past six months, and 65'08 per cent, of the
lead. The result can be expressed in another way :
Silver, oz. Lead, tons. Zinc, tons.
Metal Contents .. 962,912 10,606 12,141
Metal Recovered .. 355,800 6,932
Metal Unrecovered .. 607,112 3,674 12,141
And even what is here represented as recovered is not all-
recovered ; there are still considerable losses in smelting.
The enormously greater quantity described here as un-
recovered is the field upon which the magnetic separators
and the acid zinc-processes have just made a start. In
the Block 10 case the metals in the ore milled during the
six months were worth, at market value, approximately
£481,500; and of that £121,700 was recovered, while
£359,800 was left in the tailings, or was otherwise un-
recovered.

“I’llbuy Brothers One ! I’ll buy Brothers One ! ”
Thus Langford for weeks past in the Melbourne Stock
Exchange ring. He jumped shares from 7s, to 15s. 6d.
without getting a set-back, so that his average can’t be
high. Who is at the back of him? Is it a bull deal or
genuine buying? Bears look blue, but the little man
plugs away just as he did when Briseis bulling was on.
Some of those outside don’t like him taking up Brothers
One, and they say that the little man is suffering from some
variety of share madness. Let him tell the history of the
North Lyell deal, and then they may realise that man-
nerisms don’t count for much. As for Brothers 1., it’s a
moot point whether the mine is worth £60,000. A lot of
calls have yet to be made, and it is not demonstrated that
the wash will pan out high-grade. The drift has been
riddled by drives on the false bedding, and the best of the
stream tin extracted.
* ■* * *
Pioneer, Bradshaw Creek, is the best tin show
in Tassy. It struggled along for years in A. Mellor’s office
in Queen-street, but shareholders would not put up
money. So Gibbs, Bright and Co. got the show in hand,
and it has proved a gold mine to them. The capital they '
subscribed has long since been returned, and divs. are
regular and big. Mellor had hard luck in having to let.
the show go, and in afterwards replacing it with the dis- -
appointing Mayne’s.
* * * *
New Moon, Bendigo, is a big mine with a fair-
sized battery. Estimate of ore in sight, nine years. Yet
co. does crushing for other shows. It is in little ways like
this that Bendigo is miles behind the rest of the world—
except, perhaps, Charters Towers. When stone is proved,
why keep it in the mine ?
Zeb Lane and George D. Meudell are whispered
to be conjoined over some outside Bendigo leases. Any-
way, G.D.M. has left for London. So it would look as if
Bull’s purse-strings are to be loosened. Meudell some
time back sought to get for his people rights to a big
block of mining land on the Bendigo extension in return
for doing some boring, but Travis, at the Mines, wasn’t
on. Too much land was wanted.
* * * *
Old North Duke, Maryborough (Vic.), is not
giving up the ghost without another kick. Bores ate to
go down to try aiid pick up the run of westerly wash
being worked by the Duke United. This is a sort of for-
lorn hope, especially as the wash out that way is dropping
in grade.
* * * *
Hustlers Reef No. 1, Bendigo, is knocked back
because the end of the tribute is getting within sight.
But Reefs don’t improve in proportion. Why ?
* * * !*—
Lansell, Dyason, Mueller and co., are very slow
about opening up the Virginia, Bendigo. They ought to
have picked up the Great Northern shoot months back,
but are going about it like a front mark man that doesn’t
want to win. Mine ought to have a big future, what with
existing profits from upper-level dirt and the certainty of
obtaining the Great Northern stone left at the boundary.
Lansell owns about half the show, and it’s a big mine.
* * * * €
Sebastopol Plateau and Ballarat East co.’s are'
quarrelling over water troubles. Meanwhile, water is
rising and men are idle. The folly of the thing is that, if
the co.’s don’t take care, they’ll shut-down the mines alto-
? ether. Divs. have not been conspicuous from the
lateau for many a day, and holders will welcome any
pretext to end dead call-paying. Only real pros-
pecting being done is at the Star of the East, which
is going out to try and pick up the old Albion lode. It,
too, is partially hung up over its drainage fight with
the Central Plateau. Difficulty lies in a legal quibble as
to formal registration of co. 20 years back,
♦*- * *
Dead J. A. Wallace’s Chiltern Valley-mine
(Vic.) is turning up trumps again, yields having got well
over 300 oz. Last week’s output was one of the biggest
for the field for years, though Great Southern has been
doing exceptionally well. If Chiltern Valley comes out
O.K. it will be a big feather in the cap of Wallace’s widow,
who fought for and won the right to handle the old man’s
mines and scrip. •
* * * •
Duke of York, Bamganie (Vio.), ja promising
sortie fun again. Brokers seein inclined to forget that the
stock not long since was the central figure in a corner
which passed out some of their number. Shares dodge
up in little spurts. A poor return was preceded by a nice
little strike of stone at No. 2 level. The balance thus re-
mained even. Very little is needed to set the market
jigging, but the dance will lack the old profes-
sionals, who shy at handling a stock with a history.
* * # *
Gympie (Q.) is using the diamond drill in
prospecting for the first time. Bore is to be sent down
1000 feet from the bottom of the West of Scotland mine,
and the carbons will therefore produce a sample from a
total depth of over 4000 ft. Mention of 4000 ft. at Gympie
resurrects one of Philp’s Bills for concessions to Scottish
capitalists, who were to sink a shaft to that depth, and
generally shake Gympie to its foundations. Gympie is
still waiting for those capitalists. The use of the drill on
this field is cheering. Gympie men do not take kindly to
modern ideas of exploration or extraction as a rule, and
in this connection the fact that last year’s production was
a record, in approximately 37 years’ work, is noteworthy.
The stone is getting poorer, but Gympie is beginning to
learn what quantity means in economical work.
* * * *
Croydon (N.Q.) has had two important develop-
ments, and now announces itself convalescent. In its
time it has produced well over 1,000,0000 z. of gold, but,
like most Queensland goldfields, has been prosperous
only in spasms. Its erratic output and its remoteness
are responsible for the little attention it gets from
outsiders. Recent crushing from Murray’s Block (400
■tons for 15380 z.) made things hum locally. Show, worked
by tributors, declared dividend of £7O per 30th share,
and the “ big men ” of the field fell over one another in
the rush to get in. Probabilities are that this rich patch
will give way to a lower average when a reasonable tonnage
is attempted. Big tonnage and low average, however, is not
in the creed of Croydon and some other fields. They
“ wolf ” during the fat period, and when the low-grade
stuff fills the faces they wail for foreign capital. This is
the trouble pretty well all over Queensland. Croydon’s
other development—the strike of gold at the 920 ft. level of
the Golden Gate—is of considerable importance. The
show, owned principally by Charters Towers people, sank
Plant’s shaft to 1200 ft., and then evidently decided that
what it was looking for—the Golden Gate reef —had
been passed through at 920 ft. The crosscut put out struck
what is alleged to be this reef at 60ft. Quite possible it
is the right reef ; but it will be well to wait and see what
gold the stone contains.
* * * *
Charters Towers (N.Q.) last month accounted
for 47 per cent, of Queensland’s gold yield, 45 per cent, of
its divs., and 43 per cent, of its recorded calls. Croydon
calls were not recorded, or latter figure would be less.
Figures for the field for April: 23,935 tons stone for
20,4820 z. ; cyanide gold, 48670 z. ; old tailings, 550 tons for
1230 z.; total, 25,4720 z. Divs., £31,400 ; calls, £7367.

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open, competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have' accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Rivited Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

THE TIMIDITY OF CAPITAL.
BERT, THE STOWAWAY (from Australia, explain-
ing his position to captain): “ Well, you see,
dear hoy, the—aw—beastly Labor party had
just come into power, and capital was leaving
the country, don’t you know, owing to beastly
socialistic legislation , and the — aw—exodus of
the better classes of the community had—aw—
definitely set in.”
The Queensland Full Court has rejected
the appeal in the case of Angus M ‘Donald and his
wife, who beat, starved, and tortured their 14-
years old daughter to death by a process of slow
misery, extending over a number of months. Their
hanging, if they are hanged, will excite about the
irreducible minimum of sympathy. Horton, of
Adelaide, the eccentric individual who shot his
wife, and who was strung up by Jenkins Iscariot,
was, on the other hand, a case which might have
aroused some sympathy in the heart of any
Premier who hadn’t been hardened in his youth
by having the dog sooled after him when he was
trying to sell a Picturesque Atlas.
* * • *
The proposed conference to deal with local
government in N.S.W. does not meet until the
last week in May, but already the chief item on
the business-paper has been settled. The harbor
excursion has been fixed up, the Government
having chartered a steamer to take the visiting
country cousins for a day on the big dam. No
conference in Sydney is complete without its day
out on the harbor at the country’s expense.
• * • *
At a large and fashionable Melbourne
bazaar held recently, a young woman was
decoyed into a raffle for a gold chain, valued at
about something like 15 guineas in the shade.
By some mischance she was the winner. When
she went to the stall to claim it, the gorgeous
ladies gathered round and remarked: “What a
lucky girl ! ” After some delay the Marquis of
Moonee Valley or the Earl of Footscray presented
her with the chain amid much palaver. His
concluding words were touching. “ May you live
to wear it out,” he said, bowing gallantly till his
bald head wrinkled like a heliograph. She did.
Next week there were unmistakable signs of brass
coming through the dye. A jeweller informed
her that it might be worth half-a-crown. From
there she went to a solicitor. The Marquis of
Ascot Vale or the Count of Mordialloc may live
to regret the fulfilment of his gallant wishes.
• • • *
A further extension to the Divorce Law of
N.S.W. has been given by Justice Simpson in the
Kinnear case. He ruled that if a husband was an
habitual drunkard for three years, and during that
time did not support his wife, she was entitled to
a divorce, even though she had had ample means
of her own and had not required support from her
husband.
• * * •
There was a humorous touch about the
escape of two men from the Terrace Gaol at Wel-
lington (M. L.) recently. There were three prisoners
in the cell, and a hole a foot’square was bored in
the wall with a brace-and-bit. Two got through,
but the third was fat and stuck ; so, while his com-
panions were scudding across country in the cold,
wintry night, he stuffed a blanket in the hole and
sat down to consider how [best to explain that he
hadn’t noticed the orifice, or observed the departure
of his mates.
* * • *
That Poo Bah, the Victorian policeman,
has to hold on to his head with both hands at times
to keep himself from getting hopelessly
tangled amongst his many appoint-
ments. Recently, in a country town,
he was directed by one of the
numerous departments he serves to
take legal action against a householder.
He did so, served the summons as
policeman, prosecuted in court as sum-
moning officer, and as clerk of the
court recorded the conviction. Costs
were allowed ; in his capacity as police-
man he collected them and handed
them over to himself as C.P.S. As
C.P.S. he paid them into his special
account, drew a cheque and paid it to
himself as summoning officer duly
issuing a receipt in the latter role.
Then as S.O. he sent the money along
to the department, which was almost
the only thing in the whole concern
that wasn’t himself.

“F.C. ”: lam pleased to observe
that, immediately on his arrival, the
A.D.C. of Governor Tallbottle, of
Victoria, was appointed to an hon-
orary captaincy in the Commonwealth
forces; but lam grieved to see the
Governor of a democratic community
allowing his aides to bestow all their
honorary patronage on one profession.
An A.D.C. who would become an
honorary barrister, or an honorary
medico, or an honorary clergyman,
during his term would be a pleasing
innovation. Also, what’s wrong with
an hon. cook ?
The Cooktown (Q.) Independent says that
Mr. R. Garvey recently discovered an interesting
relic of the visit of Capt. Cook to the Endeavor
River. This is a halfpenny coined in the year
1749 ; it bears the head of George II on one side
and Britannia, in a form and dress different from
the modern picture, on the other. The coin was
found when cleaning out a well near Webber
Esplanade, where it is supposed Cook watered his
vessel. But anyone could lose a George II half-
penny at any time, and this unseemly haste to
ascribe the battered brown to Captain Cook, who
isn’t likely to have brought money ashore with
him when he went to look for water in a savage
country, seems rather ridiculous.

* * * *
“Miss Siam,” the Adelaide Zoo elephant,
died on May 9 from calculus —a stone as big as a
man’s head being found in the bile ducts. She
had been in the gardens for 21 years and was, like
almost everything else in Adelaide, presented by
the late Thomas Elder. The present director, in
bewailing the old days when his father filled the
position, remarked recently : “We don’t get as
much money from the Government as we used to,
and the entrance money has fallen off because we
have to make Saturday a free day. Then we have
no Elder. In father’s time Elder would say :
* Minchin, why haven’t you got an elephant ? ’
‘ Can’t afford it,’ he would reply. ‘ How much
will it cost ? ’ ‘ About £250 by the time it gets to
the gardens.’ ‘ That’s all right, get it, I’ll pay for
it.’ The same with other things. It was always
‘doit’ or ‘ get it, and I’ll pay for it.’ ” Adelaide
still has wealthy men, but they don’t throw
elephants about with the freehandedness of old
Uncle Tom.
# • * *
On top of their chronic state of over-
crowding, Melb. hospitals are now rushed with an
epidemic of diphtheria. The big institution has
two little patients in each of its five diphtheria
ward cots, and 11 more in six beds in the septic
ward. Also cases, of both classes, have to be
turned away daily. The little sufferers are treated
and sent home, returning again next morning for
further treatment—and, of course, spreading infec-
tion by the way—until they get better or die. All
the other hospitals—even the Eye and Ear —are
similarly congested. Meanwhile the incompetent
noodles who allegedly “manage” the Queen Vic-
toria Infectious Diseases Hospital fund have kept
that finished institution lying idle for three years
now, because apparently they haven’t energy
enough to open it and get it going.
» * * *
The “honorarium ” again ! The other day,
after a Melbourne police magistrate had, without
comment, fined a restaurant-keeper 305., with
475. costs, for working one of his waitresses 72\
hours a week —12J hours beyond the legal maxi-
mum—an amateur beak sitting alongside him,
one J. J. Rogers, must needs chip in that “he
thought the Factories Act was altogether too
severe in making a prosecution of a case like the
present: it savored of persecution.” Rogers
didn’t explain how many hours he thought a
waitress should be sweated before the Law stepped
in. In comment next day, Chief Sec. Gillott said
“the legal 60 hours was enough in all conscience”;
but he didn’t do what he should have done— i.e.,
order Rogers off the bench for condemning a law
he is sworn to administer honestly and impar-
tially. And, anyhow, a man who openly apolo-
gises for such conscienceless sweating seems unfit
for any public position—except, perhaps, that of
gaol-flogger.

Urgent military news reported by the Age
war correspondent at Lancefield encampment:—
This morning- a pair of horses in a D.D. waggon bolted
down the drift over some very rough country, but were
brought to a halt before any accident occurred. The
waggon contained the lunch of the G. 0.0. and his staff,
but a broken glass summed up the damage.

The fool Carlton (Vic.) honorary beak
again! An unsober individual who watched this
season’s first football match, against Fitzroy, got
so excited at Carlton’s defeat that he stoushed
three inoffensive strangers, badly battered a con-
stable, and nearly caused a general riot. He had
■fifty prior convictions, yet the beaks let him down
with a 40s. fine, assuring him they were dealing
with him thus severely because he appeared in
court in a state of beer ! The honorary magistrate
seems to exist for the encouragement of assault,
battery, cruelty, and general blackguardism.

Melbourne seems rather a desirable place
just now for giddy little damsels wanting pro-
tectors. One such, charged with vagrancy and
remanded on bail, tripped gaily into court, in a
new dress and an artificial blush, when her case
was called, and proffered her scareely-dry marriage
lines to the staid old beak as a complete answer to
the charge. She had snapped up a young Gipps-
land farmer, and was going away immediately to
dwell in the bush and be virtuous and happy. The
solemn old magistrate smiled inscrutably and
passed on to the next case. ,

Sailormen fight shy of Fremantle (W.A.).
The local police magistrate is a native of Bunbury
and a schoolmate of Bigjohn Forrest, which
accounts for a lot of things in Westralia. Sailors
seem to be his pet antipathy, and he punishes
their trivial offences with a severity that is pro-
bably unparalleled in any other part of Australia.
Poor devils, half-starved, and knocked about by
harsh skippers, apply for leave to break their
articles when there is no hope of escape from their
floating hells by other means. Deaf to all ex-
planations, they are sent to gaol, and their pay
docked in addition. Recently, eleven men on a
barque, just arrived from America, struck work,
as a protest against the bullying of the captain.
The men swore that the captain had threatened
to shoot them, that they considered their lives
were in danger, and they refused to sail with him
again. That entire crowd was sentenced to three
weeks’ hard labor, and ordered to pay all costs of
the proceedings. The whole conduct of this court
wants digging into.
* * * #
The Westralian Chief Justice gave a deci-
sion the other day which he owned was just about
as likely to be wrong as right. It was an inter-
esting situation. The local Truth had told the
local Trustee Co. that it ought to be ashamed of
itself for turning respectable white tradespeople
out of shops and letting them to the little fat
brown woman from Japan, and the co. felt
lacerated in the conscience, or the balance-sheet,
and asked for a large amount of compensation.
The old idea of a co. in such matters was that it
hadn’t a body to kick nor a soul to lose; and
though the Chief Justice thought that the position
was not quite as bad as that, he didn’t quite know
how you could put the co; into the witness-box and
cross-examine it about its conscience and how it felt
when it was torn. It was suggested that the
chairman of directors might say how he felt about
the matter (under the W. A. libel law it is neces-
sary for the plaintiff to go into the box and submit
to cross-examination); but, on the other hand, it
was suggested that the rest of the co. might have
a different sort of conscience altogether—might, in
fact, have a conscience that liked being handled
this way. And when the chairman did go into
the box he did not know anything much about the
co.’s feelings, having only just been appointed
chairman ; and the question was whether the co.
could be said to have gone into the box according
to law, when all that went there was a gentleman
who didn’t know anything to speak of about the
co.’s affairs. Apparently the easiest way out
of the difficulty the Judge said that he would let the
case go on, and so give the jury the chance of say-
ing whether there had been any injury done or
not; and the jury said £lOO. It seems a good
deal of money to make a paper pay for saying that
a co. had let shops to a dear ally of ours, for
whose _ success against a white nation we are
whooping for all we are worth. In fact, it looks
like treason or something.
• • * •
Kangaroo Island, the stronghold of Dowie-
ism in S. Australia, is beginning to have its doubts
about the prophet. Kangaroo Island mostly lives
by growing barley, being a rocky wilderness which
will grow hardly anything else. Now, barley is
used for making malt, and malt goes to make the
beer which is anathema to the prophet; and the
question arises whether they are to give up Elijah
or their living. A few of them have crayfished
out of the trouble by selling their rocks to sinners
who mock at the prophet; others (the supply of
sinners who want to grow barley having ap-
parently run out) are making it a subject for
prayerful consideration. Meantime, they are
planting more barley.
“ War E.” : Be the Junee (N.S.W.)
hospital scandal (B. 5/5/’O4), here are a couple of
incidents of hardship out of many. A child,
whose parents can ill afford it, was sent by Dr.
O’Flanagan to St. Vincent’s Hospital for treatment,
because the local hospital is closed against his
patients. A woman suffering from enteric fever
was debarred the right of going into the local
hospital, because she asked that Dr. O’Flanagan
should attend her ; as a consequence of having no
food, no nursing nor proper attendance, the under-
taker secured a job ! Dr. O’Flanagan commands
an extensive practice amongst the poor of Junee,
and to every one of these the State-subsidised
Junee Hospital closes its doors ! The whole busi-
ness is a monstrous scandal, and in the interests of
justice and charity the Chief Secretary should
institute a searching inquiry. Surely the Govern-
ment has a right to ascertain how its subsidy is
misspent!

The philo-Japanese of N.S.W., up to May
7, had subscribed £447 to help the pagan to down

“F.C.” : At a Melbourne boarding-school
one of my room-mates was a youth named Sammy.
He was the concentrated essence of a prig, and
once, when he returned after holidays, brought
with him a choice enlargement of his own photo,
E resented by his sisters, cousins and aunts. This
e stuck over his bed while heathens scoffed.
Next term (he had in the meantime captured
most of the prizes) he brought back another
presentation—a wax bust of himself, which he
proceeded to instal on his locker. This was too
much, and the bust promptly disappeared, being
subsequently found, in a disgracefully mutilated
state, in a place that need not be mentioned. I
had forgotten Samuel until I read lately that
Sammy Gillott, Melbourne’s ex-Gaud Mayor, had
been presented with his portrait in oils and a
marble bust of himself. Now lam trying to
remember what was the surname of my old
Sammy.
* * * *
The case of the barque Magnet, recently
towed into Hobart for repairs, reads like one of
Clarke Russell’s interminable, droning sea-yarns,
in which there are pages and pages of the gentle
wash of the deep against the side, in between the
stereotyped calamities—the fire, the mutiny, the
storm, the going mad of the captain, the appalling
discovery that the mate is a gorilla in disguise,
and the falling out of the bottom of the ship. The
Magnet left Glasgow in April, 1903. It came to
such bad grief inside the first 100 miles that it put
into Ardrossan the same month for repairs.
Started again, and fell out of one calamity into
another, and struggled to Mauritius for repairs.
Caught fire there and needed more repairs. Then
the Magnet pursued its sorrowful way with ill-
omens and albatrosses and disasters thick upon it
till it reached Hobart clamoring for repairs. The
voyage occupied a year and a day. Such a vessel
should be re-christened the Cassandra.
• • * *
Catmittee of Melbourne Women’s Hospital
has shed its claws at last. In future patients will
simply be labelled “Mary Jones” or “Kate
Smith,” as the case may be, and often is; and no
married woman will be able to flaunt her moral
superiority over the infant-laden spinster. Until
lately, the patients were subject to a system cf
numbering—which, amid their continual comings
and goings, made Ann Brown, just arrived, liable
to receive the close of physic prescribed for Wini-
fred Robinson, just left. Under the common
sense system of full names, without prefix of any
kind, it is reckoned that each woman will get her
own physic without her character being inquired
into.
* # * •
Since the vituperative Torrey visited
Maoriland that place has been overrun with
peripatetic evangelists who have found that as a
religious “claim” it produces good results. Now
the local sky pilots are beginning to kick
against this foreign competition, and talk of a
Parsons’ Union, with a demand that the alien
evangelist should be kept out. Rev. Dr. Gibb
told the Wellington Ministers’Association ( apropos
of an application to welcome a certain touring sin-
smiter) that they were getting too much of a good
thing. •. He felt that by these constant visits of
excited and extraneous evangelists the people were
being tempted to belittle the ordinary dull-brown
services of the kirk. The Association signified
that it thought so too.
* * * *
Some interesting remarks were made on
the Bible-in-Sehools question at the Presbyterian
Assembly, recently, in Melbourne :
The Moderator (the Right Rev. Arthur Davidson), in his
opening address at a meeting of the commission of the
Assembly to-day, said that there was no blinking the fact
the continued exclusion of Scripture lessons from the
regular curriculum of our schools had been largely, if not
solely, due to Romish influence. Politicians had been
deterred by fear of alienating the Roman Catholic vote.
Boiled down the theory is that the Protestants
want the Bible taught in schools and the Catholics
don’t, and that politicians deliberately flout and
scorn and offend and throw away the votes of over
900,000 Protestants in order to secure the votes of
260,000 Roman Catholics. The Right Rev. per-
son had better think out a more plausible tale than
that.

MOGG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Reported from Bacchus March (Vic.) that a numerously-
signed requisition was presented to Mr. B. B. Mogg
yesterday, asking him to become a candidate for the
Southern province. Mr. Mogg thanked his friends for
their good opinion of him, buo said he had no desire to
enter the Council.
To a far-off Southern town
Came a deputation bland,
Put a requisition down,
Spoke with oratory grand :
Six electors and a dog
Came to wait on Mr. Mogg.
“ Save the country ! ” so they spake
“ From the Socialistic flams ;
Put your own name in the wake
Of the Pitts and Gibbs and Hams 1
Added to the catalogue
Be the lustrous name of Mogg 1
“ Unto Mogg the settlers cry,
’Tis for Mogg the farmer pleads ;
Only Mogg can satisfy ;
Mogg is what the country needs.
Lead the country forth from fog
Into daylight, Mr. Mogg ! ”
But that noble soul replied
“Nay, the letters M.L.C.,
Tho’ their fame be blazoned wide,
Have no charm at all for me.
Cease your tempting apologue 1
Let me still be simply Mogg ! ”
Thus the hero they did leave
By his hedge of eglantine,
Lulled to sleep each dewy eve
By the lowing of the kine ;
Or the music of the frog—
Manly, modest Mister Mogg 1
A.
THE FALLEN STATUE .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SAD, 0 sad white thing !
f J 0 Miracle of Art !
What windy hireling
Hath flung thee here apart ?
0 uttermost despair, »
0 mystery of Fate ;
No might, nor skill, nor care
Gan thee reintegrate.
Is it for rage, or shame,
The night-dews, in thine eye,
With cabalistic flame
Flash tears that will not dry ?
See, how the heavens glow!—
And Night enfolds the spheres ;
And fiery thunders throw
Their sparkling battle-spears.
The ivy and the grass,
That once, about thy feet,
N.S.W.
Did cling and hold — alas,
Now weave thy winding-sheet.
God wot the time is now
Arrived the world may see
The mould upon thy brow,
For all Eternity.
The moss within thy lips,
A web about thine eyes,
The rose’s thorny strips
Across thy naked thighs.
And pime will never cease,
Nor will thy fetters fall,
But climb and blind thy name,
From off thy pedestal.
0 uttermost dispair,
0 cruelty of Fate,
No might, nor skill, nor care
Gan thee reintegrate.
HUGH R. M'CRAE.
TRIOLET.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
Thought reads his riddle book
Until Life dies.
Within Time’s eerie nook
Thought reads his riddle book ;
There’s none therein may look
With mortal eyes !
Thought reads his riddle book
Until Life dies.
K. CRCWFORD.
