A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 17, 1904.
My Dear Abini
Last Thursday Lady Northcote, who has been
busy making a nest for herself at the Macquarie-
street house, took her first pleasure trip since
arrival in Sydney. In the forenoon State Governor
and family called for the Northcotes, and the
party got on board the launch Premier and
headed for Middle Harbor. Her Ex. is so de-
lighted with her first dose of Sydney natural
beauty that nothing short of a counter-irritant—
say a view of Broken Hill—will restore her to the
normal nil admirari attitude.
With a musicianly G.-G’ess at Government
House one foresees a rather good time for local
musical talent. Constance B. Usher and
Yvonne Leverrier are amongst those already
bidden to make melody. It will probably not be
long before someone tells her Ex. of that remark-
able player, Florence Taylor, whose rendering of
a Lisztian concerto with the Amateur Orchestral
Society last week is still a theme for admiring
comment. Miss Taylor is credited with genius!
She is a Goulburn girl, and has never been out of
this State for tuition.
To return to our Northcotes: The G’ess.-Gen.
is a great horsewoman, and rides often and far
with himself. Two Arab steeds, a brown and a
grey, which carry the vice-regal weights, are
snorting in the Macquarie-st. stables. Possibly
some indolent Sydney women will now gal-
vanize themselves into a love for horseback
exercise which, according to an old riding
master of my acquaintance, “strengthens
without enlarging the limbs.” Maybe to it, in
some degree, Lady Northcote owes her good
figure. She has a bend in her back that is worth
one of her necklaces. So far, the Northcotes have
not done much motoring, but they are thinking of
getting a ’bile, and so increasing the perils of the
South Head Road. By the way, all their horses,
save the Arabs, are N.S.W. beasties. In India
also, with few exceptions, their horseflesh was
Waler. When I used to read in Kipling of the
colonel’s daughters rushing about Simla on un-
groomed Walers I did not know that the typical
Waler was Monaro born. But, as someone has
remarked, even the youngest of us doesn’t know
everything.
On Thursday morning the consuls called offici-
ally on his Federal Excellency, so there were some
awesome uniforms abroad. I note that the Consul
for Honduras was amongst them, and I can
imagine Northcote anxiously asking beforehand
“Where’s that? Quick, Nevill ! Look up the
value of its geographical position and the what-
ness of its staple products.” There is, by the way,
a funny contest going on as to who is the genuine,
hall-marked consul for Patagonia, or some such
place. There are two fierce claimants to the office,
and the military may yet be called out to
settle the question. Meanwhile a friend of
mine has applied for the position of consul for
Thibet, on the off-chance that the last of the
Hermit Kingdoms may be dragged out of its
shell. If only Jorgen Jorgensen’s republic of Ice-
land had lasted there might have been a consul for
Mount Hecla. I believe some consuls compose a
uniform to their own fancy. The most gorgeous
uniform ever seen was built at a George-street
tailor’s a year or so ago for the King of Tonga.
His Majesty designed it himself, and also invented
the orders to be worn with it!
Last Tuesday week the Northcotes met Sydney
folk at St. Vincent’s Ball. Seems a far cooee
from now to then, but the mere memory of the X
rays that streamed from Lady N.’s diamonds and
emeralds on that occasion makes me want to reach
for smoked glasses. Little Miss Fanny Doyle and
others who work like beavers for this annual whirl
are quite capable of inviting the Archangel
Michael himself to come down (with his sword
and wings) and balance to corners in the vice-
regal set of Lancers. And before ever they
had got his celestial reply they would have
advertised that “The Winged Captain of the
Heavenly Host and the members of his staff
will enter the Town Hall on the tails of comets.
In consequence of this Distinguished Patronage
the price of gallery tickets will be doubled, and
the coffee will be made in a coffee-pot instead of
as heretofore in a tea-pot.”
It was a beautiful ball, and Lady Northcote’s
gown of apricot-colored satin, with mother o’
pearl sequins, “went” very harmoniously with
its neighboring gown—that of Lady Rawson, who
wore pale heliotrope with rose point-lace. Her
Federal Excellency’s bodice was tight-fitting,
with a fold of apricot chiffon round the top of the
decolletage and a pink rose in front. Below a wide
collar of diamonds she wore an emerald and dia-
mondnecklace. The green stones are, I’m told, quite
flawless, and amongst the finest in the world. Her
tiara, bracelets, and the jewels in the front of her
corsage did the ball proud, and the wearing of ’em
all proved that her Ex. wanted to look her shiniest
in Sydney’s sight. Lord Richard Nevill intro-
duced all the Somebodies and Anybodies to the
new people, and the galleryites who were opposite
the dais got the worth of their two shillings in
laughing at the bob-curtseys made by the intro-
duced women. I hear that the curtsey should not
be made to an Australian G.-G. at all. It is due
only to the G.-G.’s of Ireland, India and Canada,
and to the Jam of Mysore. In the Northcote
wake were Captains Balmain and Stevens. The
latter looks as if he wrote love-sonnets and did silver
point etchings. He has soulful blue eyes and the
bind of moustaohe that Lord St. Asaph presses on
the Hon. Eleanor Boscawen’s jewelled hand. The
Balmain man looks like the young gentlemen in
David Jones’ advertisements of their new Spring
overcoats. He might be a pirate or an academi-
cian. A forbear of his gave Balmain its name,
and once owned that suburb. If Captain Balmain
doesn’t know it already, I’ve the pleasure to
inform him that he has the honor of being related
to a most charming Mosmanite (or should it be
Moswoman ?).
Amongst those who bobbed a curtsey was Mrs.
A. A. Blow, an American lady, wife of the
manager of the Dapto smelting works. She is a
large, really handsome woman, well-dressed and
well-jewelled, who has bulked considerably in
Sydney society for the last year or two. I felt an
interest in Mrs. Blow from the time I was told that
the gold shield lettered “ F.F. V.” which she wore
on her breast signified that she belonged to the
“First Families of Virginia.” Presently I asked an
American of some note and considerable travel if
well-born Virginians did such things. He gasped
in horror, “ Not on your life ! No more than the
English aristocracy or Knickerbocker families of
New York.” Then h® sat down murmuring
“Well, that’s the limit! To have to label your-
self well-bred. Why even a well-bred dog doesn’t
need to carry a placard to prove he’s not a
mongrel.”
A group of statesmen at the ball took their
pleasures seriously, as if they were digesting the
Athanasian Creed. Hairy, beardy Justice
O’Connor, who danced with Mrs. B. R. Wise in
the vice-regal set, looked as if he could gobble up
the slender little woman. If she had been in red
instead of white the pair might have posed for the
wolf and Little R. Ridinghood. The O’Sullivan
went about looking like a deficit cartoon of him-
self. His daughters almost danced themselves
thin. Before 12 o’clock a stately minuet was
trodden by Mrs. J. C. Williamson and the mem-
bers of Her Majesty’s co.
The Northcotes are busy depasturing official-
dom. On Saturday noon Premier Watson was
yarded for a pink-and-white-clover browse. On
the evening of the same day they gave their first
dinner party. Acting Premier Wise and Mrs.
Wise, the Suttors and a few others were guests.
A long-drawn case concerning corsets, now run-
ning its length to the great profit of the S.A.
Bar, includes a batch of evidence taken on com-
mission in the United States. The “cute”
rejoinders of Yankee dressmakers to Yankee
counsel cause much amusement to the court that
gets them at secondhand. A general disposition
to object to some of the evidence, as being
elicited by leading questions, was suppressed by
Chief Justice Way, who says that, in his
experience, you really cannot tie down
American lawyers to the rigid rules that
are obeyed here or in London. Another nice
point arose through John Downer’s dramatic
reading of the evidence, and Symon protested
strongly against having to accept all the fine shades
of meaning which Downer’s organ-like voice gave to
what somebody said in New York. John Downer
can read about his own party’s corset as if it were,
a poem, or a work of art, or a great effort of
philanthropy, and put a subtle inflexion on the
enemy’s corset which at once arouses hatred
towards it and conveys the news that it is a ser-
pent and a malignant invention of the devil.
Why weep ye by the tide, ladye—for the tribe
of Australian singers, players, and writers who
have gone hence to London ? Think of how
cheaply they may live —provided they are earning
something. A dinner of ox-tail soup, roast lamb,
two vegetables, plum pudding, and coffee for
! That’s the dinner the Queen and an orchid
retinue ate lately at a restaurant founded by Her
Majesty. And, on the authority of the Times,
she took pot luck. We are assured that
the factory girls had just the same menu.
It is significant, though, that the Queen
and her retinue were served in a private room.
That shakes our faith in the statement that
the ’Arriets were dining off equally good fare.
And the Queen didn’t go down in her working
clothes—the ones she wears when she dusts the
drawing-room in the morning—nor did she travel
incog. The suite arrived unostentatiously in two
carriages, and her Majesty tendered 2s. 6d. for
eight tickets, and was subjected to the ignominy
of being bailed up for another 6d., and by this
time the news had spread, and the crowd got on
the tables with its boots in the soup to look at the
visitors.
I did not know, till a few nights ago, that a
Sydney city-street pould appear a spiritualised
thing—something large and noble, with the star-
light streaking the quiet roofs, the lamp-glow
lying on wet pavements, and the clear tones of
bells that told the hours sounding down the quiet
ways. “Our Lord God holds the watch.” Imagine
anyone murmuring that (quite appropriately) in
Lower George-street, too ! It was close upon one
o’clock in the morning when 1 and my maternal
progenitor, returning from a ball, and ignorant of
the ways of all - night trams, sat down on
the steps of the Bank of North Queensland
to await a car that after midnight runs only every
hour. We had the city to ourselves, save
for a few gigantic policemen casting double
shadows in the cross lights. Now and then a
sympathetic officer would come along and talk
tram with my m.p. whilst I took a pasear down
Bridge ; stj , eet. Bank and warehouse cats were
peering from every alleyway and porch with the
large, curious eyes of woodland creatures. Cats
were crossing the street to see one another and
exchange cellar gossip, and when I rejoined the
m.p. quite a cloud of cats occupied the bank step
ivith her. One was a fixed-deposit cat—she was
fat and _ sleek, with defiant green eyes, and evi-
dently lived comfortably on ner income. Another
was a half-grown, starved pussy mewing “n.s.f.”
(not sufficient food). A third wore a proud,
corpulent air. She might have been an over-
draft cat that was backed by good security,
and felt that she represented profitable business.
There was a cat with weird expectancy in her eyes
whom I at once diagnosed as a promissory note
cat, who felt she was rapidly falling due and had
little hope of meeting herself. I don’t think the
bank had discounted her; I fancy she was just
lodged for collection. Then there was the furtive
cat, that had evidently fallen due and been
renewed with difficulty, and the cat that had been
written off as bad at last balancing time, and had
had no legal existence since. The hunted-looking,
shifty, transient cat had probably been told to
remove her account by reason of the unsatisfactory
nature of her business.
Miss Dena Cooper, who replaced Miss Mabel
Lane as “Dearest” in Hawtrey’s production of
“ Little Lord Fauntleroy,” is the daughter of a
deceased squatter of this State and a niece of
Justice Cooper, of Queensland. She made her
debut in “Ben Hur,” and her progress since then
has been considerable.
Mrs. Napier, the young wife of the commander
of the Euryalis, is the stylish-looking lady in grey
that Mrs. Fanshawe is seen about with these days.
Miss Annie Praed, who is the first woman to
take the financial-looking degree of L.D.S., issued
by the new Dental Section of the Sydney Uni.,
has hung out her brass sign in Sydney. She is a
native of this State.
Frances Ross, of the Bland Holt Co., is a marvel
who contrives to keep looking not more than £8
years, though she must be quite 30. Her figui e
actually looks that of a growing girl— at least it
does from the back view. And her face can stand
the noonday light without a veil. Yet her daugh-
ter, who has been about with her these days, is
taller than herself. Her son died some time ago
in Maoriland.
Miss Bancroft, who takes wicked or intriguant©
rdles in the Hawtrey Co., is an Adelaide native.
Her father was on the Adelaide Register from
the day of its inception up to a few months ago,
when he retired to grow roses and read Seneca.
His actress daughter is a young widow (surely
there’s a viscount or a millionaire squatter waiting
for her somewhere), and took to the stage after a
short wedded life of four years.
Signora Hazon, one of the most beloved of
women in Sydney musical circles, is seriously ill
after undergoing an operation for tumor.
School pupils of Miss Mercy Murray, B. A.
will give a dramatic performance of “Jack ard
the Beanstalk,” and “ Ali Baba” and those
memorable thieves on Wednesday, May 25, in the
Queen’s Hall, Pitt-street. The frisk will be in aid
of that worthy charity, the Women’s Hospital.
Also, do not forget St. Margaret’s Hospital hall
for next Wednesday. Mesdames Tudgay and H.
G. Quinlan, hon. secs.
North Shore Hospital is in urgent need of cash,’
and is going to earn an honest crown by means of
a ball, in Mosman Town Hall, on Friday, 27th.
Hon. secs., Mesdames Cameron and R. Arthur.
A young woman—a real grown-up—wearing
sandals on her bare feet is the latest sight in Syd-
ney streets. A well-cut short skirt gives feet and
ankles a good show, and those bold things, men,
are not slow to take advantage of the lady’s
generosity.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The way from Melbourne to Malvern was
strewn for the past two weeks with the profane
language of people going to officially call on the
Talbots. Stonington is execrably placed. No
matter how economically the journey to the re-
mote house is made, the cost amounts to the price
of a pair of gloves and involves the loss of half-a-
day’s time.
“Things were reversed at a Toorak shivoo a
week or two ago. Instead of the eiders receiving,
and any available children being just on show at
the lull of the programme, the youngsters ‘re-
ceived,’ and the grown-ups were put aside with
the pot-plants, only being called to take part when
th 3 infant principals felt fagged. It was a pitiable
game to look on—the short-frocked kids being
imitative and monkeyish to the last degree. They
employed the high shake insolently and the staccato
‘ How-do,’ and all the tricks of the Society trade.
And anon they exchanged inanities in the identical
manner of their mamas. Toorak has so little
humor that the youngsters were felicitated on
their hosting it so nicely, and the nice locality
thought nothing of the travesty of its own drawing-
room manners—the monkeying of the monkey.
“They get no end of fun in a certain theatre
watching the qualms and emotions of a harried
mummer who hangs about the wings while the
lady of his choice casts herself officially on the
bosom of the stage hero. The poor chappie
expresses admiration with difficulty, and says,
when he isn’t gnashing his teeth, that the fierce
but furtive embraces of the inflammable hero are
‘ good art.’
“No woman is a pearl to her maid or her
dresser. A wardrobe-mistress in this State turns
up her eyes every time one of the envious chorus-
girls recites from an English journal the progress
of a certain Australian beauty. ‘I wonder,’ says
the dresser, ‘if she is any cleaner by now! ’
Cleaner! The lily-like and most patrician
dazzler who is favored even by Royalty ! Cleaner !
In the old days, according to this dresser, the
beauty’s lingerie was often on the same level with
suspicion.
“A girl complained to me the other day
of her tram experiences in your city. In 10
tram rides she had a comfortable seat but
twice. Other times it was stand up or sit on
somebody’s lap. * Whose lap ?’ I asked. * Oh, it
was on any old lap. You couldn’t choose, other-
wise I wouldn’t have complained.’
“Senatah Matheson, with the newest London
trousers, is back from England. Mrs. Matheson
and a daughter just at the end of her teens follow
later ; but the trousers came with him.
“ Bard of Works and Drains Eitzgibbon is many
things besides being master of ceremonies, autho-
rity on etiquette, verse-builder, and chief of the
Kow-tow tribe. He is an authority on Blood, not
the ‘product of the bullock that falls down with
a thud,’ but Blue Blood. Just now he is giving
his attention to the Talbots and their family, and
the Governor will possibly be rejoiced to find an
appreciator of Blood in a community that very
often can’t track down its own father.

“Miss Maud Hobson has, of course, changed
with the years. She is riper, heavier —as large as
Blanche Wallace —and within an ace of forfeiting
her elasticity of form. With the aid of the dress-
builder and the corset-maker she just manages to
swing her massive figure into graceful lines. She
wears the f rocks of the show. One is autumn
grey with an elusive silver streak waving horizon-
tally about the skirt. A second is in mauve tones
and a Marcus Stone hat. A third is in magpie
shades and has generously shed its entire top tier.
“ The tootle about * Humbugging Men,’ with
whioh Miss Madge Crichton hits the humor of the
crowd, was Ada Reeve’s particular triumph in the
city by the doggy Thames. Tourists, on Saturday
night, told of Ada’s exquisite treatment of this
saucy songlet and her irresistible piquancy through-
out the star part, in which she coruscated for one
jolly week before her break with the co. Miss
Crichton shrewdly leaves the Reeve business out.
■ “The ‘three little maids’ of the Gaiety group
made a dainty first appearance in Melbourne, in a
typically English scene, with flowery golf-house,
sunny links, frigid fashionables, ‘flannelled fools,’
and a real live donkey. Stage charmers are so
prone to fluff their thatch and dock their skirts
unduly, whenever an appearance of comparative
youth is required, that tne Gaiety trio won a good
mark straight away by depicting the parson’s
nearly-grown-up daughters as nice girls, with their
pretty hair in demure ‘pigtails’ and their white
muslin gowns just short enough to show their
natty brown shoes.
Maurice Farkoa, the Frenchie who croons love
lays with a wealth of satin gesture and a seductive
softness of tone never before attained by a male
tripper on our boards, wears a grey wavy streak as
a. forelock. Globe-trotters grinned when they saw
it. Eighteen months ago he had London audi-
ences guessing at its genuineness. It’s evidently
a mere coquetry on the part of the mummer.
“ The latest idea in theatre-dress is an exquisite
kind, of scarf not much weightier than a spider’s
web and as delicately toned as a tiger-lily. It is
drawn taut over the shoulders in shawl fashion,
or slackened till it merely falls over the elbows
and makes a graceful drape for the arms.
“Society is in the grip of anticipation at a
coming divorce suit. The fair dame is the
petitioner. She returned from a trip abroad
quite recently, almost incog., landed with an
equal degree of quiet, and is domiciled in strict
privacy for the preparation of the rdle of plaintiff.

meeting, last week. The event that was once the
gayest of all the fresh-air affairs passed off as
tamely as the altruistic prayer that reminds us to
love our enemies and be good to them that dis-
parage our best hat.

lady, and now he has been again reminded of his

“Last week a returned globe-trotter (a
Federalist) sampled ‘The Marriage of Kitty’
here. He had seen the original performance in
London with Marie Tempest playing Kitty.
Knowing this, one of the Williamson staff
approached him for an opinion and comparison.
There wasn’t much difference, said the playgoer,
in the two productions, after all, and the pair of
Kitties, though so far apart in comedy experience,
came within coo-ee of one another in the part.
But, said he, Rose Musgrove squeaks like a rat
when disguised as the rural spinster, with absolute
absurdity. Marie Tempest gave a hoydenish
cackle that stamped the situation and colored the
disguise to perfection. Next morning a Williamson
emissary waited on the Federalist to ask, with
double bowing, if he could give any idea of that
cackle. He couldn’t, but ever since has been
rending the air of Melbourne’s principal hotel in
an effort to strike the right note.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.


MAD WITH NEURALGIA.
“Neuralgia is not a disease,” runs one of the favorite sayings of the great Dr. Williams, “It is only a
symptom. It is the surest sign that your blood is weak, watery and impure. Bad blood is its one cause—
good, rich, red blood is its only cure.” There, in a nutshell, you have the real reason why Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People cure Neuralgia—because they actually make new blood. Read this proof :
“ Two years ago my nervous system went all to pieces,” said Mrs. Margaret Purkis, Thorne and Forsyth streets, Newtown,
Wagga, N.S.W., “ owing partly to worry. I had been in pretty poor health for some time, and my blood had got thin and
watery. Sharp, stabbing, neuralgic pains ran like darts of red-hot fire through my face and head. Then the nerves all over
my body became sore and bruised. The pains in my head became so bad that they were just like red-hot table knives being
stabbed in and Out. Sometimes an attack like this lasted for weeks ; one attack went on for two months. I was almost mad
with agony. I only slept from sheer exhaustion. I was so weak that I could not lift my head from the pillow. Often the
pains started all of a sudden, and my nerves seemed to lift clean out of my body. More than once I screamed aloud with the
• agony. Even a light footstep on the floor would set my nerves alive until pain. If I tried to eat, the first bite would make the
nerves in my head throb with agony. For months I lived on soft and sloppy foods. Then one of my little girls brought me a
pamphlet which told me how Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People cured Neuralgia by enriching the blood, from which the
nerves derived their nutriment. I sent to Mr. Williams, the Fitzmaurice-street chemist, for three boxes •of these blood-
building pills. Even one box enriched my blood and steadied my nerves. Two boxes took all the pain away. Three boxes
made me eat and sleep well, and I have been in good health ever since. lam positive that Dr. Williams’Pink Pills have
built up my blood and nerves and thoroughly driven every trace of Neuralgia from my body. lam sure no one ever suffered
more from Neuralgia than I did.” Mrs. Purkis went before Mr. L. A. Fosbery, J.P. (son of the late Inspector-General of Police in
N.S.W.), and made a declaration that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills had permanently cured her of excruciating Neuralgia.
Write for Free Medical Advice. Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney. 2s. 9d. a box.—Refuse all substitutes.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Richard Bourke, aged 93 (a son of the
early N.S.W. Governor of that name), recently
died near Limerick, Ireland.
Some provincial papers still publish the
portrait of the much-deceased Dr. Harman Tar-
rant, and announce the address at which he can
be “ consulted daily.”
Another old Yic. goldfields battler passed
out this month —W. F. Salmon. He was one of
the party that worked the Creek claim, which
afterwards became the Band and Albion, for years
the best-known at Ballarat.
Clement Wragge, ex-rain prophet of
Queensland, has been giving Maoriland audiences
a scientific proof of the religious theory of the im-
mortality of the soul. But bringing religion to
M.L. is like carting coals to Newcastle.
April appears to have been a deadly month
for veterans in Sydney metropolitan district.
Eliminating the infant mortality, one-fifth of the
deaths were of persons aged 70 and upwards, the
top-scorer reaching 96. It is recorded that 42
persons between 70 and 79 handed in their resig-
nations to the Almighty that month.
Dodery, who succeeds Adye Douglas as
President of the House of Dodery (Tas.), is a
youngster who has only been in the State s Parlia-
ment for a little over 40 years, and can’t be
expected to know as much about rules and things
as Douglas, who had been 49 years in the business.
But he will probably drop down to it in time.
During the late contest for the two Laun-
ceston seats in the Tas. Upper House, Adye
Douglas went to a civil servant and asked him for
his vote. “Ask me for my vote?” said the
indignant C.S. “You have passed an Act retir-
ing me from my billet at 60, and you want me to
send you back to the Council till you’re 95 ! No,
sir.”
Cabled that
Pictures by Australasian artists, Roberts, Streeton,
Quinn, Pox, Lambert, David Davis, Bunny, Burgess, Full-
wood, Parker, Ashton, and Lever have been well hung in
the English Academy.
Thus Australia comes out with quite a respectable
showing. Another message tells that Lever has
three pictures in Paris Salon, and that one of
Rupert Bunny’s pictures, also in the Salon, has
been purchased by the French Government for
the famous Luxembourg Gallery.
There are good chances of the Westralian
Labor party holding at least one sawbones after
the elections ; Dr. Ellis is the selected candidate
for Coolgardie. If he gets there he will be the
only doctor now in an Australian State Labor
party, though the House of Reps, has two—
Maloney, of Victoria, and Culpin, of Queensland.
The first N.S.W. party, in 1891, had one, the late
Dr. Hollis ; but though he was on the best of
terms with it till his too-early death, he did not
long continue his membership.

The police-court appearance of Ebby Ward,
the nearly extinct political volcano of S. Australia,
and his vigorous assertion that the policemen had
made a mistake about his condition, recalls his
defence of ’B7. There had been a noise about just
the same sort of thing then, and the matter was
dragged into Parliament. “I deny,” said Ebe-
nezer, waving his long arms and legs, “I deny
that I was ever drunk in my life— except medi-
cinally..”
Gov.-Gen. Northcote’s private Goliath,
Bulky Johnson, apparently takes an adult
interest in politics. His countenance gleamed
continuously from the gallery, what time Fisher’s
amendment was falling leisurely on Deakin.
Also, it shed a light on the building the afternoon
that Watson made his first show as Premier. He
is an exceedingly brilliant-visaged man, and wears
an out-size in countenances, so that his presence is
always obvious.
Sydney University Commemoration let
loose on the already overstocked professions some
130 graduates. One doctor of Laws degree was
conferred on G. W. Waddell, nephew of the State
Treasurer and son of Colonel Waddell. A Science
doctor announced was Mr. W. G. Woolnough.
He achieved some distinction by assisting Pro-
fessor David to settle the burning question whether
the coral insect built upwards or downwards.
They and others spent a long time in the South
Seas deciding this knotty point, by tracking the
fearsome insect to his lair at the bases of the coral
reefs.
William Hampson, holder of No. 1 lease
on the Coolgardie goldfield, died early this year m
Central America, where he was managing a mine,
Hampson was scratching about at Southern Cross,
when he heard something from further east.
Bayley and Ford got the Reward claim ; Hamp-
son came in with the first lease. He did fairly
well, tripped to London for a while, and then
went out looking for more trouble in a country
where you can get it pretty easily.
The pre-election decision of Bent to honor
the old agreement with the Yic. police re retire-
ment at 60 makes a scatter among the superin-
tendents and inspectors. The inspecting super-
intendent (Hamilton), the superintendent at Mel-
bourne (Crampton), and five inspectors are up to
the retiring age; and another superintendent,
Maude, will reach the limit in August. Mahony,
now of Bendigo, will then be next in seniority to
the Chief Commissioner. Six senior sergeants
will go up to the vacant inspectorships.
An Adelaide Dowieite has had a horrible
surprise. He had sold all his property and made
a song about it, and was just about to quit for
Zion, South Australia being too wicked and selfish
a country for so good a man to stay in any longer,
when the Destitute Asylum officers sent for him.
They told him that they were surprised that a
man of his wealth and lofty principles should have
left his poor old mother in the workhouse for so
long, and wanted to know what he was going to do
about it. He performed, but he also had to pay.
Albert Hinchcliffe, since the early ’9o’s
the wet-and-dry nurse of Queensland Labor move-
ment, is one of Morgan’s new nominees to Queens
land House of tallow and overdrafts. “ Little
Albert ” —who is a Kiplingesque sort of person in
appearance—is, among other things, secretary of
the Queensland Labor Federation, ditto Queens-
land Central Political Labor Executive, manager
and gallery scribe for Brisbane W orker, secretary
of a Workers’ Political Organisation, and sundry
other shocking kinds of agitator. The collective
sniff of his new colleagues should be worth
depicting.
Judge Dash wood, before whom the small
Jenkins row over the Northern Territory Chinese
should have come, does a dozen or so odd jobs at
Australia’s back door. He is both the chief ad-
ministrative and judicial officer in the N.T. S.
Australia used to separate the two billets, and in
the days when the lately-dead J. L. Parsons was
Government Resident, T. K. Pater, afterwards
Adelaide’s pungent Police Magistrate, was Magis-
trate, with the same power as a Supreme Court
Judge. (Even S. Australia proper hasn’t any
other sort of Judge but the Supreme Court
variety.) Dash wood does all the work, and then
he isn’t rushed. He used to be an Adelaide poli-
tical lawyer, reckoned fairly democratic, and was
a trusted friend of Kingston’s. Personally he
isn’t any lover of Ah Sin, and has written some
scornful things about his devious ways, especially
in connection with N.T. mining.
The Owe’Sullivan’s Deform opponent at
Belmore will be ex-“ Brigadier” Bruntnell, of the
Salvarmy. The “ Brigadier ” threw off his Booth
jacket a few months ago, and is now the paid
agent of the N.S.W. Temperance Alliance. A
party which goes into the field bearing the bene-
diction of George Reid, and cordially suppor e.i
by Jack Want, looks a promising sort of field for
an Alliance lecturer.

The memory of the first Queensland Chief
Justice is kept green by a familiar pill notice.
Lutwyche, who was resident Judge at Moreton
Bay prior to separation, should have had the
appointment as C.J., but Governor Bowen wanted
something “genteel.” Writing to Roundell
Palmer, then Solicitor-General of England, Bowen
said : “ What we want is a gentleman rather than
a mere lawyer. The principal officials in the
colony form a sort of social aristocracy.” And
Queensland got James Cockle in consequence.
Lutwyche was a horsey man and not aristocratic.
“Melb.”: In the Rev. Elias Blaubaum
Melbourne’s Jewry mourns a gifted, versatile and
industrious member, the leader of its thought, in
his Jewish Herald. Associated with him some-
what in business, I feel as if the little man, with
his cigar, must still be about. He permeated
Hebrew society in all its aspects. Blaubaum had
the pluck to come from Germany to Melbourne
and take charge of St. Kilda synagogue, without
knowing a word of English when he started. He
illustrated those embodied paradoxes which give
the Jew so much power. They lie in a mixture of
perfect conservatism with perfect assimilation.
“ Demos” : Prime Minister Watson’s face
charms me more than any yet thrust forward in
Australian politics, except Higinbotham’s, and as
much as that. There is the boy in Watson, and
there was in Higinbotham. I apply to them
Emerson’s sentence on the beautiful male
countenances he saw in England—“lncorrupt
youth in the face of manhood.” In John Christian
Watson I read both humility and power. There
a peculiar wistful gaze, such as Longfellow
would desire to have imprinted on the lad who
carries the Excelsior banner. He is the statesman
who looks to the next generation, not the politician
with an eye to the next election.
Big landlord Onslow, who has feverishly
wooed various N.S.W. electorates of late, rules on
his Camden Park estates with all the dignity and
show of a pasha. When living on the estate he
and his family drive about with the sun shining
like a limelight on them and their liveried foot-
men. In Sydney the Colonel strides along with
lanky legs like any ordinary human being, but in
the country it is necessary to overawe the tenants
and other caterpillars. So a gorgeous chariot and
uniformed menials meet the warrior at Menangle
station and furiously gallop him to his palace,
distant about twice his own length. The burden
of his ancestral greatness hangs heavily on Onslow
—who is also celebrated for paying his employees
255. a week.
Died at Waverley, Sydney, (29/5/’O4),
Nicholas Head, journalist, formerly of Orange
(N.S. W.), where he owned and edited the Okangb
Liberal. For a country newspaper proprietor,
Mr. Head was very independent ; he was a man
of means, and could afford the unusual luxury of
telling his readers what he thought of them.
Writer remembers an alderman—-Pat Kenna no
less—with whom Nick had a tiff, stalking off to
the office in Lord’s Place “to stop his paper.”
“Mr. Head,” said Pat in his happiest brogue,
“ I want to sthop me paper. You’re unpatriotic;
you attack the Council that is thrying to do its
best and put the lamps in the best places, and for
your want of pathrioticism I’ll sthop my paper.”
The editor told the irate alderman that he might
take his dirty money somewhere else. Pat and
Nick were devout R.C.’s, the former always
“ humping the plate,” but Nick avoided Pat’s plate
after Pat withdrew his subscription.
Ernest Terah Hooley, who, in the days of
his boodle, presented a Hooley communion service
in solid gold to St. Paul’s Cathedral, and who has
lately been living on the money he settled on his
wife while his sun was still above the horizon, has
fallen in. Along with an individual named
Lawson he is charged with inducing a publican to
invest £7950 in a bogus Siberian gold mine for
which he professed to have got a concession from
the Czar. The meteors of the London Stock
Exchange are, on the whole, a poor and transitory
lot. They practically all burst, and end up in
gaol, or the workhouse, or the gutter, or obscurity
There have been many of them since the time of
Baron Grant, who died worth about 2d and a stale
scone, but hardly one of them had a tithe of the
staying power of a Gould or Vanderbilt.
Died last week, Robert Reid, the Melb.
softgoods importer whom Kingston happened to
bowl out. Possibly the bowling-out hastened
Reid’s decease, in which case he was a solemn
lesson, teaching that honesty is the best policy.
Anyway he was a solemn lesson of one sort or
other. Reid began life as a drapery boy, and
ended it as a drapery boss, an inferior sort of Sar-
good, with a reputation for having prospered by
means of commercial hanky-panky rather than
Elate trading. During the Boom time in
urne he was roughly criticised at some of the
meetings where he loomed large on the platform.
After the Boom, when other men in, his line of
business were speculating as little as possible, Reid
figured as a bold, enterprising person, making some
investments that looked bad, but apparently doing
well out of them. At that time, too, he entered
the Vic. House of Fat Landlords, and made an
object of himself as Minister of Defence. His
ignorance of political things in general was as
patent as his self-conceit. Making a speech at
one of the “ Foundation Day” luncheons at Mel-
bourne Exhibition Building, Reid referred to the
occasion (Jan. 26th) as the anniversary of Captain
Cook’s landing in Australia. And it was this
inflated draper whom the Bent crowd nominated
for the first vacant seat in the Federal Senate,
where he sat unabashed whilst his Customs
swindles were sheeted home to him.
ROBERT SIEVIER, the Distinguished English
Blackleg, formerly known to Australia as
Bob Sutton, the Cash Bookmaker.
(An old Melbourne photo.)
The yarn of a Melbourne man who makes
a fad of that sort of thing :
Judged fairly, there are more gentlemen in the Watson
Ministry than there were in the Deakin. Some of them
don’t know the small rules; have an idea that the best
way to “ dress up ” to a ceremonial occasion is to wear a
buttonhole ; don’t see the incongruity of wearing brown
boots with a claw-hammer coat; but, taken by-and-large,
they are a crowd that I would sooner introduce to an
English country house than the last Cabinet, which had
some absolute bounders who’d be tolerated only in official
circles. Snobbish, you may say, to notice such things.
Perhaps so, but I’m a radical in politics if a bit of a snob
in other things, and seeing those quiet self-possessed men
labelled “ Labor Ministers ” goes for pleasure.

OUT OF IT.
As a popular spectacle no new Governor has a
look in against a fairly good dog-fight.—Recent
Bulletin par.
In the drought days a man out Dubbo
(N'.S.W.) way lost all but six of his 3000 sheep.
After that he became a picturesque lunatic, and
his sole occupation consisted in riding round look-
mg for blades of grass. When he found one he
cut it carefully in six pieces—one for each sheep.
“ Thurla ” : Be “ Curlew’s ” suggestion (B.
17/3/04);as to the whyness of the aboriginals’ small
camp fires. They have better reasons than either
dread of bush fires or that tired feeling. Once
asked the late King Cutabush, of the Castlereagh
blacks, why he. made so small a fire when there
were tons of dead wood handy. “ Cut ” grinned
and said, “ More better have little fellow. Spos'n
wild black see big fire come spear me, in the bright
light, no good that. Haven little fellar and hear
noise in hush, put out so ” —suiting the action to
the word by instantly heaping dust with both
hands on to the blaze which was reduced to a
smouldering heap. Which recalls an incident that
happened on the Murray during the ’9O floods.
A miscellaneous crowd had been driven in to
Angledool to await the fall of the water. Amongst
them was a Scotch piper with bagpipes in good
order. _ About a mile up the river a big camp of
aboriginals was planted between the river and a
lignum swamp. The whole camp was drunk and
m a great uproar. We got the Scotchman drunk
also, and led him silently through the darknes s
to the river bank opposite the camp. Then he
advanced suddenly but unsteadily on the enemy,
playing “ The Campbells are Coming,” which was
untrue, as our names were respectively Smith,
Ferguson and Jenkins. Instantaneously every
fire was smothered, and a rush of niggers, old and
young, could be heard splashing through that
lignum swamp. For long afterwards that camp-
mg ground was regarded as “ debbil-debbil.”
* * * *
“Darb”: The following incident will
serve to show that though money was scarce and
crops, grass and water unknown quantities in a
certain Mallee district during the drought, social
feeling was by no means dead. A settler who
could no longer hold out against the many trials
imposed upon him, decided to leave. When the
fact became known, his numerous friends and
well-wishers determined not to let him depart
without marking in some way their respect and
esteem, &c. A canvass of the district was made,
and the net result was 10s., which was about all
the coin in the place, and the canvasser’s horse
died from over-exertion. It was agreed that the
departing friend and his wife should be made the
recipients of a gold albert and brooch respec-
tively, and, as a temporary respite from the
drought, beer to the value of 7s. was procured to
irrigate the presentation. That left 3s. to pay for
th i gold albert and brooch, which were bought
from an Indian hawker. It was a joyful
party. The 7s. worth of beer flowed freely, and
on every hand you could hear snatches of conver-
sation referring to the fact that Bill or Alf. had
lost another horse and 10 sheep, &c. After the
presentation some members of the gathering
is^ low fight, and I left. It took very little
of the unaccustomed beer to make the Mallee
drunk m those days. I believe there was a scrap
towards the end, but whether or not the eighteen-
penny gold albert was trodden upon in the confu-
sion I am unable to say.
* * * *
“F.C.P.”: Have read Sorenson with
great interest on “ Bushman Junior.” His obser-
vations are generally correct. He might
havesaid something of the “homeliness”
of the youngsters (one of their chief
their mateship at an early age,
and their self-reliance, probably unique.
Another striking feature is their know-
ledge of subjects usually only known with
maturer age. This is unfortunate, and
due to parents talking of all sorts of mat-
ters in front of the children. The bush
children have a strange sense of humor.
I have read to Australian outback child-
ren stories from Ethel Turner, from the
“Children’s Page” of various weeklies,
also foolishly tried them with Kipling’s
“Just-Bo” stories. All these raised no
laugh—plainly bored them. But to illus-
trate pluck and determination I told a
class of 10 or 11 years the well-known tale
of Paul Kruger cutting off his own thumb.
This, to my astonishment, was hailed
with shrieks of laughter. And when
casually describing the ancient method
of execution, i.e., the axe, I was inter-
rupted with continual ripples of laughter !
Another point, a sad one—their sore eyes.
Surely Australian children (of the interior
at all events) must be the weakest-eyed
young among all the nations, yet they
are almost the healthiest, otherwise !
••* * *
A Perth (W.A.) reporter, attached to a
daily which is always boosting up the wonderful
agricultural resources of that great country,
recently spent a few days holiday on the South-
western Coast. He found that, although some of
the cookies had been settled on their so-called
farms for 30 and 40 years, they were too lazy to
till the soil even for the growth of a few vege-
tables. Occasionally the pressman struck a
selector with a few half-starved cows, but when he
asked for a drink of milk he was served with the
condensed article out of a tin. The tired feeling
existing everywhere was simply appalling, and he
does not at all wonder why strenuous efforts are
being made to get farmers from the Eastern States.
Here is one of the many stories he tells of his
experiences:—
He was a farmer, and related to a successful Westralian
Personage. Unlike the Personage, however, he had
stayed at home and lived on his farm, where he tilled a
little of the soil and kept a few cows. Among the
customers of the farmer was the landlord of an hotel near
hy. The greater portion of the product of the few cows
was bought hy the hotelkeeper. In fact, he looked to
the farmer to keep him supplied with milk daily. The
supply was very irregular, however, for the farmer would
lose his cows when he wanted to milk them, or they or he
would he unwell, or something or other. Anyhow, the
hotelkeeper became tired of the shiftless, irresponsible
irregularity of the milk supply, and the frequency with
which he had to fall back on the canned article. An
opportunity arising, he bought a cow of his own, and, a
while after, a second milker. The farmer accepted the
situation without comment, and finding out that the
hotelkeeper could supply him with milk regularly every
day, and also with fresh butter—for the hotelkeeper in-
vested in a separator—he made a contract with him.
Now the farmer has turned out his cows, receives fresh
milk and fresh butter regularly from the hotelkeeper
and feels greatly relieved at not having the bother of
milking* cows.
* * * *
Mr. T. Whitley, of Blackheath (N.S.W.),
in a paper read before the Australian Historical
Society recently, knocks the stuffing out of the
claim of Wentworth, Blaxland and Lawson to
have been the first to cross the N.S.W. Blue
Mountains. Mr. Whitley showed on good evidence
that a convict named Wilson had crossed in 1798.
He lived with the blacks for a number of years'
and it was through his intercourse with them that
he learned the route.

* * * *
The A. W.U. is still the “ringer” among
N,S. Wales’ labor unions, though its tally isn’t
what it was by a long way. Drought and reduced
nocks have been mostly responsible for cutting
down the membership from 20,891 at the end of
1901 to 11,538 at present. The bogus Shearers’
Union is now alleged to have a membership of
2717 including, presumably, those who shear the
wild merino in Pitt-street and the others who
pursue the jumbuck in Hyde Park. The New-
castle (Northern) Colleries’ Association, with
6502, ranks next after the A. W.U. ; then the Rail-
way and Tramway Service Association with 3794.
u* 8 i amon f? the bigger unions, is the one
which has made most progress ; it had only 1755
members two years ago, so it has more than
doubled itself. Perhaps the Tramway Employees’
Union could show as good figures, only it hasn’t
sent them in to the Registrar. The Journeymen
Tailors have also made a long jump—from 334
last year to 1084 this year; and the Shop
Assistants are going the same way fast.
* * * *
Let us all settle on the sand and win our
way to bloated affluence by poultry-farming. That
is the advice of the Queensland Agricultural
Journal for March
If 2000 farmers and other settlers kept 100 fowls each,
and each hen were to lay only 100 eggs a year, the annual
output of eggs would equal that of Maoriland—say about
20,000,000 or 1,666,666 dozen eggs. At 6d. per dozen, a
sum of over £BOO,OOO would be poured into the State.
Evidently Joe Rudd, who made the famous com-
putation of the value of Dad’s wheat, has joined
the staff of the Q.A. J. as mathematician-in-chief.
* * * *
“Petramos”: Re bull-ant-breath discus-
sion. This may be applied to fleas. By careful
microscopic observations I have found that the
reason why the ever-busy flea cannot be found
when most wanted is that he holds his breath.
Likewise, I have found that if we sleep with-
out breathing we will never be troubled by
his bite. My knowledge only extends to
city fleas, but if anyone is willing to pay my
fare I am prepared to further experiment in
the country.
* * * *
“ Huffin-Gruffin Re sweating of children.
Writer has just had a glimpse of life on a Victorian
farm. The chief impression left is that of the
children rushing about their “duties,” haunted
continually by everybody shrieking “’Urry.”
“Now Violet, ’urry up with that fire and let’s get
breakfast over!” “Bill, ’urry with the pigs’
feed!” “Jack, ’urry yourself and separate the
cream!” “Eor goodness sake’urry, Jim. You’ll
never get to school.” And, after being ’urried for
two or three hours, they are ’urried off to school
two or three miles away at about 9 a.m. As they
rush away a final admonition is yelled after them
to ’urry ’ome after school, to see the sheep are all
right in the paddock as they come, and bring the
cows home with them, and not forget to ask the
Browns to send their Tom over to-morrow to help
with the thresher, and sundry other messages.
After school hours, and the five or six mile walk
involved, it is “’urry” again till after dark
(although summer-time) and then “ ’urry off to
bed ” and “be sure and ’urry up and dress as soon
as called in the morning.” Verily it is small
wonder that the farmers’ kids grow up wooden.
The wonder is that they grow up at all.
“Louis M” : “ Cockney
the Outcast” (8., 14/4/’O4) finds
fault with “War E.,” and I,
in my turn, wish to pick holes
in “Cockney.” Whether men
—without what they may be
justified in term'nj fair reasons
—ever refuse work X cannot
say, but of this I am positive •
they will throw it up on the
least provocation—in fact, with-
out any reasonable provocation.
I’m strictly in truth when I
write that I’ve known men to
quit a job because they had
not enough to do. (“Dead
slow ” is the term for this
trouble.) Further, to say that
shearers, as a rule, lack in rhos-
pitility is utterly incorrect.
“Travellers” have always been,
and still are, a big factor in
building up the tucker bill.
“Cockney’s” sneer at the
“cocky” shearer is, in a re-
mote way, suggestive of an
urchin throwing mud at a de-
cent piece of statuary. I don’t
mean that the ‘ ‘ cocky ” is per-
fect—we’re none of us that—
butat least he shears while he
shears, and doesn’t loaf on all
and sundry between times, and
all the while he’s trying to re-
move himself from the labor market—a virtue in-
deed. In the bush, from what I can see, any man
who reserves his earnings for legitimate outlay
is a “cocky,” or bracketted with “cookies,” and
the only “ white men ” are those who, whenever
funds permit, persistently provide Mrs. Public-
house with a new silk dress.
* * * *
In M.L., where every charwoman has a
bicycle and every drover is known by a linen
collar, the swagman is dying a natural death.
Ihose of him who still survive are the old-age
prodigals whose pensions have been stopped by a
flinty-hearted beak. Now and then some genius
does visit the back-country, with a new plan which
be works upon the run-holder. For instance, the
other day a sundowner successfully travelled from
Palliser Bay along the stations to Napier, with a
dog as his only companion. His custom was to
haul the dog by the collar up to the run-holder,
and state he caught the animal sheep-worrying.
He would then propose to exterminate the dog by
a quick process, take the confederate away and tie
him to a manuka, and come back and overload
himself with tucker and tobacco from the grateful
sheep-owner.

“Caber” : When is a man drunk? The
drunkest man I remember seeing was an old
shepherd who had come into Drayton to “blew”
i c heque, way bade in the sixties. After a
skinful he wandered out on the green to sleep it
off. When we school-kids saw him his faithful
dog was standing over him, wagging its tail. The
tail was white-tipped, and the poor old jumbuck-
sentmel, with a short black cutty in his mouth,
was dodging his head to the wag of the collie’s
tail, trying to get a light from what he thought
was a waving firestick. He was drunk, I think.
SPORTING ITEM.
JACK: “I swum this crick when it was in flood.”
BILL: “ Thet's nothin’. Larst week I swum three
miles, with the pup on me hack, to ther doctor,
and it was up-hill all the way.”
At Gaffney’s Creek (Vic.) the usual humor-
ist has been dropping a harmless white powder
into his beer, saying “Good-bye, Bill” in a hollow
voice like an open grave, and drinking off the
mixture. And he struck the usual consequences,
for the Creek seized him in its alarm, sat on him,
gave him 2oz. mustard, one cup of brine out of
the butcher’s corn beef keg, some castor oil, a
packet of salts, some senna, and a horse-ball; put
10 hot bricks on his stomach; and otherwise
raised Cain. And all the time his howls to the
effect that he had been only playing a joke on the
Creek were taken fdr delirium ; and his curses
were regarded as a sign of approaching dissolu-
tion, wherefore the Creek put more hot bricks on
him. And when he kicked out furiously, the
Creek said he had convulsions and gave him
another horse-hall.
TO A SWAGMAN IN THE CITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O hairy-faced, old Swagman,
I doff the cap to you ;
I, too, have been a bagman
And had to battle through
The ordeal long and dreary
Of destiny adverse,
Long, lean and worn and weary
And humping Duncan’s curse.
I, too, have scaled the ridges
For many a lonesome league,
And camped beneath the bridges
Near dead with dull fatigue ;
Have wakened, grimly groaning
O’er joints atwist with pain,
To hear the night-wind moaning
And feel the drifting rain.
I, too, have fed on messes
Not found in cookery books,
And stuffed myself with cresses
Regardless of the flukes ;
Have lain to slowly frizzle
Without a shrub in sight ;
Have slept throughout a drizzle
And waked with hoar-frost white.
b too, my wrinkled sinner,
Have tramped from shed to shed,
And seen myself grow thinner
And wished that I were dead ;
Have dossed in houses haunted,
In spite of sage advice—
But ghosts are easy daunted
And shrink to squealing- mice.
I, too, have borne the flouting,
The townman’s ugly sneer,
The furious cookie’s shouting—
His mouth from ear to ear ;
Have camped beneath a waggon
Tired-out at set of sun,
Till stalked by some she-dragon
To pot me with a gun.
1, too, with curious yearning,
Have pondered puzzles o’er :
Why, spite of law and learning,
’Tis sinful to be poor ?
Why, robed in saintly vestment,
The reverend shepherds thrive,
While on such poor agistment
Their flocks just keep alive ?
N.S.W.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLDON.

“A Zelwood” writes (Bulletin, not
having seen the map alluded to, takes no respon-
sibility) :
The large wall map of Australia, published by Smith
and Sons, Charing Gross, London, in 1877, and daily used
in many Victorian State schools, must have been drawn
by someone in the horrors, and accepted by an Inspector-
General who shared the draughtsman’s potations. Maffi a
is placed in S.E. of N.S. Wales; but in compensation
Kosciusko’s bald head rises from the Victorian uplands.
A town named Hoading occupies the site of Sale, and
Bendigo is severely omitted, while twopenny-ha’penny
places, like Buninvong and Violet Town, receive mention.
Anticipating our legislators, thedraughtsman(in 1877) gave
Melbourne direct railway communication with Ballarat.
Other errors might be mentioned ad nauseam. It was
probably during the same spree that a little “ geography ”
by G. Sutherland was placed in every State-school library.
Here is an extract“ The tea-tree grows generally on
poor soil, but is of considerable value to the colonists, as
the bark of the tree can be utilised in tanning.” Armed
with this information, I remonstrated with selector Simp-
son for burning off such a valuable asset. He thereupon
offered me 70 acres of the asset for two beers.

“Rita S.” :
So "0.0.” (B.
14/4/’O4) has
nothing but
scorn for the
soft-loaf-and-
tin-of-sardines
cattlemen of
to - day, has
he? Wonder
if it is as
scorching as
the scorn auc-
tioneers and
stock - dealers
would have
for their drover-
men if they dal-
lied at the way-
side pub and hung
over the stock - rails
tickling Betsy Jane
under the gills, as in the
bad old dawdling days.
To-day, time is the essence
of the contract, and the
romance is knocked out of the game with the “move
on ” travelling stock clauses, not to mention the
drought. The successful drover must be a hustler
and as sober as his dogs, otherwise he is left idle.
But the old order giveth way to the new. The
drover has fallen from grace with “C.C.’s”
ilk because he wears collars and ties, and uses
language terse, yet less lurid, than in the days of
old. He can also talk politics, exercise his vote,
and use soap and water —three small items un-
known to the old hard-drinking cow-Ishmael.
Even the sleeper-cutters on the Murray are
Eerishing in the eyes of the old-timers because they
ave a live association to protect their interests,
with the ignoble object in view of seeing the
members’ cheques are “knocked up” instead of
“knocked down.” These little changes are “ruin-
ing ” the country out-back, and I don’t regret it.
The old clieque-scuttler of days agone has had to
close his shanty and seek an old age pension.
Billjim has taken unto himself a selection, like-
wise a wife, and is engaged raising the census and
other crops; and instead of wrestling with blue-
dragons and carpet-snakes a league long, he
dodges the mortgagee, and chases round looking
for a shower of ram. The lost romance of the
past is not much to grieve over.

“ Elmore ” : A horse cannot buck with his
tail outstretched. When he is bucking all he
knows, his tail is tightly tucked in. A bull or
bullock cannot buck unless his tail is well
elevated. Don’t know the why or wherefore, but
nevertheless it is a fact. Will Bulletin artists
please note? [Obviously, therefore, the remedy
for the bucking disease in a horse is a sizeable
balloon tied to his tail, unless the tail can be made
to rise by means of yeast. —Ed. B.]
“ Chasselas ” : Came across
a curious case of conformity to
environment among the rosella
parrots which frequent the blue
gum plantations reared by land-
owners in S. W. Vie. plains. There
are no hollow trees in which they
can build nests, and I happened
on a parrot’s domicile, with two
young birds, in an abandoned
rabbit burrow. Mother parrot
had made her nest and reared her young ones
quite comfortably under the new conditions.
* * * *
Stereotyped letter from a man on the
sand :
The owner of a very big N.S.W. station well east of the
Darling, and not in the worst drought area, drove away
from the scene of his labors the other day. He went to
the station about 20 years ago with £BO,OOO cash ; he left
it a few weeks ago with a buggy, two poor horses, a
water-bag, and a few old one-pound notes. The station
has been blown clean away, and of the tens of thousands
of sheep that once ran over it, nothing is left but a few
white bones and old horns sticking up here and there
through the sandheaps on the plain.
A large tank at Colar Station (N.S.W.), where they used
to wash the wool, is full to the surface with sand. The
woolshed at the same station is almost covered over with
sand, and the management thinks it will pay better to
build another shed than to shift the stuff.
At Tarrawong Station (Albert Tyson’s) in the Lachlan
country (N. - W.), where they used to shear 160,000 sheep,
or thereabouts, last year 27,000 were shorn. The wool
waggons rolled over great banks of sand 12ft. high, where
gates used to be once upon a time.
Between Oxley and Banranald several of the telegraph
poles (after a duststorm) have been left without a particle
of e&rth around them, and they have to be replaced with
longer poles. The storm takes the earth in a face at
places and sweeps every grain of sand and loam before it.
At other places the sand lodges and heaps up and
becomes great sandhills.

The “ debased habits of the starling ” were
the subject of complaint before the council of the
Victorian Agricultural Society the other day, when
H. M. Gooch stated that:—
The numbers of starlings were increasing, and the birds
were migrating north. He had had eight acres of grapes
destroyed by them. In England the starlings be-
haved themselves, but directly they came to Australia
they gave way to misconduct, like a great many other
birds and animals. (Laughter.) In England the sparrow
was a gentleman, but out here he was a miserable nuisance.
So with the fox in Australia ; he grew three times the size
and took to killing lambs, which he did not at home.
Government Entomologist French in a letter con-
curred with regard to starlings
The starling as a fruit destroyer takes the bun and no
mistake. I have looked fairly and squarely at all sides of
the question, and in my opinion if the starling does not go
the fruitgrower must. Which is it to be ?
It was sought to urge the Agricultural Department
to secure legislation to deal with the pest, but the
motion was lost.
Ernest Favenc writes
Why have not the names of the following rivers (a few
out of many") retained their rightful spelling and pro-
nunciation ? First Of all the Murrumbidgee was originally
spelt Morumbidgee—the nearest approach to the phonetic
spelling of the word as pronounced by the natives.
The Australian newspaper of 1830, hazards the spelling
of the Murray as Murree, but this is obviously a mistake
as we know whom Sturt named it after.
Next comes the Barwan. This is what Major Mitchell
christened it, and how it should be spelled. There is no
authority for spelling and pronouncing it “ Barwon,” as
people do now. Walter Bagot, one of the early settlers on
the river, gives another rendering, namely Barwin. But
as it is in reality the Upper Darling there seems no
necessity for any of these names.
,, T1 ? e Narran was first called the Narreen and the Balonne
the Bolloon. I could give some more instances, hut the
above will suffice as samples of our uncertain nomencla-
ture.
TARANTULAS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Poor Bill had been upon the booze
For the last month or so,
And meeting' Ike, his bosom pal,
Thus told his tale of woe :
“Gorblime, Ike, you should er seen
The time I had last night,
Stretched out upon me bloomin’ bed
Fair paralysed with fright.
“ Over the bed, me face as well,
An’ on the blanky wall,
Tarantulas as big as plates
Was on the blanky crawl.
“ When I was dressed (this is Lorstruth)
I daren’t leave me room
Until I’d called the missus in
To brush me with her broom.”
“ Stiffen me, Bill,” so Isaac said,
“ Come-in-an’-’av-a-wet;
The missus slummed her sweepin’ job—
They’re crawlin’ on you yet! ”
SLIDE VALVE.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in; ami in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

borrowing, and announced the amount of its proposed
first loan, but Watson stopped the game. (2) The Bul-
letin has never supported the N.S.W. State Labor party
in its financial policy, or want of financial policy....

Depends wholly on the assumption that U.S. birth-rate is

and Toledo, 0 per 1000 ; Scranton and Detroit, 9 ; and so

The N.S.W. Coal Lumpers’ Basket Act
will have to be followed up with a similar measure
dealing with wheat sacks. Twenty-four bags
taken from one truck railed to Sydney lately from
Lyndhurst for export averaged 3921b5. ; and the
lumpers had to struggle under this shocking
weight of nearly 4cwt. Down to a few years ago
the average wheat sack held a little over 4 bushels
(usually about 2601 b.); but the size has been grow-
ing until it has reached something that makes the
handling of it inhuman. And all to save a few
pence. It’s a shocking commentary on a country’s
civilisation that little things like this have to be
legislated upon ; but there seems no other way of
meeting the ghouls who sweat pence out of men’s
lives.
AFTER THE BATTLE.
GINGER JIMMIE: “ Yes, these ’ere ’ Orspital Satur-
days makes a man bloomin’ well dead broke.”
“ Caber ” has an idea :—
The retail storekeepers are on the warpath against the
Chinese tradesmen. The Chow is under-selling them, and
this they attribute to evasion of the Arbitration Act
and other -statutes. Well, the High Court has decided
that the Arbitration Court has no power to determine
how many hours a man may devote to his own business ;
so, as the man who works 100 hours a week has a big pull
on the man who only works 50 hours, if we want to com-
pete with Goom Chuck or Li Hung, we must work at
least 90 hours a week to keep up our end of the log.
There is one way to stop it. Impose a business tax upon
all vendors of merchandise, making the license fee £1 per
annum. The State has then the right to grant or refuse
a license ; all aliens are refused, and that little trouble is
over. Will the Liberal and Reform party make this
reform one of the planks of its fighting platform ?

Prophets, False and True.
There has never been an age wanting for
prophets, but the true as compared with the false
have always been disappointingly few. False pro-
phets have been known to incidentally confer
benefits in unexpected quarters, as in the case of
an impostor in England last century, who pre-
dicted the end of the world on a certain definite
date with so much insistence, that some people
sold their property at panic prices. Needless to
say, the world did not come to an end on the day
foretold, and the purchasers secured some very fine
bargains. When it is asserted that Mother Seigel’s
Syrup will cure indigestion and the diseases that
arise from it, that is no prophecy. It is a statement
founded on the fact that it has done so in many
thousands of cases. “For years I suffered from
liver and kidney disorders,” writes Mr. Charles
Brooks, of 17 Little Church-street, West New-
castle, N.S.W. “The best that any medicine
could do for me was slight temporary relief, until
I tried Mother Seigel’s Syrup. After taking three
bottles of that remedy, I was myself again.”
Mother Seigel’s Syrup is always a bargain.*

B. MOUNTGASTLE & SONS BRISBANE,
POLITICAL
POINTS
It is pathetic to note the efforts of Sydney
Telegraph, in its eagerness for a coalition, to
convince the dissentient Freetraders that really
Freetrade does not matter a bit.
It is significant that the N.S.W. hangman
and the public flogger are both ardent supporters
of the Carruthers party. A politician is often
known by the hangmen who vote for him.
Retiring Defence Minister Chapman left
for his successor a strong minute recommending
justice for Major Lenehan, and he also urged
favorable consideration of Major Carroll’s griev-
ance.
Early in May the N.S.W. Freetrade organ-
isation, at two different meetings, condemned
the proposals for a Reid-Deakin alliance, but the
daily newspapers, in their hunger for a coalition,
suppressed the news.
Just now, when the first Australian Labor
Government is at work devising a sane and
workable programme of Democratic legislation,
it would be a blessed thing if some one
would take Tom Mann out to the Mac Donnell
Ranges and lose him there.
This is what book-fiend Jenkins’ own
State (at a Port Pirie meeting) said about his little
abortive revolt against the Australian Govern-
ment :
This meeting expresses great gratification at the fact
that the attempt of Mr. Jenkins and Mr. Von Doussa to
foist nine fraudulent Chinamen on the Commonwealth of
Australia has proved a failure, and congratulates the
Federal Government on its firm stand.
G. H. Reid at Mosman (N.S.W.) the other
day:—
I find that in politics, as in every other human
experience, the older we get the more knowledge we
get, and the more likely we are to discover that there are
two sides to every question.
Let’s see : the Yes side, the No side, the Yes-No
side, and the side the bread is buttered on. Four
sides, George !
