TUMMY BENT GRIPS THE SITUATION.
“The taxpayers will agree that the situation is safe in
Mr. Bent’s comprehensive grasp.”— ARGUS.
About the most amusing contribution to
the great mass of coalition literature is that of
oily Joe Carruthers, who “heartily approves” of
Reid’s attitude, and says the time has come when
Reid and Deakin, in the interests of the country,
should “sink personal aggrandisement”—and
grab at office. It is so like oily Joe to describe the
proposed all-round selling of principles for the
sake of office as a sinking of “ personal aggran-
disement.”
Finding of the Victorian Commission as to
the allegation that Montague, of the Red Indian
Assurance Society, tried to bribe Peacock and
other members of Parliament, is that Montague was
corrupt and his Society equally to blame, but that
Peacock was a stainless lamb. It’s one of those
delightfully legal verdicts by which the law is able
to discover that though Mrs. A. committed
adultery with Mr. 8., Mr. B. didn’t commit
adultery with Mrs. A.
_ The Tory press is disgracefully unprin-
cipled and unashamed. Here is Hobart Mercury’s
invitation to Premier Watson to turn traitor to
honor and party :
He has pledged himself to a policy which is almost sure
to be his ruin, and he can only avoid this policy by doing
as many others have done before him, calmly ignoring the
rungs of the ladder by which he has climbed up.
That may be how Tory politicians work, but it is
not Watson’s style.
Defence Minister Dawson has given a final
“no” to the request of the rifle-shooting pot-
hunters for a subsidy to take them to Bisley. He
minuted in refusing :
Every penny that can possibly he spared by economical
administration will be spent on equipment and the pur-
chase of warlike material, and that, as a matter of extreme
urgency, has the first claim.
In short, the money is to be spent on getting rifles
here instead of in sending riflemen away.
Mr. Power, a member of the N.S.W.
State Labor party, took a prominent part in a
recent conference on the unemployed question.
His contribution to its deliberations was an assur-
ance that, if only the conference could show the
Government how it could borrow money, the
Government would be only too glad to borrow it
for the purpose of starting reproductive (or un-
productive) works. It is that sort of member and
that sort of opinion which keeps the N.S.W.
Labor party off the Ministerial benches.
The unspeakable Bent evidently intends
to abolish altogether the Victorian old-age pen-
sions. Argus report of a recent speech of his :
It might be necessary to have a better system of dealing
with old-age pensions. A suggestion has been made that
the money would be better spent by the ladies’ benevolent
societies, and he leaned somewhat to that idea.
From a national gift to the superannuated tax-
payer, down to a dole of pauper charity dribbled
out by local catmittees—the history of the Vic-
torian old-age pension illustrates the political
history of the State.
Regarding the secret and cavernous nego-
tiations between Reid and Deakin for a mutual
desertion of their principles, Sydney Telegraph
complained last Monday :
In political circles the cry of the secret coalition is being
raised to damage the movement, just as the cry of the
secret conference was used. over the Premier’s amend-
ments to the Federal Constitution Bill.
It was the Telegraph which raised the “ secret
conference ” cry above-mentioned ; so this sneer at
its old self seems to suggest that it is getting
rather ashamed of its great failure in the days of
Anti-Bill.
In Irvine’s constituency (Vic.) a poor
widow, the mother of a large family, 65 years of
age, earning a little by nursing when health will
allow, applied for the old age pension, or rather
pittance. She was told that unless she was pre-
pared to hand over her scanty home, a poor old
cottage on a small allotment, to the Government
it would not grant the application. And there
was no offer of any guarantee that the blessed
Government wouldn’t stop the pension again after
the first year, and keep the cottage, and give the
old dame 12 months for vagrancy.
Meldrum, lately defeated for Taverner’s
vacated seat at Swan Hill (Vic.) but now having
another flutter in the Bent interest, explains his
views thus: “He was a strong opponent of Social-
ism in all its forms. . . . He was a strong advocate
of a complete permanent water supply scheme in
the mallee. . . . He advocated land resumption
for closer settlement purposes, but would guard
the interest of the farmers who took the land and
the squatters who at present held it.” Bent
doesn’t know how to spell socialism, and his
followers don’t know what it means.
The word “reform” needs to be defined
anew. It is not the old respectable word of the
dictionary. It has fallen into evil company, and
is now travelling under false pretences. Every
hashhouse that serves up strips of leather when
asked for rump steak calls itself “ The Reform
Food Emporium.” Tailors who use material that
has the same tendency to slink up one’s legs on the
approach of rain as the produce of any other
tailor call themselves “reform” tailors. Even
the “ reform ” dog-stealer is coming. And Carru-
thers and Bent are “ reform ” parties in politics.
Notwithstanding a distinct promise made
by Iceberg Irvine in the House that Victorian public
and railway servants, in electing their separate re-
presentatives, would have exactly the same rights
and privileges as those enjoyed by ordinary electors,
Roaring Bent has issued regulations preventing
them from “criticising the Government, or any
member thereof, or any act of the Government.”
This is a fitting sequel to the Strike Coercion Bill—
a Bill that provided a penalty of £lOO or 12 months’
imprisonment for anyone, man, woman, or child,
who spoke in severe terms of the blackleg’s small
boy.
“Demos”: With a record only one year
short of half a century, Tasmania’s most notorious
Tory leaves the political stage. Adye Douglas
dates from the irresponsible Leg. Council of 1855,
and has suffered defeat at the hands of the most
democratic candidate ever nominated for the
House of Lost Hopes. Peter M'Crackan is an
advocate of the abolition of the superfluous Upper
House, and he polled over two votes for every one
given to the ancient upholder of privilege. Laun-
ceston has farewelled Conservatism; her other
seat in the Dead House was given to the most
progressive of four candidates.
One of the most remarkable features of
the election campaign in Victoria is the candida-
ture of M. K. McKenzie. McKenzie is the person
who had to resign from the Irvine Ministry and
from Parliament, in consequence of a report of a
committee of the House which declared that he
had used his position as Minister for Lands to
grant himself and his family valuable land con-
cessions. In his first speech to the electors,
McKenzie tried to explain away his improper
conduct by saying that he had simply mistaken
the law of trusteeship. “As a Minister of the
Crown I was a public trustee,” whined McKenzie.
“ But this position I did not recognise at the
time.” Is this a fair sample of Tory intelligence,
or of Tory principles ?
Rodney shire council (Vic.) has received a
report on the sanitary state of Kyabram, which
sets out that . “ present conditions are both dan-
gerous and disgusting.” It was from dirty and
disgusting Kyabram that the “Reformers ” started
out to politically create Irvine and Bent, and the
smell of their politics is just like that of their
town.
DEMEANOR FOR WORKERS.
“Since ‘Comrade ’ Fleming a few days ago retired
from the leadership of the unemployed that body has
been re-organised on less bombastic terms, with Mr. J.
Murray as president, and Mr. J. Exell as secretary. The
committee yesterday waited on the Rev. A. R. Edgar, at
the City Mission offices, and stated its difficulties in a
modest and earnest manner. Mr. Edgar gave them
a sympathetic hearing, and promised to interview the
Premier on behalf of the unemployed, and to report the
result to them on Friday next.” —Melbourne Paper.
FATMAN (noting modest, earnest manner):
“ Ah! —now we can talk!”
Trashworth, whom Yic. Premier Bent
shunted off to hustle for a seat in. Hampden, got
badly broken up at his first meeting. Following
the example of other State muddlers he was raving
wildly about the “incapacity” and “inefficiency”
of the Federal Parliament, when a listening clod-
hopper asked him to state exactly what crime the
F. P. had committed. Poor Trashy hadn’t
reckoned on being asked for particulars (he hadn’t
a particular about him), and hastily muttering
that he couldn’t discuss Federal politics he went
on to deal with “our vast natural resources,” or
something equally safe.
Sammy Lancaster, M.L.A. for Rodney
(Yic.), is to be tackled for his seat by Captain
M'Kenzie, auctioneer, and popular handy-man
generally, of Echuca. W. T. Webb, M.L.A.,
whose old seat of Mandurang is wiped out, will
probably retire, as Lancaster is sure to be the
Ministerial nominee, and the “go” should be an
interesting one—with a shade of odds on the
sitting member. Lancaster visits portions of the
electorates in a clandestine way, conducting ser-
vices at the outlying Bethels on Sundays, and
ends up the services with expressive handshakes
all round. Between Bent’s yell and curse and
Psalmyell’s own snuffle his prospects are pretty
good.
“ T.S.” : Launceston (Tas.) Examiner
recently published a leading article headed “ Cul-
tivating a National Spirit.” I started to read it
with enthusiasm. Thought it would be something
about promoting education, teaching the country
to give up relying on the foreign money-lender and
to get itself out of pawn, persuading it to depend
on itself for the production of its own manufac-
tured goods, and instructing Young Australia that
his whole duty doesn’t lie in tearing wool off a
sheep, borrowing, and looking to Britain for his
defence. But it wasn’t anything of the kind. It
was just a long, sickening howl about hoisting the
British flag in schools and teaching the infants to
salute.
N.S.W. iron industry seems at last to be
on the verge of birth. In response to Sandford’s
offer to put up mills if the State guaranteed him
50,000 tons (roughly £300,000 worth) of custom in
five years, the Government has resolved to call
for tenders for a contract of that magnitude,
specifying local manufacture. What Sandford
has in his mind is the provision of the Australian
Customs Act that “with the establishment of an
iron industry ” duties on ironwork ranging from
10 to 15 per cent, come into existence. With the
N.S.W. State contract he can establish his works,
and then with the benefit of the duties he hopes
to hold the local market against foreign compe-
tition.
Probably no more essentially ignorant
man than Reid ever won to a great office in
politics. (Bent might be an exception, but Bent
knew something about manure.) Speaking last
week, Reid said that Australia was the only
country in the world which had a Labor party,
and, to make his ignorance clearer, continued that
Britain, Germany and France had not such
parties. In Britain there is a small Labor party ;
m Germany the Labor-socialist party of Bebel is
nearly the strongest organised force in the
Reichstag; in France the present government of
M. Combes is made up chiefly of the Labor-
socialistic party. Combes himself is an avowed
Socialist.
Sydney Telegraph by May 11 had per-
suaded itself that Deakin was willing for a Reid
coalition on Reid’s terms, and set forth the terms
which Reid must insist on :
To remove the ill effects of that (the Democratic party’s)
domination in the past will, therefore, be as necessary as
to prevent its continuance in the future. The foolish and
impracticable “white ocean” policy which was forced
upon the late Government by the caucus will certainly
seriously affect the trade of the Commonwealth. That,
for example, will have to go. A still greater evil threatens
from the law under which the case of the six hatters
arose, and which is now being strained to prevent white
immigration to the Westralian goldfields.
And so on. The Telegraph’s instructions to
Deakin and Reid how to cut their own throats
and give Watson a reliable majority are very
complete.
If the Watson Ministry goes under, Aus-
tralia will lose the first Federal Cabinet of which
it could be said that every member of it paid his
debts, and no member of it ate with his knife.
Both the Barton and Deakin Governments con-
tained at least one notorious knife-swallower.
In their anxiety to be the first to congratu-
late Watson on his Prime Ministership the govern-
ing labor bodies of Perth and
Fremantle (W.A.), have come
to loggerheads. Perth Trades
and Labor Council, which has
never been popular at Fre-
mantle, wired away congratula-
tions, and added that it desired
the common rule clause should
apply to all mailboats from
their first port of call in Aus-
tralian waters. Fremantle has
always been in deadly earnest
the other way, and the Fre-
mantle Trades Hall Council has
solemnly decided that mail
steamers should be exempt
from the common rule clause
until such time as the Trans-
Australian railway has been
constructed, and it has also
formally protested against the
action of the Perth crowd in
telegraphingto Melbourne with-
out having conferred with the Fremantle crowd.
The Argus, in an article which professed to
congratulate Peacock that he was not found guilty
of corruption, pulled that bird’s tail most cruelly:
Alexander Peacock made two statements to Parliament
on this matter which professed to be full and frank. It is
not too much to say that those who heard those state-
ments, and accepted them as an open avowal of all that
his memory retained of the circumstances, were shocked
when they read his sworn testimony. Mr. Justice Hood
states the favorable impression which, as a witness to the
truth, Alexander Peacock made upon him. Exactly the
same impression of absolute candor and sincerity was con-
veyed to Parliament. Hence the amazement with which
it was discovered that one of the most salient and damag-
ing facts of the whole case—that Alexander Peacock had
himself accepted and retained the £5O which Mr, Mitchell
refused—was withheld on both occasions. . . The charge
that arouses the strongest resentment in Parliament is
that a member has wilfully misled the House. How can
Alexander Peacock clear himself of that offence?
A Launceston (Tas.) man about the down-
fall of Adye Douglas, after being continuously in
Parliament since 1855:
It was the making of poor men and women pay
income tax that killed Douglas politically, in the 89th
year of his age and the 49th of his political career. At a
big meeting held in Launceston the night before the
election some instances of the villainous taxing of small
incomes were given. One man said he had paid “two
quid ” income tax, while his boss paid nothing. It was
mentioned that an old woman, with 30s. a week coming in,
had got a “judgment summons” for 7s. income tax.
Douglas was afraid to meet the indignant people of Laun-
ceston. He took the Mechanics’ Institute for a night, but
when he found what the feeling towards him was, he can-
celled the engagement, and held no meeting. At the last
minute he apostatised from even his own party, and de-
clared he would vote for the repeal of the “ personal exer-
tion ” clauses of the Income Tax. So his own old sup-
porters were riled at his perversion, his old political foes
would not trust him, and he fell in.

Ballistite is'rapidly displacing the other smoke-
less pdwjiers for sporting purposes. Its admirers claim
that it is stronger than any other, cleaner in the gun,
perfectly smokeless, and entirely unaffected by heat or
dampness. It can be soaked in water and fired without
any loss of efficiency. Since the professional pigeon shots
have largely adopted it,, and the weekly scores in the
sporting papers show the majority of kills to its credit,
the shot-gun fraternity, so numerous in England, have
taken to it en masse. Ballistite is made in three forms :
in cubes for cannon, in minute rings for rifles, and in
square flakes for shot-guns.*.

Tommy Bent will hang on to the tail of
any animal that he thinks has the slightest chance
of helping him towards office after next election.
Speaking at Moorabin, the loud-voiced slobberer
denounced former Governments for not having
kept their promises to the soldiers who fought for
the Hempire on Afric’s burning kopj e. ‘ ‘ Nothing
would be wanting if he remained in office to see
that justice was done to returned soldiers. He
would see that they would get the employment
promised them.” Well, Bent has been Premier
for some time now, and has been 1.1 office for two
years, and did nothing for these battle-scarred
heroes. This sudden remembrance of the poor
wounded soldier of the Hempire just on the eve of
a general election smells fishy. Also, are all the
billets for the returned heroes who believe in
Bent and Deformity ?

The annual meeting of “ the great Reform
League” in Melbourne was a mildly squashed
affair. The league claims to have 197 branches,
and yet there were not 60 places represented at
the meeting. This number includes all the Mel-
bourne and suburban branches. _ The few members
in attendance swelled around in immense style.
One of them, during the proceedings, moved
“ That we hereby repeal the Immigration Re-
striction Act.” This proposal to immediately
start abolishing Federal laws wasn’t carried out.
The treasurer of the concern reported that, during
the year, £25 10s. lid. (keep an eye on the lid.)
was subscribed by the branches, but that £633
had been given in private subscriptions (no doubt
from Flinders-lane and the Melbourne Stock Ex-
change). With pride he announced that the
credit balance was now £41195. lOd. (keep an eye
on the 10d.)

“ Burglar Bill ” : The religion of three
Chinese prisoners in Goulburn Gaol was entered
on the description cards hung outside their
respective cells as—“Pagen,’’ “ Hethen,” and
“ Pagin.” The Chows always smiled profusely as
they glanced at the cards on leaving and entering
their cells.

‘ ‘ Spring ” sizes up the situation :
It appears as if Young-husband is the glorified bagman
for John Bull and Son’s unrivalled Tenpenny Tea. The
Thibetan’s regular beverage is made from China brick tea,
which is the solidified dust and odds-and-ends of the
Chinese market. A lump of fat, or cook’s dripping* or a
bit of cindered bull is added to the brew. A pannikin of
this mixture is guaranteed to create a green buzz under
the most hardened bush tea-drinker’s belt. The Chinkey
Government knows that John Bull wants to force this
brick horror out of the Thibetan market and to introduce
a taste for Indian post-and-rails and choice Ceylon. Hence
the Llama’s stubbornness (due to Chinese pressure), and
hence Young-husband’s threatened interview. Britannia’s
method of tickling the heathen has changed. Formerly
she went forth with a Bible in one hand and a rum-bottle
in the other. Now she advances with a feasible explana-
tion in one hand and a tea-pot in the other.
From Grecian ages up to our own day,
Have bronchial cures appeared and ceased to be;
But this, the world admits, has come to stay,
And to be known as The Great Remedy.
The trite old saying’s right, the best will last,
And last it will, of this you may be sure,
For coughs and colds are ailments of the past,
By timely use of Woods’ Gkeat Peppermint
Cuke.*
Suburban local bodies are humorous in
their unreported moments. One of them, the
Karori (M.L.) Borough Council, got into holts
with itself the other evening. One of the coun-
cillors was erecting new premises as a future arena
of municipal wrangle. A fellow-councillor objected
to him placing a supporting pillar in a position
that would pierce the centre of the council table—
said that the job was most amateurish. “If that’s
all you’ve got to say,” said the builder-councillor,
“then I say to hell with such people as you.”
The councillors then adjourned to the back of the
hall.
Father Robinson, the engineer of the
famous Amy Castles boom and entrepreneur of the
present Melbourne bazaar, is one of the keenest
advertisers and most astute business men who ever
graced a theatre or a pulpit. Robinson was
originally an Anglican, and made a great mistake
when he became a convert to Rome instead of
founding a wild cat religion of his own. With his
shrewdness and his beauty-actor countenance he
might have been another Mahomet. As it is, the
name of Father Robinson causes J. C. Williamson
and Bland Holt to look apprehensive. In addi-
tion to his other qualifications, Robinson has for
seven years held the clerical cycling championship
of Victoria, having beaten with ease every one of
his holy brothers who ventured to dispute his
supremacy.

WHAT’S IN
A NAME?
There is a good deal in a name when it
comes to the question of saving your life ! If
you have the “ wrong name ” you stand a good
chance of losing your life, but if it is the
“right name,” you have more than a chance,
you have a certainty of saving your life. And
the right name which saves your life in cases
of throat and lung trouble, as well as in cases
of blood diseases and marasmus (wasting
away) is “Scott’s;” “Scott’s” when coupled
with “Emulsion”; “Scott’s Emulsion.”
When a remedy has achieved such a world-
wide success as Scott’s Emulsion, it is bound
to be imitated. Imita-
tions are never as good as
the original, and those
who make the imitations
seldom have the knowledge
of “making” that belongs
to those who make the
original. That is why
there is a good deal in a
name. If you get Scott’s
you get the cure that
goes with Scott’s ! If you accept an imitation
or another emulsion, you get only the result
that belongs to those things, you never get
from them the cures that belong to Scott’s!
For your benefit and protection therefore, we
print here our trade mark. When this trade
mark is upon the package of emulsion that you
buy, you get that guarantee which brings the
cure. And if you are seeking a cure you have
only to satisfy yourself that this trade mark is
there. The right emulsion with the right
name— Scott’s —can be had from any chemist.
If you want perfect Health yourself, or desire
it for your children, write to Messrs. Scott
and Bowne, Ltd., 483 Kent-street, Sydney,
N.S.W., send 4d. (for postage), name this
paper, and you will receive a free sample
bottle.

A Missed Chance.
It is not possible to doubt that the N.S.W. Labor
party, if ft had taken good counsel 12 or even six
months ago, would hold the commanding position
for the coming general elections. ihe See-
Owe’Sullivan Government is thoroughly dis-
credited and with, seemingly, a mournful con-
sciousness of the fact, drifts on to its doom. Mr.
See is away nursing an illness and holding to an
office the duties of which he does not perform,
with a desperation which hints that he knows it
must soon be snatched from him. In his absence the
party, of which he is the head but not the leader, can
do nothing. Under the circumstances, Wise, who
as Acting-Premier has shown a good deal of pro-
mise, might do something, but his hands are tied ;
anything that he might enter upon is liable to be
upset by the real Premier. In Crick the party
has another man of parts —of brilliant parts—Uut
he is quite helpless to declare a policy. The
Ministerial party thus drifts helplessly to wreck.
It embraces practically all the talent of the Parlia-
ment, and, with a firm purpose shown to change
its evil life, the electors might be induced to
remember not its past sins, and trust to it for the
future. But it doesn’t even ask the electors
for confidence ; it just slides. The second party,
that led by Carruthers, is composed chiefly of men
bankrupt of character, devoid of intelligence, and
enslaved to noisome sectarianism. Its professions
of “ Reform ” enthusiasm are not taken seriously
by anybody, because it can’t be induced to
definitely promise to reform anything ; it merely
uses large, balloon-like words about the re-estab-
lishment of true constitutional government, or the
restoration of sound principles, or the rehabilita-
tion of flapdoodle, or something like that. Its
accession to power would be viewed with dis-
may by everybody with an atom of regard for the
reputation of the State. A weak and flabby imi-
tation of the party led by Bent in Victoria, it has
not half of the brute strength of that gang; and, all
other qualities lacking, even Bent’s small measure
of brute strength is something. The most ardent
enemies of the See Government distrust the
Carruthers Opposition, and recognise it as a
party of evasions, platitudes and back numbers.
Their true view of their “great Liberal and
Reform” leader is shown in the fact that they
have tried all sorts of expedients to supplant him ;
poorold Solemn Lee was one of them, JohnStinson
another. With a Government discredited for its
past, discomfited in its campaign for the future,
and with an Opposition equally discredited, almost
equally inept in its appeals to the people and
much inferior in talent, the chance should be for a
third party with no discreditable past and which
could offer something for the future. But there is
no appearance of such a party. The Labor party
might have met the need. Had it, 12 months ago,
put forward some workable plan for ending the
policy of drift and muddle, of debt and extrava-
gance, and given, in the meanwhile, some earnest
of its sincerity, there is not the shadow of a doubt
but that in July a Labor Government would be in
power in New South Wales. But the chance
was missed. In spite of strong urging, the Labor
party refused to come forward as an entirely
different party, a Whole Hog reform party, and it
wanders helplessly to the electors, its fortunes
bound up to a great extent with those of the See-
Owe’Sullivan section. True, it decided to go a
little way along the path of Reform last January
with a set of abstract resolutions which it has
since, seemingly, forgotten, but it baulked at a
Whole Hog policy, and appeared to strive to be
as much as possible a dull grey affair, with a
desire to be nothing striking and to do nothing
out of the way. If it were a bold party, with a
flaunting crimson faith in honesty and self-reli-
ance as an Australian political creed, it would
have now the ball at its feet. Even as things are,
as a party which professes to be only moderately
virtuous and reasonably honest, the N.S.W. State
Labor party will probably do fairly well at the
polls. The discredit of the two other parties, and
the fact that a Federal Labor Government has
come into existence without a single capitalist’s
throat being cut or a single bank closing its doors,
will fight on its behalf. It may come back as
strong as before, or a little stronger than before.
But what is that when it might have won a
majority of the seats and the reins of government ?
Fortune offers to the Labor members a bald noddle
since they refused to grasp her by the forelock.
On the recommendation of its Public
Works Committee, Melbourne Municipal Council
has adopted a proposal for a sick and accident
fund and a retiring allowance scheme for its em-
ployees—both to be built up by contributions from
the Council itself and from the employees. To
provide for any claims which may arise in the near
future before the fund is fairly on its legs the
council has insured 600 employees against accident
for one year.
The claim of Tommy Bent and his crew to
be the friends of the working classes is shown up
as a cheeky lie by the inroads which they are
making on the eight-hour principle in the railway
service. On every platform that Bent has infested
he asserted that he has done more for the “work-
ing classes than all the labor members put
together.” And yet, wherever he can, he is com-
pelling men in the Government service to work
9, 10, and sometimes 12 hours a day. Many of the
engine-men are now doing nine hours a day ; the
men in the Government’s Newport workshops
work over-time at the same rate of pay as for
ordinary time. Also instructions have been issued
to about 20 signal-men that in future they must
put in 10 hours in their boxes. Some of these
boxes are on busy lines, and when a worn-out
signalman pulls the wrong lever !!!
The Mission to Thibet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Colonel Younghusband, the political head of the British
mission to Thibet, has cabled that the Thibetans are now
displaying a most friendly feeling toward the British, and
are freely ordering goods from India.
In old Thibet the praying-wheels were wont to
churn in peace—
The psalm-machines were well-supplied with
water-power and grease.
The Llama used to go to roost secure from fear and
dread,
And Lassa folks, without a qualm, would seek
their nightly bed.
The hairy yak would stalk the hills, and browse
upon the ice,
And no one sent his invoice there nor “ letter of
advice.”
In calm the years of peace revolved, secluded from
the wet,
Till lo ! at last the Deluge came—the Mission To
Thibet!
• * • •
Through Scinde and past the beetling Ghauts a
fearless Bagman went,
When rolling boulders threatened him he muttered
“ Cent per cent! ”
That charm staved off the avalanche, and paralysed
the rock;
And so, at last, at Lassa’s gate the Bagman made
his knock.
The j unior Llama scratched his head, and mumbled
to his Joss,
But harsh the steadfast Bagman yelled, “ Hi,
there—trot out your Boss ! ”
And then the senior Llama came, a prayer-wheel
in his hand,
And quick the Bagman turned on him his stream
of language bland!
He flourished socks and handkerchiefs upon the
Pamir’s crest,
He strove to sell the Dalai-bloke a fancy-patterned
vest.
He waved the haberdashery all British bagmen
wave,
While on the whole performance strange the
Priest gazed grim and grave.
He twirled his midget prayer-machine, and
solemnly said he—
“ Oh, Bagman Khan, we do not need your haber-
dashery,
We’re satisfied” —but here he stopped, and won-
dered where to run —
The Bagman from his sample-box had taken out a
gum.
“ It works like this,” the Bagman said, and then a
distant yak
Fell down with twenty bits of lead embedded in
its back.
The Llama dropped his scripture-wheel, and
mumbled in the dust—
“ If you declare that we must trade, why, then,
we simply must !
Send twenty thousand handkerchief five million
boxer hats;
Send up four tons of anchovies, and six em-
broidered mats;
Send up a case of razor-strops, or their equiva-
lent.”
Then did the bagman chant again his watchword
“Cent per cent !”
And so it comes those monks neglect their distant
praying mills
Within the wilds of old Thibet, and scan their
monthly bills !
The psalm-machines, for lack of grease, have
ceased to churn remarks
Unto the misty Deities who dwell where Sirius
barks.
The bailiff soon in Lassa-town will leave his beery
trail,
And on that ancient, dreamy land the commerce
curse will hail.
The senior Llama goes to roost in worried mood,
you bet,
For the Bagman took a rifle with his samples to
Thibet l

SPORTING NOTIONS.
R. S. Sievier, alias Bob Sutton, was the first
to start the cash-betting- system in Adelaide. He arrived
in the early eighties with lots of cheek, good looks, a
loud, voice, and louder clothes—checks that shrieked at
you in divers tones. Bookmakers didn’t yell in saddling-
paddocks those days. You got at them, and vice versd,
through Tattersall’s Club. Tattersall’s Club wasn’t
taking any of Bob Sutton. That didn’t matter a cent to
Bob. Armed with a huge bag, he betook himself to East
Adelaide racecourse the day of a big race-meeting, and
began to yell “ I want to lay 1 I want to lay ! ” The
cackling attracted a crowd. Desperately long odds he
laid for a start, and, with the luck of Nick’s own, soon
acquired wealth and notoriety. Later he took on a clerk
to jot down bettings. One day at Morphetville he lost
heavily, and the vultures came swooping for their pick-
ings. But the clerk with the bag (empty) was kept dis-
creetly out of the way. Sutton, with a “ Now then,
gentlemen—keep calm ; can’t part up till the man comes
back with the bag-. D—n him ! Don’t waste time. Any
bets for next? Six to one the favorite,” managed to fill
up the interval. Next race his wonderful luck was in
again. Sievier scooped the pool, and blandly invited his
creditors of the previous race to hand in their tickets, as
“ the clerk had now returned with the bag.”
* * * *
The bookmakers were for once able to point out,
over the Adelaide Oup, that they had laid 50 to 1 against
one or two outsiders the evening before the race, whereas
£27 was the most the totalisator could have paid. On
the other hand, Moses had some money in the bag over
three horses that were scratched on the morning of the race,
whereas the machine gives everyone a run for his money.
Where the tote really shows worst is in small fields with a
hot favorite running. Consecutive dividends on the
Goodwood Handicap day were £1 11s., £1 75., £1 25., and
£1 Is., the last a record for smallness, paid on F. J.A.’s St.
Leger. The tickets taken out for F. J.A. were 742 against
142 on the other four starters, or about sto 1. But when
£BB commission had been deducted from the total £BB4
(three-fourths of this goes to the club, and one-fourth, by
a recent Act, to the State), there was only £796 left to
pay out, or over 21s. 6d. on each winning ticket. Now
the machine never pays fractions of a shilling, reserving
them, by general consent, for a Disabled Jockeys’ Fund
and other charities. And the difference between eighteen,
pence and a shilling, as a return for a pound, is a
difference.
* * S *
Tattersall’s Welter Handicap at Randwick,
Saturday, introduced a promising three - year - old in
Tartan, half-brother, by Lochiel, to Australian Star and
Australian Colors. Backed from twenties down to fives
and fours, he made a bold bid, but was just beaten for
second place. Tartan belongs to Mr. F. Foy and was
bred'to English time, which places him at a disadvantage
of some months with others his age. Judged on Satur-
day’s showing, Tartan should more than pay for his oats
ere long, and is worth having an eye on.
* * * *
Randwick trainer-owner, Sam Fielder, has some-
thing out of the ordinary in Lamrock—a nag that can
win hurdle and flat races in good company. Lamrock,
after gathering a couple of events on the flat, won first
hurdle race at recent A.J.C. autumn meet. A day or
two later he dead-heated for second hurdles at same
meeting. Also finished third in big thing of Sydney Turf
Club meet at Randwick last Saturday week, and Saturday
last at Tattersall’s yearly fixture, won the big money
(Winter Stakes) of the programme well.
* ♦ * *
Melbourne Sportsman expired peacefully last
week, after writing its own death notice. Fifteen years
ago, and for a long time later, the Sportsman must have
been a pretty good property. It was a pink paper, pub-
lished at threepence in its palmy days. Ikey Mo’s advts.
were scattered freely among its columns, and a number
of feeble contributors repaid Ikey by buttering him
individually and collectively. Then the people of Victoria
lost a lot of their enthusiasm for the turf, and the
“ odds ” were published in vain during the “ off ” seasons,
and the demand for misleading “tips” became beauti-
fully less. A small penny rival, the Sporting Judge,
began to cut into the Sportsman’s waning connection,
and there came a sad Tuesday when the old threepenny
organ of Ikey was reduced to Id. Now it is no more.
The other penny rag has absorbed the Sportsman.
**- * *
Commenting on the appearance of Sport Royal
at the V.R.C. Autumn meeting, where the Westralian
nag ran unsuccessfully, a Bulletin par suggested that he
was a likely outsider for Sydney Cup. Sport Royal was
scratched for Sydney Cup, but he proved good enough to
win Adelaide Cup last week, in record time. Like most of
Malvolio’s stock, Sport Royal is built on stylish lines—not
very showy, but attractively racing-like. As a 3-year-old
in Victoria the gelding couldn’t win a race. During the
next two or three years he earned his corn in the West,
but since his return to his native Victoria, in February
last, trainer Hickenbotham has made a new horse of Sport
Royal. It is a lamentable fact that somebody who had
the luck to draw the good thing in a Tatt.’s sweep was
foolish enough to thrust a bit of his luck upon the stable.
This is how other stables are encouraged to demand a share
of the sweep chances as a “right.” A Juggins stands a
99,999 to 1 chance of a winning ticket in Tatt.’s, and when
he has pulled off a ticket that looks like a winning chance,
his first idea is to discount his luck by making terms with
the stable. As though the stable, having got a horse fit
to win a big stake, would be such a fool as not to have
a go without the extra inducement of a bribe.
* * * *
“W.F.E. ” in answer to “Deeby” (May 5) re
Polo: “Deeby” must have a very limited knowledge of
the game, and the only occasion that he saw it must have
been a “ knock up ” practice by some very raw amateurs.
To commence with, the players of polo are not generally
“snobs,” but “sports.” If “Deeby” loves the horse as
he says, does he object to racing ? Polo ponies are minia-
ture racehorses thoroughly trained to stand the exertion,
and, after all, a game is no longer to them than a race—
two 10-minute “spells” being all a pony does, at most
twice a week. And he thoroughly enjoys it. -I have a
pony myself who will follow the ball without a bridle,
turning and twisting as the ball is hit. Would he do that
if he was not fond of it ? A trained pony ridden by a
player is seldom hit on the leg, which is well'covered with
leather bandages in any case. In conclusion, it is the
best game known, embracing riding, racing, football,
golf, cricket. It requires more hard work from the man
than from the pony, as there is only one man and
generally three ponies to play the game, which has been
handed down thronghgenerations as the acme of “game
science.’ 1

Though yet only a syr.-old (a time when most
others are at their best) whilom high-class sprinter Ibex
shows very much in the light of a “ has been.” Reasons

week, when backers sent him out a hot first choice, were
many, and all admitted there must have been something
wrong, as that wasn’t near Ibex’s form, consequently with
only 9.7 up in Flying Handicap at Tatt.’s meet Saturday
last he looked fairly choice goods. The thing would have
been a “moral” for him a season 'or two ago. Ibex
stripped capitally to the eye, but, in the race, and opposed
tp-certainly not a first-class lot, he cut a sorry figure after
the home turn, and never had a look in with Camira, who
streaked away from him at a great clip. Ibex was com-
pletely out of it at the death.
* * * *
Fragments from last meeting of Tatura (Vic.)
R.C. It was resolved not to join the newly-formed
Murray River Association. One speaker advocated intro-
ducing the tote. “It might purify things, but associa-
tions were useless ; swindling was done under the eyes of
officials, and could not be prevented.” “It was worse
this year at Tatura than ever,” said another. “Every
race meeting he attended he left more and more dis-
gusted.” A third remarked that he “ hoped no officer of
the club would back horses, and anyone who did so and
sat on a protest ought to be expelled. A circumstance at
the last race meeting absolutely shocked him.” A fourth
told that “the conduct of the bookmakers was scan-
dalous. Members of the club who betted should be
immediately asked to resign.”
JOHN CARKEEK, THE WRESTLER.
Photo, by Rembrandt Studios, Adams’ Chambers,
485 George-street, Sydney.
Alleged and evidently pronounced non-stayer
(judged by showings in previous events), Camira, came
very much out of his shell in two races (Farewell and
Flying Handicaps, 6fur.) at successive S.T.O. and Tatt.’s
meetings. Heavily backed, he gathered both deci-
sively, and as if an increased journey would have made
little difference to him. Remarkable how suddenly some
horses come on at times, and how nicely stable money
anticipates the improvement.

Concerning recent cable that a son of the Earl
of Jersey had won considerable coin at Monte Carlo, Lon-
don Sportsman tells that Lord Yilliers had a remarkable
run of luck. He got away with £82,000 first, and a friend
did his best to persuade him to send it to England and
come away at once ; but young Jersey was obdurate. He
had won so much, why shouldn’t he win more ? The
friend, disgusted, left; and Yilliers spread himself out
again and scooped £BOOO more.

Race between Tresidder and Towns for world’s
sculling supremacy is now assured, and will be fixed up
this week-end. Delay occurred through Towns endeavor-
ing to get his £5OO stake beforehand, and a Sydney publi-
can came to the rescue with the comparatively small
balance that seemed harder to find than the bulk of the
coin. Spectacle of a world’s champion sculler going round
begging for backing is new to the sporting world ; usually
there’s someone willing to put the money up straightway.
The challenger seems to have had no trouble at all over
the matter, which indicates how most people size up
possibilities. Years ago, in sculling’s boom days, £5OO
would have been subscribed over and over again for a
world’s champion without the asking, and the subscribers
would have considered it a favor to be in the bet. What
a falling off 1 -
* * * *
Victorian football season was initiated amid
serious troubles, and eventually the Permit Committee
stepped in with much-needed measures framed with a
view to suppressing the veiled professionalism that has
become rampant. It set to work to checkmate pirate
clubs desirous of seducing the stars in the possession of
their rivals. The new rule provides that no player who
has played with a league club or clubs in other States
during previous three years shall be granted a permit to
play with any league team, unless he can produce a
clearance from his old club. The reference to other States
was necessitated by the growing practice of “ birds of
passage ” going to the West, playing for a season, and
then returning to Victoria to play for the club offering the
highest inducement. The proposal to check this game
etoked strong dissent from one section, whose secretaries
had used “strictly moral suasion” on sundry valuable
players, and who objected to a change of rules being made
at the commencement of the season. Other delegates
expressed regret that the rule could not be made retro-
spective. The most striking result so far has been the
refusal of a permit to Monogle, the newly-elected captain
of Melbourne South, he having done the usual wander to
the West. If the permit people have the pluck to keep
going there is a prospect of the game becoming almost
respectable.
* * * *
At the beginning of the season N.S.W. Rugby-
Union subscribed £25 to a Sydney hospital, and so
great has been the damage that already footballers
have had most of the worth of it in attendance,
patches, and medical aid. Considering the work the
sport gives the hospitals, the clubs should certainly agree
to a gate tax of Jd. on eygry person paying for admission,
deadheads to pay 3d. as a special tax. Saturday last,
somewhere about 20,000 people watched football in Syd-
ney, and a £d. tax per head would have yielded £4O odd.
* * * *
The chief excitement in Rugby football last
Saturday was the match between University and Newtown
at University Oval. Thirteen thousand people paid to
watch it and shouted directions to the players to “ kick
’is ’ead in.” Occasionally, as some popular player was
sent trundling heels over head for a dozen yards or so,
some large mouth would open and breathe forth language
which entitled the owner to three months’ cheap lodging
at least. The game was. needlessly rough, but the play
was superior to anything writer had seen this season.
Both teams made good passing rushes, and dribbling and
line kicking were distinctly good. Newtown was beaten
by 12 to nil solely through the poor quality of the back
play. The backs were painfully slow ; they failed to get
away with the ball, and took so long over their picking
up that the Uni. forwards were down on them in a heap
before they could get off pace. Consequence was that
when the game was three parts over the .Newtown for-
wards were exhausted, and the Uni., still going strong
scored rapidly. The Newtown forwards played by far the
better game, and had they been properly seconded by
their backs the scores would probably have been reversed.
The most equal match of the afternoon was that
between Glebe and Eastern Suburbs, when the Suburbs
went under with three points to Glebe’s five. From start
to finish it was anybody’s game, and raged all over the
field. Glebe’s backs show big improvement, but on the
Suburbs’ side the improvement seems to be limited to the
forwards, the backs proving very weak in defence.
Western Suburbs and North Sydney drew with eio-ht
points each after an exciting tussle. South Sydney
blotted out Balmain, kicking up 20 points, while the Bal-
mainers failed to score. Strangest feature of the day’s play
was the utter absence of any attempt to score by potting
goals. Some excellent line-kicking- was indulged in, and
showed that every team has men capable of potting goals
at long range. Had Newtown backs been smarter they
might have scored in that fashion over and over a»-ain
after their forwards had rushed the Uni. well back inside
the 25 line ; but it was never done, and the Uni.
escaped by forcing. When the Englishmen come N.S.W
will receive a rude awakening on that score.
* * * *
The unregenerate pro. ped. N.S.W. Athletic
League, newly-formed for the purpose of “lifting" pro-
fessional running, held its first meeting at Sydney Sports
Ground two nights last week, but the runner was at his
old games as busily as ever. Non-triers were thick and
strong. One heat was declared “no race,” and all five
starters disqualified for three months. Attendance on
second wasn’t half that of first night, and yet the finals
were to be decided, and a second big handicap run right
through. What looked like a probable boom has been
badly pricked already.
* * * *
The proposal of N.S.W. Amateur Athletic
Association to send Dick Cavill and Nigel Barker, to
represent Australia at the St. Louis Exhibition Games,
is one that cannot be too strongly condemned. Possibly
a rabid freetrader like Dugald Thomson, the newly elected
vice-president of the Association, may have some va°me
idea that in some mysterious manner it may benefit “ com-
merce” ; or he maybe even able to justify it on the strength
of some obscure maundering of Cobden’s when the prophet
was half seas over ; but as far as physical development in
this country is concerned, the export of Australian
athletes can serve no useful purpose. We don’t want to
encourage young men to go in for athletics that they may
eventually be sent globe-trotting at public expense, but
that wholesome exercise may enable them to grow up with
sane minds in healthy bodies and make good, independent
citizens. This export business threatens to produce a
huge crop of sporting parasites, travelling round the world
in search of prizes and medals while living on the charity
of other people. The athlete who wants to gather in
foreign prizes should go abroad at his own expense.
* * * *
Huge Dutchman Harry Placke, who, long ago,
swam long distances off M.L. coast, and came to Sydney
to swim from Circular Quay to Manly, and do other mad
things if the police allowed him, but they wouldn’t,
did a madder thing, without anv interference, when
he fought Queensland giant Dick Barker at Foley’s
Athletic Hall, Woollahra. He was an inmate of Sydney
Hospital for weeks, most of the time unconscious, as a result
of that punching. Trying his hand at the bruising biz
again in Philadelphia recently, he got another unmerci-
ful thumping from light heavyweight Kid M‘Coy, which
rendered him a gruesome spectacle, till police interference
prevented absolute slaughter. The thing was over inside
four minutes, and Placke went back to his well-beloved
Amsterdam. In Sydney, Placke, Oft. sin., was the “little
fellow ” when opposed to Barker, 6ft. llin.
»** ' *
How professed judges differ ! Recent middle-
weight champion pug of Australia, Arthur Cripps, left
these parts endorsed as a forefront fighter, but no great
shakes as a boxer. Landing in London, he was tried out
and found so much wanting that he couldn’t get a purse.
Next the Australian betook himself to ’Murka, where he
landed in March, and, a few days later, was tested with a
known good man, whom he settled inside two rounds,
and New York’s onlooking sports labelled him the best
pug article that had invaded Yankeedom in recent years.
Cripps beat Soldier Thompson twice in Sydney.
* * * *
This long-suffering country has lately been the
dupe of the brassy Yank with a smattering of pugilism
and an alleged record, who introduces himself suddenly
with a bow as “ Icicle Ike,” or “ Torpedo Teddy,” the re-
nowned American smasher, and issues a challenge through
his nose at the “best yew’ve got in these hyar parts.”
Then the expectant fool-public pays high prices to see the
bounding stranger knocked out in two rounds by a husky
local man of moderate account, and it yells “ fake 1” like-
wise “ robbery ! ” and glares and talks severely over its
beer. But it rolls up again to the next “ fight ” that is
merely a benefit for a broken-down has-been or
a never-was from the shifty land where he
who asks for a nutmeg receiveth a piece of wood.
Chief among these pug wild-cats have been “Kid”
Parker, wiped out by Snowy Sturgeon in a couple of
rounds ; Jack Grace, beaten by Geo. Johns in a round and
a half ; Lundy, squelched by Blaekmore in three rounds ;
and now Al. Neill knocked out by Soldier Jack Thompson
in a round and a hit—though his case was more
or less accidental. The bright exception of recent
times was Scanlan, who proved himself a good second-
rater. Each of these men was announced as a champion
in his class in America. So hath the screaming Eagle
been made to appear as a moulty and squawky hen,
whose tail is ignominiously dragged in the mud of a
strange country.
» * * *
Al. Neill, the Californian with the white, girlish
bust, soon recovered from the mighty concussion on the
nose which ended his fight with Soldier Thompson at
Sydney National Sporting Club. 4 On the following Mon-
day night he fought E. Williams, of Port Melbourne, in
the southern capital, and after 12 rounds of artistic
slugging the local man’s seconds fluttered his household
linen, Williams being incapable of rising from his chair.
The condition of the Californian appears to have been as
deplorable as ever, but his opponent lacked the hitting
power of Thompson, and the Yankee got a chance to dis-
play his beautiful evolutions. Neill is what is known as a
“ pretty fighter,” and his side steps and ducks are as
graceful as a minuet. His left is not “ dirty,” but beauti-
ful as the arm movement of the Abyssinian maid in Cole-
ridge’s opium dream, and gives the idea that Neill has
practised many, many hours before a full length mirror.
His guards make one think of some little Jappy geisha
waving the flies off with a pair of fans, nevertheless they
are effective—Thompson’s blow was a sheer accident—
against vicious hooks and swings ; Neill’s blows, though
they fall as easily as the soft spring rain, soak in just as
thoroughly and break the enemy up. The only vicious
looking blow in Neill’s cabinet of graces (’twould be sacri-
lege to speak of Neill’s “ bag of tricks ”) is a right-handed
swing on the jaw which carries cold oblivion to the man
who stops it. This writer believes that Neill, in spite of
his prettiness, will, provided he can be got into condition,
about which there seems some doubt, break up every
welter-weight in Australia, and wipe the floor with some
of the middlemen. To see him perform is better than
watching a skirt-dance any day.
* » * *
The Shaw-Green bantam scrap at Sydney Golden
Gate, Saturday night, was a lively illustration of the fact
that Napoleon Bonaparte was speaking of pugs when he
remarked that the best defence was to be the attacking
party. Shaw seemed to have the most skill, but Green,
rushing in, smote him hard and often, and kept Shaw so
busy defending himself that he hadn’t time to make a
counter attack. Only in one round did Shaw get a chance
to assume the offensive, and then Green wobbled worse
than George Keid ever did. Realising his danger, Green
rushed things at full speed for the rest of the fight till, in
the eighth round, he landed a heavy left on Shaw’s jaw,
and the latter gentleman’s towel flew skyward. This
faculty for fighting a better offensive than defensive battle
is not peculiar to pugs, but belongs also to tigers, wolves,
bull-dog ants, &c.

Baseball is taking root in Sydney. On Satur-
day last no less than four first and two second grade
matches were played in Sydney; and the players were
not all Yankees.
not a poor man’s game, and no one who has to live on his
weekly earnings, or has anyone dependent upon him, can
afford to play it. It is a game exclusively for well-to-do
B gentlemen to whom three months in a private
al means nothing worse than the loss of their daily
motor drive. Writer once played football in Seddon’s
land and took no thought for the morrow, although men
got hurt occasionally there ; but the way it is roughed in
Sydney someone must get hurt, and it isn’t good enough.

Final of rink bowling supremacy of N.S.W.,
between Chatswood and Ashfield fours, captained by
Messrs. Young and Moses respectively, was decided on
Waverley green, Saturday. Contrary to expectations,
Chatswood went to the front from the beginning, and
stayed there. When Chatswood had 23 Ashfield had only
9, but in the next six heads they notched 12 to Chats-
wood’s 3. In the end Chatswood won with 7to the good,
29 —22. In one head the ultimate winners credited
themselves wdth 6of a possible 8. Points scored by and
against the winning quartette right through the competi-
tion show a surplus of 77 ; they defeated Gladstone Park
four by 3, Redfern 21, Balmain 25, City 21, and Ashfield 7.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The Australian Home Journal (2/5/’O4) presumably
tried to say that the “ Marriage of Kitty” was “ pursuing
its course” in Adelaide, but the compositor fiend made it
read “ proving a curse,” and then the heavens fell. The
compositor has a rich fund of humor, and his mistakes are
mostly cheerful. In a recent Bulletin par about the late
Duke of Cambridge and his relations it was mentioned
that a lady had been a columbine in early life, and the
compositor made two manful efforts to set it up as
“concubine.”
A young actor who has made a hit by his rendering of
a minor part in “The Darling of the Gods” is quite a
novice on the stage. He was lately rescued from a life of
ribbon-selling in a big Melbourne chiffon shop.
Draycott, who plays the part of the Husband in the
“Marriage of Kitty,” is an enthusiastic botanist, and
spent most of his leisure hours in Adelaide investigating
strange vegetables at the Botanical Gardens. In this
respect he somewhat resembles Titheradge, “ whose
heart was with his daffodils way down in Ascot Vale.”
One-night revival of “ Lady of Lyons,” at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, recalls that it was in this play that Irving
achieved his record. He was up in Scotland, “The Lady”
was announced, and not a single soul turned up to hear
him.
Bad luck for Annette Kellermann to meet with an acci-
dent on her first appearance as “ leading lady ” at a
theatre. In the Coogee scene of Bland Holt’s drama she
turned backward somersaults into a tank dozens of times
without a mishap, but when she attempted the same feat
in her “ farewell ” entertainment, at Melbourne Bijou,
t’other Saturday, something went wrong with the diving
platform, and the bonny brown water-nymph was dropped
into the tank so helplessly that her head struck the
bottom. It was her first and last performance for the
night. When the stage-manager announced that Miss K.
had, unfortunately dropped out of the entertainment,
there was a chorus of youthful voices from the amphi-
theatre, “ Gi’ us our money back.” The interest taken in
champions by young “ sports ” of the barracking class is
not a very sympathetic interest. If the girl had hurt her-
self it was her trouble, but if she was too much hurt to
reappear it was their trouble, and they wanted their coin.
“ Melb.”: Theatre proprietors are hit between wind
and water by the Board of Health’s ukase. Why not
begin with the old churches ? Not a word can be wonned
into a Melbourne paper about their hideous unsafety.
The two examples that stick out most are St. Francis’
and Wesley Church, both in Lonsdale-street. It makes
you shiver to notice how long a large congregation takes
in coming out of those places. The porch at St.
Francis’ is a death-trap. Wesley church has rickety old
wooden staircases, which would flame up like tinder.
The Melbourne Athenaeum Hall is another shocking place,
with only one door of exit, and a long passage.
Dobler, the wizard, had for his great feat the decapita-
tion of a man. He used to appeal for somebody to come
forward, but would quickly say, “Ah ! Nobody will take
the risk, so I must decapitate my assistant.” He could
only do the feat with a confederate. One night, in a
French town, the mayor came forward, and insisted on
being decapitated. A riot ensued, and Dobler had to
return the money.
The recent discomfiture of Bob Sutton
Sievier in an English law court was made all the
more trying for Bob by the “loyal” drivel of the
judge who heard the libel case. A few of the
plaintiff’s shady dealings in Australia and England
came to light; also the evidence showed that his
presentation at a Royal levee (Holy smoke!)
was officially cancelled when the Lord Chamber-
lain heard of his having punched Lord Deerhurst’s
nose in a Melbourne pub. Sievier’s awful pre-
sumption in nearly being presented at a levee
was the point that pierced deepest into the soul of
Judge Grantham, last week. His Honor, after
summing-up the various blackguardisms of the
adventurous plaintiff, said that “ Queen Victoria
would rather have given up the throne of England
than be addressed by such a man.” Seeing that
Y.R. during her long reign was addressed by
hundreds of titled rascals and contemptible
bounders who lacked even the physical gift of
good looks, and by two or three royal murderers
of the worst description, the judge’s reference to
the old lady and her Throne call for only one brief
comment— ‘ ‘ Rats ! ” The Bulletin’s disreputable
old friend Bob was seemingly too much broken iip
to say “Rats.” He merely wept on the floor
of the court.

Sydney Morning Herald is jubilant
because “Flinders” Nelson has ceased to be a
Ministerialist. The Flinders man ceased to sup-
port the See Ministry because it kept him waiting
on the door-mat for something and didn’t give
it him then. At least that’s the Why put forward
by Mr. Nelson; and it is the Why which explains
nearly all the recent defections from the Minis-
terial ranks.
Some curious mews drifts across from
Fremantle (W. A.) about the methods of the Barton
and Deakin Governments. The importation of con-
tract labor is prohibited in the Commonwealth, yet
Italian contract laborers were pouring into Wes-
tralia, and people wondered why. It now appears
that the Customs official at Fremantle never ques-
tioned the immigrants. He simply asked the
purser if he had any contract immigrants on board ;
the purser said he hadn’t—and the matter ter-
minated. Possibly the same affable official, if he
saw 42 coal-black Ashantees on deck, would just
as cheerfully accept the purser’s assurance that all
his passengers were white. An excess of affability
seems to have prevailed all round in the West. -
Victorian Standing Committee on Railways
got some light on the great land-gamble industry,
lately, when getting evidence as to a line asked
for from Beech Forest to Birregurra. Speculators
who had taken up blocks along what they reckoned
the likeliest routes —at £1 an acre, payable in 20
years—had in some cases actually refused £4 or £5
an acre for the timber rights alone. Some “ selec-
tors ” admitted they were only holding till the line
came, when they reckoned to get up to £lO an
acre. Commissioner Tait, foreseeing a loss on
working expenses, reported adversely,, so they are
likely to wait.

The “packing” of N.S.W. Deform Leagues
formed for the selection of the Carruthers candi-
dates is open and shameless. One engineer failed
badly, however. He got names of electors to the
number of over 700 ready for the selection meet-
ing. Walking up to the secretary, he planked
down his own cheque for £35 odd, to make them
members, but the chairman ruled the names were
not in order, as they had not been enrolled by the
secretary.
Melbourne Chamber of Manufacturers has
just elected its president, vice, and council for the
year, and, as usual, the former’s address consisted
of a vain bellow about socialistic legislation,
arbitration, and so forth. The president, by
the way, is an oilmaker in a medium way ; vice-
presidents, a dyer and a shirtmaker; while the
council contains a buggy cushion manufacturer, a
galvaniser, a mirror-maker, a blacking constructor,
and a few other trifling factory owners. The
really big employers seem to fight shy of the
organisation. By the way, £ £ Smith is on the
council, though what he manufacturers, except an
improvement in the innards of the young man who
doesn’t feel well (“ £1 is enough to send”), heaven
only knows.

When angry men get fighting, and when nations go to war,

To never strike a fellow-man; but merely strike a light.
It s always more effectual than soldiery or p’flce
To gather round thecouncll fireand smoke the pipe of peace
And nations will not go to war, nor men each other knock,

A notice in N.S.W. railway carriages tells
passengers in an off-hand way to “ break the glass
and press the button to communicate with the
guard.” It is understood that this is only to be
done if some great necessity arises for stopping
the train, and that there are heavy penalties for
frivolous breakage, but the notice says nothing
about that. It seems rather to imply that if the
passenger feels lonely he can break the glass and
press the button with a view to having a pleasant
chat with the guard about current topics. This
insufficiency of comment on the part of the notice
got one Major Walters out of serious trouble at
Parramatta Police-court a few days ago. He
accepted the friendly invitation to break and
press because he wanted to get out, and the court
held that the notice should be more explicit, and
not so foolishly affable.
William John Smith caught a cold in his head,
But he treated his feet for the trouble instead ;
O, quaint is the humor, but sad is the tale,
For consumption consumed him, and he died
without fail.
But happy Tom Jones, when afflicted was he,
By wisdom was guided, as doubtless you’ll see,
In treating his ailments so speedy and sure,
With doses of Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*
The effrontery of the Victorian “man on
the land,” who gets public money expended on
water schemes to improve his property and wants
to charge them to the city worker, is absolutely
immeasurable. Last year, when the drought was
on, the men on the land around Swan Hill,
Ultima and other places out from the Murray
River, clamored for a water scheme known as the
Long Lake Pumping Scheme. Irvine and Bent,
being terrible anti-socialists, of course agreed to
carry out the work. The cost was to be £27,000
and the men on the land agreed to allow their
acres to be rated for the purpose of providing
working expenses, interest and maintenance. This
year there have been good rain falls, and the
water from the Long Lake scheme will not be so
much required. Thereupon the men on the land
—the counter-parts of those eminently respect-
able firms in the city who defrauded the customs
and admit taking secret commissions in the butter
trade—flocked to Melbourne to ask the Govern-
ment not to put the rate of 2d. an acre on their
land as arranged, and to relieve them of all
liability, except for the few gallons of water that
they might use during the year. They also asked
that the distributing channels, which they had
agreed to construct themselves, should be paid for
out of the general taxpayers’ fund. Minister
Swinburne did not send for the police and order
the arrest of the men on the land as a pack of liars
and thieves. He said in the mildest possible
manner that the scheme must have a trial at least
for 12 months. And these are the people who
objected to any money being spent except “on
strictly reproductive works,” and who are at pre-
sent engaged in howling against the public servants
that they bring special influence to bear upon
Governments and Parliaments.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lhe little town of Rajool nestles on the
railway line between Brisbane and Gympie. Less
than two its extent are alienated. Yet it
can boast a 17-acre cemetery reserve, a 15-acre
school reserve, and police, post and telegraph, and
other casual reserves on the same extensive scale.
It is not going to be taken by surprise when its
tuture comes along.
New Life, New Love.
The breezes blow an the river below,
And the fleecy clouds float high,
And I mark how the dark-green gum-trees match
The bright blue dome of sky.
The rain has been, and the grass is green
Where the slopes were bare and brown,
And I see the things I used to see
In the days ere my head went down.
I have found a light in my long, dark night,
Brighter than stars or moon ;
I have lost the fear of the sunset drear
And the sadness of afternoon.
Here let us stand, while I hold your hand,
Where the light’s on your golden head —
Oh ! I feel the thrill that I used to feel
In the days ere my heart was dead !
N.s.w,
HEHRY LAWSON.
Love’s Pagan.
Our love was sweet while it lasted, dear,
How could we then do ill,
Who wantoned it not nor- wasted, dear,
Starved nor took our fill,
Chuckled at rapture and tasted, dear —
Call it a sin, if you will.
Wise you may deem them—quaint they were—
Who made of their love a thing
Pale, sickly, breathing a boudoir air,
And held by a leading string ;
Easy of cheek; too tame to dare
And have, at all cost, its fling.
For us, thank God, it were more than this,
And built of a sterner stuff.
Could wisdom ever have brought such bliss ?
Is Thought not a blind man’s buff,
Luring from Love with a Judas kiss ?
We loved —is not Love enough ?
Enough ? Ay ! child, it were all in all,
Too ravishing sweet for name ;
What matters it now what our lives befall,
Come shame as our lot, or fame.
We have heard and answered Love’s crowning call.
We have loved—it is all the same.
S.A.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
The Baby.
I lived once in a cottage
Set high above the sea—
The view from the verandah
Was living poetry.
I loved it for its cheapness—
For I was then in debt;
’Twas one of two small houses—
The other was to let.
I loved it for its quiet;
No sounds of infant life
Disturbed its fine Aloofness
With hints of squalid life.
Then cam ) a pale young couple ;
The empty house one day
They took—they had a Baby—
And Sweet Peace fled away.
They sang to it and nursed it,
And kissed it red and sore—
They might as well have cursed it,
It simply yelled the more.
I knew not what the name was
Of this sleep-killing cub—
I swore I would have called it
(If mine) Beelzebub.
And then there came a morning,
When silent was its voice—
And, with a heart malignant,
Thereat I did rejoice.
Alas, the pale young couple
Showed me their small, white dead,
With candles at its feet, and
With candles at its head.
The little form lay quiet
In its little coffin blue;
No more its voice would pierce me,
But pity pierced me through.
I think when life is over,
That sinners though we be,
God will—in spite of parsons—
Feel so to you and me.
N.S.W.
CREEVE ROE.
The Distinction.
If she makes the pace with an old scapegrace,
Who is shockingly wicked and wary,
If she flirts her eyes till they hypnotise
Like Malusine, the serpent fairy ;
If she tilts her clothes till her fretworked hose
Would enrapture a Geil or Torrey,
You can bet your shirt it’s her first long skirt,
And she’s out in the quest of quarry.
If she saunters slow in the sunset glow
With a soulful and sighful stripling,
Who lisps love lays in his lady’s praise
With the vim of a Burns or Kipling;
If her pulse is stirred at each passionate word,
As he calls her his star, his starling,
You can bet your life she’s an old man’s wife,
But would fain be a young man’s darling.
VIC.
O. CRAWFORD.

N.S.W. Juvenile Smoking Suppression
Act has already begun to breed “law points.”
r,!® otner day Louis Moss, Balmain agent for
Mick bimmons, the big Sydney tobacco merchants,
wa,s fined for selling tobacco to a boy, the tobacco
being for the use of the boy’s father. Quarter
Sessions, on appeal, upset the conviction, holding
that the Act only prohibited the sale of tobacco
to children for their own use.

Manly (Sydney) has long lacked a first-
class hotel. Yet the seaside village is the most
popular holiday resort south of the Equator, and
is simply infested every summer by drought-
stricken squatters from the interior, anxious to
revel by the ocean, and willing to pay high prices
for accommodation on the surf-beaten beach. A
co. has now been formed to purchase the old
Clarendon, which has the finest site on the ocean
parade but very little else in the way of attrac-
tions, and to build a new modern hotel of 130
rooms. Plans of the proposed new building show
an intelligent recognition of the possibilities of the
site, and of the requirements of a first-class
accommodation house. With anything like good
management the new Clarendon hotel should
boom, and the shares of the venture should be as
good a spec, in “industrials” as could be desired.
the mutoscope.

At Millchester, outside Charters Towers, Iden-
tity and Hope trial crushing was a failure, value being
only £1 4s. per ton. Millchester Gold Mines* shaft struck
a reef at 181 ft., which is encouraging*. Ruby M‘Donald,
one of the Ruby litter, has had a strike of 3ft. of mineral
stone, and Ruby United has recovered its reef. Other
Ruby shows are holding their ends up. Marshall’s Queen
just reaches the half-oz. ; unless the sands hold 5 or 6dwt.
where is the. promised increase? Tributors in the Bril-
hant P.C. have 80 tons of 2oz. value ; the old mine will
stand a lot of rooting yet.
* * * *
(0. Towers) is getting down, and
v. .u -°* m^ c hinery will soon help the plucky Plant to see
what his venture is stuffed with. Deep Levels are getting
out a trial crushing. Something - good in Vesuvius’ surface
bore, which is down about 250 feet, is watched for. The
market is very solid. Well-informed holders are plentiful,
and speculation is possible only at fancy prices.
* * » *
i J^ eve ™ nce ’, Kalgoorlie, in spite of treating
133,000 tons of ore during last year, has increasedits reserves
above the 700 ft. level from 383,000 to 402,000 tons. Levels
are being driven at 900 ft. and 1100 ft., but no account is
taken of the ore in reserve there. At the 700 ft. level there
is still some 550 ft. of driving to be done before the boun-
daries are reached, so reserves above that level are not
a 6 ’’, Mocked out. Recent developments on the
south lialgurli go to show that the Perseverance is likely
to have a long stretch of rich ore north. In addition to
paying for the sulphide plant out of revenue, co. has also
PyovHled £475,000 for development and depreciation, and
, /or divs. As the original working capital was
oo'y £50,000, CO. holds a record hard to beat. Most of
the big Rand mines are provided with cash
working capitals of £250,000 to £400,000, and
are fully equipped by the time they begin to
eai n revenues, and can distribute their profits practically
up to the_ hilt. A lot of disparaging reports have recently
been published about the mine. Most of them were
circulated with a view to draw the officials, but failed.
cai *'® out a t the meeting that, when co. was in
1898-1900, Frank Gardner advanced about
£125,000 without security and interest, and that, during
last year, to return the compliment, co. lent Gardner
£86,000 on security and interest, of which £50,000 had
been repaid. Manager Ralph Nichols had to admit, after
a rather severe heckling - , that the small amount of work
done below the 700 ft. level showed that “of course the
lode is poorer where we have got it than above, but the
poor zone is in'all the workings at different levels.” Co.
is estimated to have enough ore blocked out to run the
mill for three years, and to maintain present rate of divs.
What more can one reasonably want in a gold mine ?
* * * *
Golden Link, Kalgoorlie, appears to be badly
managed. In Dec., ’O2, a 10-head mill started crushing
the oxidised ore on the Eclipse, and was joined by a
second 10-head last Nov. After that returns immediately
tailed off, till April’s run culminated with an average of
2dwt. only, necessitating the closing down of the mill, and
men. In all mill has treated some
50,000 tons, much of which was simply rooted out. It is
now announced that the milling plant will be remodelled
to treat the sulphide ore. But there is absolutely none of
itdeveloped, as the shaft was stopped in June last at
300 ft., and no development has been done in the sulphide
zone. At the 300 ft, level the lode is said to be 4ft. wide
and worth loz. Thismay be so at one point, but certainly
has not been proved by blocking - out. The lode in the
Link lease at 1400 ft. is worth 12dwt. for a width of 4ft. ;
but here, again, no ore is opened up. The co. is heavily
handicapped by having most of its money invested
m such wild cats as New Chaffers Extended, and
foreign and Colonial Gold Recovery Co., the holders of
the so-far worthless May’s patent. John Bull has already
at various times, forked out £320,000 to run the show,
and must be getting very tired of sinking - his sovereigns in
such a pit. The remodelling of the present 20-head mill
to treat hard sulphide ore is practically impossible. The
plant consists of old batteries originally on the Golden
Crown (Kanowna) and North Star (Mt. Malcolm), and no
amount of remodelling will make them serviceable for
hard rock,
* * ♦ *
Golden Horsehoe, Kalgoorlie, is hustling on the
surface at present. The new 50-head mill is going up,
and on its completion, co. Will have the biggest battery
in W.A. A lot of labor-saving appliances are being intro-
duced, with the view of reducing costs. Two locally-
made rock-breakers, a modification of the Blake principle
are to be introduced, as well as a 350-ton ore bin. When
all these improvements are completed a material reduc-
tion in costs is anticipated. .Owing to the breakdown of
the new compressor the opening up of the mine is
suspended, but the old compressor can easily keep the
battery jigging pending the necessary repairs. Two 2-ton
self-dumping skips are to be installed in the main shaft.
These will greatly , facilitate hauling and do away with
truckers. Nearly all the “ silver tails ”on the mine have
either been fired out or “ docked,” and one surface shift-
boss now fills the place of some half dozen. Yankee
Loring is evidently determined to work as great a trans-
formation on the ’Shoe as he did on the Sons of Gwalia.
The sending away of rich sulpho-telluride ore to Fre-
mantle smelters to be paid for at £4 an ounce for 95 per
cent, of the gold contents is to be discontinued, and this
ore treated on the mine. This will very materially reduce
costs.
* * * *
South Kalgurli (W.A.) stands a very good
chance of yet becoming a div. payer, as recent develop-
ments are highly favorable. The sinking of Morty’s shaft
on the Perseverance lode is being continued, but has a
long way to go before the rich ore located by bore is
reached. The Lake View lode, now being exploited from
/00ft. to 846 ft. levels of the main shaft, shows very good
values. Practically the whole of the treatment plant
has been scrapped, and an entirely new equipment will be
installed. Every day goes to show that past management
was very far from being ideal, and Bewick, Moreing and
Co. can gain a lot of kudos at a cheap price. Co. is in a
very strong financial position, as its cash balance is over
£70,000. It must not, however, be overlooked that, from
start to finish, the directors had £188,500 to play with,
and yet ran the co. on the rocks. If great care and
economy are not exercised, a mine is one of the most
rapacious monsters on earth.
*** / *
Hannan’s Main Reef (W.A.), which now holds a
property, called Parnell’s, at Niagara, will probably be
“pushed” soon. Co. is equipped with a 10-head mill, which
is expected to start operations shortly, but mine is a one-
horse show. New property was acquired in March, ’O3
and two parcels aggregating 400 tons were treated for an
average of 19dwt. Reef is now being opened up at 260 ft.
where it is said to be 9ft. wide and payable. People
behind the co. have been connected with a dozen or more
wild cats, and not one unalloyed success. Anyone
anxious to get rid of his surplus cash is recommended to
follow the tips of the share pushers.
* * » *
Lady Shenton, Menzies (W.A:), is pushing on
developments on the new property situated a mile south
of the old show. Original holders of the leases won
27830 z. gold from 1918 tons, but it is unlikely that so
high an average could be maintained by a co. working on
a big scale. New property consists of 103 acres, and four
lodes traverse the leases, but very little work has been
done on them so far. The deepest shaft is 200 ft. Menzies
has proved such a patchy field hitherto that, until the
mine is properly opened up, it is venturesome to predict
how it will turn out.
The Coalition Negotiations.
As The Bulletin went to press on Tuesday night
it seemed practically certain that a coalition
between Deakin and Reid would be effected ; the
only contingency that stood in the way was an
offer to Deakin of a coalition by the Watson
Ministry, and that seemed improbable. If Deakin
and Reid unite it will be probably found that
Reid will be Premier, and Deakin will not accept
any office in the Cabinet.

THE PILLS
have gained their wonderful reputation by sheer force
