The recently boomed township of Wickliffe-
road, in the centre of the S.W. Vie. “Closer
Settlement” area, strongly emphasises the neces-
sity of a different land-taxation policy. The
sharks who bought the township allotments are
lying low and watching others prove the lead and
build business premises. If the show pans out all
right, they will simply hold out their hands for the
increased increment.
Be the flunkey in “ The Admirable Crich-
ton,” this letter comes from “ Bot ” :
It is stated (B. 21/4/04) that there is no future for the
butler or flunkey. Poet Browning’s father or grandfather
(I forget which) was a butler, and he buttled so success-
fully that, on his death, a stained-glass window in his
honor was added to the Anglican church of his native
town. Also, King Murat of Naples, Bonaparte’s brother-
in-law, was a French tavern-waiter, who had answered to
the call of “ garijon ! ” hundreds of times. In Australia,
I once knew a successful M.L.A. and metropolitan-city
Mayor who had been a garbage gentleman.
Now, the ex-garbage gentleman isn’t quite a case
in point—he doesn’t come under the head of
flunkey. As for the butler who rose to such
dazzling eminence that, when he died, his native
Little Pedlington honored him with a stained-glass
window, there is not, of necessity, anything very
dizzy about his greatness. Of Joachim Murat, it
is certainly said that his father was an inn-
keeper. It is also said that he was an affluent
fanner. Anyhow, Murat had a good school and
university training, started out to be a priest, spent
all his money in Hooliganism, joined the cavalry,
rose to be a sergeant by the time he was 21, was
given unlimited leave of absence for riot when he
was 22, was again in the army and a royal guard
at 23, and a sub-lieutenant at 24. A man who was
in such a hurry as that hadn’t much time $0 wait
or buttle. The flunkey who rose to greatness has
yet to be discovered.
The following letter in thieves’ slang was
part of the plunder seized in a recent raid on a
house of ill-repute in Melbourne. It left the
court in a confused tangle of law and fact, and
with a terrible buzzing in its head :
Church Bill, —Meet me at net to darkman in blooming
slum near the old padding ken to get off the swag. I
know a lavender cove and a swag chovey bloke that ■will
toke some of the white jacks. We must get a thimble-
fakir to christen and church the red jacks. I gave a
skister’s red thimble and slang and a cat to my mollisker
for stalling while we cracked the fakir’s chovey. The
noise of milling the glaze brought tray flies. She chucked
a reeler, and was lugged before the beak and fine a bull.
I met owt old men of the world tray lightman’s ago. I
chucked a dummy while they buzzed the clys of owt old
blokes with nailcans. They made out finups, a couter, a
red jack, and jerry, and a red spark-prop. I am now flush
of balsam, but on the shallow, and don’t go near the
boozing kens. When we get off the swag and get our
regulars we will go to the Bullumrancum next week. I
met an old flame to lightman that I thought was put to
bed with a spade and shovel stretches ago. I’ll dance at
my funeral if I knew her till she said, “ Is that you, my
bear cove. Tip us your doddle.” She does not now
attend the Cock and Hen Club, as she got japanned by
the Salvos. Don’t forget net to darkman. Mum now.
Don’t let the Johns see this.
An old Yictorian pressman reminisces :
Vic. Premier Tom Bent and the late John Woods, for-
merly a Vic. Minister of Railways, visited India just 20
years ago. Bent was recovering from an accident. Woods
was running his railway brake at the time, and went
across to the Calcutta Exhibition, where he arranged for
a practice trial of the invention. The judges were unsym-
pathetic, or did not know their work, and only gave the
Woods’ continuous automatic brake a certificate of the
third-class and a bronze medal like a swollen halfpenny.
This broke poor old John financially and physically, and
he was never the same man afterwards. Tom went up
the Ganges visiting Benares, -Wa, Delhi, Cawnpore,
Lucknow &c Travelled right up to Hurdwar, the
sacredest of all sacred places, for if. is there that the great
river starts to compose herself after her wild downward
flight from the Himalayas, 13,800 ft. above sea level. The
Ganges drops over 11,500 ft. in 180 miles, after which it
flows in a majestic body of water for over 1000 miles to
the Bay of Bengal. Bent inspected and authorised the
river, and it has flowed under his sanction ever since. He
met on his journey two other Australians, Jimmy Thom-
son and the late Mr. Bosisto, of eucalyptus oil memory,
and Jimmy has still in his possession a cabinet photo.
endorsed “ With great respect.—Thomas Bent, Delhi,
Jan. Ist, 1884.” No great or notable utterances made by
Thomas as he gazed upon the glories of Indian architec-
ture have been preserved. Bent doesn’t drop into poetry
as he walks heavily amid the monuments of ancient
civilisations ; he simply perspires.

‘ Jack bhay about the late dearly
beloved ” :
, , ~ , , , , , .
A phrase that makes me laugh is the late dearly
beloved, commonly used in death notices of husbands
and wives by the .survivor in domestic strife. I have
known many cases in which, the dearly beloved was an
unadulterated Reid financial statement. Recently a man
who had a writ out and three co-re s named died in one
of the States. His wife dearly beloved him, probably
because he was late. Men whom their wives would like
to have made ‘ late ’with an axe, women anxious for No.
1 to die to take on No. 2, and No. 1 knew it; men and
women who hated one another and parted, or swopped,
or lived a hair-rending, high-screeching and bashing life
—only let one die, and he or she becomes the late dearly
beloved. They go further, too, and perpetuate the slan-
der or perjury on tombstones. A week or two since I
visited a local cemetery to see an old friend off, and took
a walk round. Saw one stone, and know the creator.
The party underneath was his first dearly beloved. He
has had another dearly beloved recorded in another State,
and has since married a partner .Whom he left two months
after the wedding. He now lives with a fourth party.
Probably both those ladies will become “late dearly
beloveds if his luck holds good. On another stone a
man, whose wife told him to go out and drown himself, is
“ the deeply lamented and dearly beloved.” He did
drown himself when the remark above-quoted was made
once too often. Perhaps someone will call me a late
dearly beloved when I m dead. Let ’em,

Noticeable of late years that lodges are departing a
good deal from their old millinery. One seldom sees a
Forester nowadays in the plumed hat, green jerkin and
white doeskin breeches that were said to have roamed
Sherwood Forest in the days when the world was drunk
a r>d stole its neighbor’s bull. Now the Druids of the
North Island (M.L.) constitution are discussing the ques-
tion of discarding, for sanitary reasons, the patriarchal
white billy-goat beard worn on state occasions. It is
argued that the old second-hand beard which has served
as a chin-piece on many dead citizens who ought to have
known better is not a desirable thing to dangle on the
countenance of a new and clean generation, however
an xious it may be to keep the memory of Stonehenge
white. Heaven knows what wild things might be lurking
j n mU rky undergrowth of these dingy appendages ana
keen delight with which thev may spring out upon
modern Druid, who really works on the wharf. The
beard is doomed, but the feeling is strong as to retaining
|he methylated spirit flame humbug on the altar at initi-
afcions. The candidate is led into the room blindfolded at
such times, the lights are turned out, and only the un-
healthy glow from the spirit flame throws a sickly, green
g h r i e k of illumination on a half-moon of shapes clothed in
nightshirts and wearing the left-off and infected whiskers
of past generations. The effect is astounding on the ig-
norant, who savs, like Macbeth, “ Can such things be ? ”
or wor^s that effect.

READ THIS
LITTLE BIT OF
OUR HISTORY,
IN the year eighteen-seventy-one we occu-
pied a small store in Park Road, Liver-
pool, England, where we sold a watch
now and again, but not nearly so often as
we would have liked nor as often as we con-
sidered such reliable watches should be sold.
We were confident that our watches were as
good as could be got—the trouble was how
to get the public to know. We pondered
over this subject day and night and finally
concluded that we would try what the news-
papers could do for us. Such a thing as
selling watches through advertisements had
never been done in England before, still we
were convinced that once the general public
knew that our watches were what we said
they were—Reliable—we must succeed.
You know how it is with a man who has a
dependable watch—he is proud of it and
loves to speak of its accuracy, and naturally
the firm who supplied the watch comes in for
a full share of the credit. Well—we put
our everything into the venture. How we
succeeded our eight splendid Australasian
stores give testimony. Orders flowed in
from all over the World, particularly from
Australia and New Zealand. In fact, so
numerous were the orders that we decided
to open in Sydney. Our success was
phenomenal, and in Melbourne, Auckland,
and Dunedin we had to open shops, followed
later by Perth, Wellington, Christchurch,
and Brisbane, each being a success. What
better proof of the satisfactory performance
of our watches do you want ?
This is a 14ct. Gold Keyless Hunting Cased Watch,
suitable for young ladies. It has a reliable jewelled
movement—exquisitively engraved solid 14ct. gold
cases—strong keyless work, and altogether is a sound
and serviceable watch. It is possible to sell a gold
watch at a lower price, but as the time-keeping and
construction is not such as would give lasting satis-
faction to our customers, we do not stock them.

A gem culled from the report of a
meeting of the Goulburn branch of the N.S.W.
Social Gimlet Brigade :
Mrs. M'Dougall said the fountain of the stream should
be cleansed. She did not believe it was the ignorance of
the girls that was the matter, but that they knew a good
deal too much. Parents were greatly to blame in
allowing little boys and girls to roam about the streets at
night—girls of 14 in short frocks. They ought to he kept
at home to help their parents. Their parents ought to
see what sort of literature they read. The girls were
always reading something about princesses. She believed
that knowledge of good and evil was innate, and that it
was evil hearts that made us do wrong. In her opinion
the men were not half as bad as the women. She
believed that the maternity homes for unmarried women
and the children’s homes should be shut up. A raid
should he made on them, and they should be shut like
public-houses. In other days, if a girl fell she was
branded and unhappy. The homes had a well-paid
matron and a staff of nurses with nice stiff collars and
cuffs on. It took illegitimacy to keep these institutions
going on. If the Christian public rose up they could put
an end to the present condition of things in twelve
months. The fathers should be compelled to support
the children. There would not then be married men
waiting in cabs in Sydney to take the pretty barmaids
and waitresses home. She would like the people to go
into these homes when it was not visiting day. If the
mothers took the children out to work they would be just
as well treated as now, and the matrons and nurses could
go to work_ and take off their stiff collars. Sin was not
shown as sin was to the girls. As to books, there was no
better book for teaching than the Bible.
There are points about this lady’s idea of branding
the girl who fell. Would Mrs. M'Dougall accept
the job of Public Brander, and what would be the
best thing to use to mitigate the odor of burning
flesh on branding day, when a batch of unmarried
mothers was being put through ? Also, how about
a prize (say a new dress and a hat with a big
feather in it) for the unmarried mother who made
least fuss and noise over the salutary operation ?
From a man in court:—
An unusual case of wife desertion came before Justice
Hood in Melbourne lately. It was the first so far under
the Amended Marriage Act, ’Ol, by which an absconding
husband can be brought back to the State under criminal
warrant. It appeared that in ’O2 the lady in the case,
who was then working at a Home for the Fair but Frail,
at 6s. a week, advertised for a husband, and accused
applied for the vacant billet. She excused the existence
of a little girl of five by saying she was a widow. The pair
hovered round each other until it seemed desirable they
should get married in real earnest, before another grass-
orphan had to be explained away, and they married
accordingly, the lady spending the honeymoon in the
Home still toiling for 6s. a week. Then there was an
interval of affluence when her husband put her in lodgings
by herself and gave her £2 10s. a week. Next he suddenly
stopped supporting her and faded away to Maoriland,
where the Law found him looking* at a hot spring or
something and brought him back. Defendant said he
had omy married the lady to “ save her good name/* and
that it was agreed he was not to be called on to maintain
her ; also, that he hadn’t deserted her—he had merely
e u or a holida y on bis doctor’s advice as he was in such
a highly nervous condition he might commit suicide any
minute. That His Honor accepted most of this yarn is
shown by his accepting £5O bail for accused to step up
for sentence if called upon. He was an irregular sort of
man, anyhow, and perhaps irregular treatment is the best
for him. A man who goes to a Rescue Home for a wife
(and incidentally a family), allows her to toil at ss. a
week during the honeymoon, maintains her luxuriously
for a few months afterwards, then suddenly drops every-
thing with a thud, is too involved for ordinary criticism#
No known rules apply to him.
To-day has been giving its readers the
interesting information that, at a recent horse-
show, Edward VII. made use of a pocket-hand-
kerchief with a red border; and, further, that
when he had “blowed ” the royal proboscis he put
the “wipe” in his pocket, and not up his sleeve.
This latter statement was evidently intended as a
solemn warning to the gilded youth who have
been putting the blessed “wipe” of old England
in the wrong place. The scribe, who calls him-
self “The Major,” goes on to add this stirring
information:—
For the rest, the King was wearing a stick-up collar
with turned-down points rounded at the corners. It is
not a collar that suits every man, and, if it does not fit
accurately, it is apt to make the wearer look slovenly. I
need scarcely add that the King’s collar was about as near
perfection as any collar can hope to be in this sinful,
laundress-stricken world. I noticed that the King is very
conservative in the matter of gloves.
A British king was once supposed to be the boss
warrior and statesman of the land. Then he fell
to the humbler job of being the father of his
people, and as he usually wasn’t married to the
motner of his people the arrangement excited
remark. Now he is an authority as to what part
of the human clothes the handkerchief should be
carried in.

Adelaide Critic on the recent crisis :
The Society influence has been worked for all it is worth
to impress Lord Northcote with a sense of Mr. Reid’s im-
portance, but Lord Northcote had been better advised
than Lord Hopetoun. The pinchbeck “ society ” of Sydney
and Melbourne makes a great difference between Mr.
Reid and Mr. Watson. Heaven only knows why. What
kind of a difference does Lord Northcote make, do you
think? Further than that, the Governor-General has
wisely seen for himself and judged for himself. Even
Lady Northcote has had her part. On the Thursday
preceding’ the crisis she chatted for a full half-hour across
the barrier of the Ladies’ Gallery with Mr. Watson. In
the light of after events, when we know the cables were
flying backwards and forwards to London, this public
action of her Excellency reads like a demonstration. It
was the public answer of Government House to the secret
whisper that a Labor Ministry would be a dreadful thing 1
Her Excellency never came again after the fateful motion
was moved, but the Private Secretary did day after day.
This was as it should be. Australia owns no secret
governing class controlled by a rubbishy “ society.”
• * * *
Prohibition was carried in Ashburton
(M.L.) at the last local option poll, and the sly-
grog man immediately became a leading feature of
the landscape. Some sly-grog-selling cases have
just been heard, and while the magistrate was
struggling with them Ashburton gathered outside,
armed with dead marines and the other family
relics of better days and waited for the witnesses
for the prosecution. The police got those wit-
nesses out by a back way and drove furiously for
Chertsey, 12 miles away, in order to catch the ex-
press train for Christchurch or the first boat for
Guam. Ashburton gave a howl when it learned
that it had been tricked, and broke for the railway
station, gathering blue metal on the way. Fifty
men boarded the express as it passed through,
bound for Chertsey. At Chertsey the
police had put the witnesses into a meat
van and locked it up, and they had it
shunted on to the train when that protracted
caravan arrived. The Ashburtonites rushed up
and down seeking their quarry, and at last one of
them looked through a crack m the meat van and
saw the pallid testifiers inside. Hooting and
groaning they rushed the van and climbed all over
it, and discharged blue metal, but failed to open
it. Then they ran for a crowbar and dynamite,
but while they were doing this the train whirled
off at the rate of 10 miles an hour. The baffled
electors then took complete charge of Chertsey,
and drank the contents thereof. Finally they
decided to walk the 38 miles that lay between
them and Christchurch, and slay the witnesses.
They started in great disorder, but fell down by
the way, and were found there in the morning,
being thoughtfully looked at by cows and other
produce.
* * * *
There seems to be a pretty big element of
luck even in the sentence you get. At Wagga
(N.S.W.) a few months ago, an elderly man
pleaded guilty to stealing over 100 bags of wheat
—second offence, too—and the Judge brushed
away a tear as he wondered how such a respect-
able old man had committed such an offence.
A fine followed. At Cootamundra (N.S.W.) a
few days ago, a farmer, for stealing two
bags of wheat from a neighbor, got two years
hard ! Presumably it was as a warning not to
waste the time of the court in future with any-
thing less than a truck-load.

Don’t bolt your food. If you haven’t
time to eat your dinner without hurrying
over it, you had better go without it till
you have. Better be hungry than ill.
Don’t over eat. Indeed never take
quite as much as you feel you could. If
you are uncomfortable after meals it is
a sure sign either of excess or digestive
weakness.
Machinery works best when it is
cleansed and re-oiled. It is just the same
with the human machinery, the stomach
requires periodical cleansing and the
gastric juices require stimulation.
The retention of undigested food or
fermented liquids generates poisonous

The “ Daily Express,” London, Dec. 31st, 1903, says *
“ If, among the many new resolutions which will be taken in the course of the next
day or two, the determination to introduce greater common-sense into the ordinary meals
were to find a place it would be well with the households where it was adhered to. Now it
is winter, and we wear thick clothes and overcoats; in the dog-days we shall put on light
vesture and discard all wraps. But hot or cold, winter or summer, we eat the same food ;
we stoke our bodies with the same amount of fuel. Then we are surprised by the pains and
miseries of Indigestion. This is absurd. Still more absurd is the fact that in cities, by
men of sedentary occupations, practically the same dietary is followed as was customary
when our forefathers lived in the country and were actively engaged under the open sky.”
Health and happiness are possessions we all desire to obtain ; too often failure arises from
the simple neglect of suitable food and the correction of excesses in diet. Every other
person you meet nowadays seems to suffer from Indigestion in some form or another.
Every man, and every woman, can, however, so regulate the digestive functions as to defy
this modern terror of our civilisation. Every stomach requires cleansing occasionally,
especially after rich living. Every liver requires periodical stimulation, whilst it is obvious
that to flush the bowels must perforce be beneficial. Kutnow’s Powder is the only perfect
exponent of this cleansing treatment.

Apropos of the renewed activity of the
Social Purity Brigade, a wealthy and cantankerous
citizen, whose character is not above suspicion,
and who is liable to be denounced any day,
has prepared vengeance in advance. He says
that no Gimlet Society is going to hit him with
impunity. As a precaution, he employed a
broken-down Gentleman Oneto lookup amultitude
of birth and marriage records, and find out how
long an interval there was between the marriage
and the birth of Gimlet, junior. He says that
when the Gimlet push denounces him as a social
outcast (which he frankly admits he is), it will
have to select its assailant very carefully.

Dyspepsia.
Indigestion:
Thb torments endured by a person who suffers from Ind gestion .are, unfortunately, so well known to
most of us that it is not necessary that the symptoms of that modern scou ge should bo described here.
\V nat sufferers want to know is how to become Permanently cured, end they can be enlightened
by reading this article.
There are several kinds of indigestion : Gastric Catarrh, in which the food becomes covered
with mucous, and consequently withstands the action of the digestive juices ; Gastric Platulency,
the development of wind or gas ; Gastric Diarrhoea, caused by the food being hurried through the
digestive organs too fast; and Constipation, which is a very prevalent and obstinate cause, which
must be removed, or digestion cannot be expected to proceed.
Food must be digested and converted into blood. Nature makes this one of her most imperative
laws of life. During the process of digestion food is entirely changed in composition by the action of
the juices of the internal organs through which it passes. In the mouth it is thoroughly mixed with
saliva. Then it is swallowed, and enters the stomach, where it is acted upon by the gastric juices and
becomes partly liquefied. From the stomach it passes to the smaller intestine—which is about 18 feet
in length—and there certain portions of the food are liquefied by the bile and other juices. The food
thus made fluid is in a condition to be absorbed into and become a constituent part of the blood.
The indigestible portion of the foo.d is discharged into the larger intestine, whence it is in turn
expelled from the body together with other refuse matter.
Just as certainly as that it is necessary to life that food must be absorbed, so, likewise, it is
essential that the blood must be in a condition to absorb the food. Torpidity of the liver is the chief
cause of nearly every case of indigestion, and when the liver is torpid, the kidneys are always sympa-
thetically affected to a greater or lesser extent. The blood, which should be transformed, cleansed
and filtered by the kidneys and liver, then contains urinary and biliary poisons, and is there-
fore a feeble absorbent of nutriment. This condition of the blood reacts upon the nervous system of
the digestive organs, and prevents the flow, and alters the quality of the digestive juices.
The entire nerve energy of a parson suffering from indigestion is weakened, owing to this con-
taminated Condition of the blood, and the general feeling of mental and physicakdepression, which is
experienced during an attack of dyspepsia, is due to this cause.
The blood must be continuously purified by the action of the liver and kidneys, or good digestion
cannot be expected to occur. °

cures indigestion and dyspepsia simply because it restores the liver and kidneys to health and activitv
and, as a consequence, the blood becomes pure, it absorbs nutriment freelv. and conveys this nutri-
ment to the nerves throughout the body. Nature is merely aided in her efforts to preserve a balance
m the manifold and complex processes of waste and renewal by which life is maintained.
Remember, constipation or irregularity of the bowels must be overcome. Warner’s Safe Pills
Should, therefore, also be taken in sufficient quantity to ensure one free movement daily.
Do not overload the stomach. Avoid tea, coffee, alcoholic stimulants and rich or fat foods Do
not eat generously of any solid food.

A Night in Colombo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE appearance of a white man in the street
is as of a worm flung into a fowl-yard.
From all points of the compass ’rickshaw
men converge. Dark corners of seeming respecta-
bility give birth to the human beasts of burden,
and the white man who wants to stretch his legs
has a lively time.
It requires but very few moments in Asia to
convert a man to the policy of a White Australia.
One’s black brudder grows less lovable every
moment; and here, after 18 hours’ residence in
Colombo, I am beginning to regard the late Simon
Degree as a great and good man. We tell the
’rickshaw men we don’t want them, in the tones
and phraseology which we learned years ago from
friends who drove bullocks along the Darling.
But language which would make the most hard-
ened Australian bullock wither into dust only
excites our fellow British subject of Ceylon to
further feats of grovel. He has a profound belief
that the more abject he is the greater is the respect
he will command with the ruling race, and the
result is that the ordinary garden slug is a fine
manly upstanding creature as compared with the
native of Ceylon.
As we walked along the red roadway heavily
charged with the smell of Ceylon—which is the
smell of the Cingalese—a body of satellite rick-
shaw men followed in the rear, fan-wise, making
extra endeavors towards abasement. Every now
and then pedestrian darkies stepped up to start a
respectful conversation, with backsheesh as the
objective. A disgusting old dodderer flaunted a
gnarled and deformed arm before our eyes until
we were blackmailed into securing its removal.
A strange object scampering over the roadway
materialised under the arc-light into a legless boy
who ran on his hands as fast as we could walk,
until we stayed his speed by the usual method
of making it worth his while. But these were
mere amateur beggars. The real trained ex-
ponents of the advantage of technical education
we found at the corners of the small lanes, howl-
ing dismally for the amusement of a score of
spectators with a thoroughness unknown to the
perfunctory methods of Australian mendicancy.
Their stands were illuminated with naphtha lamps
set on the ground, so that every facial contortion
expressive of extreme woe was seen to the best
advantage. When a shriek of peculiarly heart-
breaking intensity broke upon the air, or a face
was turned into some remarkable contortion de-
noting terrible anguish, the audience signified its
approval by casting small coppers to the beggar.
I would like to take one of our Australian musical
critics to Colombo and let him have a free hand
describing the timbre, the cadence, the lower and
upper register, and other details of the beggar-
men’s voices. One man who might have been a
beggar, but wasn’t, was a dwarf scarcely three
feet high—a splendidly proportioned little fellow
and an old man for Colombo —he must have been
quite 50, with his gray beard hanging on his chest,
fie looked like a gnome stepping from a story of
German folk-lore, and my commercial instincts
immediately impelled me to think of taking him
to Australia in bond and sub-letting him at a profit
to some sewing-machine people.
A lanky nigger with a native mandolin slung
across his shoulder approached. ‘‘Me no Mahom-
medan, master; me no Buddhist, master,” he
said, persuasively. “Me Christian —you want
see nice lady ? ”
We were touched at the evidence of the refining
influence of the Terewth upon the poor idolator,
and refrained from sending him away. He told
us he knew we were from Australia, and offered
to show us complimentary testimonials—presum-
ably professional—from many of our fellow-
countrymen, including the President of a Legisla-
tive Council and a very eminent Melbourne clergy-
man. He told us that he used his mandolin to
accompany himself with when he sang hymns in the
mission church on Sundays, but more often to
supply the music for Colombian damsels to dance
for the amusement of tourists. It was an instru-
ment that served God and Venus with equal
facility. He and his family, explained our
Christian friend, had been musicians since the
days of the old Kandian kings.
“Do you know Professor Peterson?” my com-
panion asked him.
“ Oh, yes, master ; he say I play beyootifool.”
“And Professor Darkhole Hale ?” I inquired.
“ Yes, master,” he replied.
“ And Professor Bland Rignold Bent Reid Cole
Book Arcade?”
“Yes, master; all Australian people know me.
You want see nice, pretty lady ? ”
We told him this was a demand that was never
fully supplied. “ But where are they ? ” I asked.
“We’ve been walking about for an hour and
haven’t seen any sort of girls, pretty or otherwise.”
“ Oh, no, master,” he replied in a shocked tone,
“ no go about the street.”
“But where we come from,” explained my
friend, “there are more girls than men in the
streets.”
“Oh,” he said in pain tempered with patriotic
pride, “ all good girls Colombo.”
Before we had time to resent the remark the
scoundrel’s black face brightened.
“But me take you nice, pretty lady—dancy
lady.” Then we decided that a Nautch dance was
an experience not to be missed.
_ Our Christian friend led us down dark, filthy
rights-of-way, squirming with black naked hu-
manity, along streets that were really passages,
with trays of decomposing, insect-covered food
jutting from shop-fronts on either side ; past
factories where naked men drove sewing-machines
and hammered tinware, and even constructed
coffins in the middle of the congested, noisy
thoroughfare. We pushed into a street compara-
tively wide and comparatively empty. Our guide
paused to tell us that the house he was taking
us to was the most select in Colombo, in all
Ceylon, in all India. Had we waited another
moment it would have been the most select in all
Asia. Its houris represented the very aristocracy
of beauty and vice.
We paused at the door of a dingy, two-storeyed
stucco building that reminded one of the oriental
splendors of Flinders-street, Melbourne, opposite
the churches. “Vare nice lady—nice pretty
lady,” hinted our guide as he knocked. My
companion interrupted that it was up to us, and I
handed over half a rupee (Bd.). “Thank you,
master, thank you, de good Christian. May
God’s blessing rest upon you. Vare nice lady.”
The door was_ opened, and a darkey wearing
European clothing, which is the insignia of
opulence and culture, stood before us. “Come
in, gentlemen,” he said: “nice lady here.” He
led us through a room where a respectable nigger
family was making its evening meal—father,
mother and children—and up a ricketty set of
stairs. True to the traditions of Asia, the
“ strange woman ” still lives in the high places of
the city, while unfortunate respectability has to
take her rejects. At the head of the stairs was a
room furnished like the kitchen of a humble
Australian laborer, and in the chairs were four
fat-faced black girls, about equal in facial and
physical beauty to the average black gin. But,
unattractive as they were, they seemed a quar-
tette of Venuses beside two horrible French
harridans who emerged from a couple of hutches—
they could be called nothing else—partitioned off
from the room,
“We want a Nautch dance!” we hurriedly
explained to prevent misapprehension,
“Vare well,” said the master of ceremonies;
“’Ow many girl—six girl?”
“No,” we cried, “a couple of girls will be
plenty, if they do the dance properly.”
“White girl more,” he explained.
“ But we don’t want white girls,” said my
companion.
“ Wouldn’t have them at a gift ! ” I added, with
a sudden feeling of nausea at the thought of the
two French wrecks in the throes of a dance.
The master of ceremonies did some rapid mental
arithmetic : “Two black girl—one pound.”
“Get off the grass!” yelled my companion
indignantly.
“We don’t want to buy them —only to see them
dance.”
“ One poun’!” persisted the man.
“Let the galled jade wince!” murmured my
companion, and we gathered up our hats and made
for the stairs. The place was none too alluring,
anyhow, and the prospects of the dance were not
encouraging.
We had gone two steps when: “Three girl—
one poun’! ” yelled the master of ceremonies over
the banisters. “Four girl, master—four girl,
gen’lmen—four girl, no clothes on, one poun’.”
We continued our descent. “Six girl,” he was
almost pathetic. “ Six girl, no clothes on, one—
-10 rupee. We stepped into the street and looked
for the ’rickshaws. One approached. There was
a clatter on the stairs, and the door dashed open.
As we spun away in our ’rickshaws we heard on
the air the hysterical shriek of the Christian of
Colombo crying pathetically after us : “Sah,
genelmen, sahibs, all de girls, no clothes on, five
rupee.”
M‘G.
Dogs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE BULLETIN and other papers have
been jesting lately about beer and counter-
lunch. I don’t see much to jest at. The
only thing wrong with them is that a bloke
can’t get enough of them.
One rainy night I was sleeping under a ledge of
rock in the Domain. Two wet dogs came in and
woke me. I put them out, but they appeared so
miserable I let them return. Next day, about 11
o’clock, when I strolled over to get my pint and
counter-lunch, they followed me. If ever you
have been in a 3d. bar about that time
you know there is rather more tucker on
the floor than on the counter, so the
three of us did well and went home to the Domain.
We became great friends, and in my leisure
moments (which are numerous) I began to teach
my dogs. First I christened them : I called one
Dry Dog (he wasn’t very dry the first time I made
his acquaintance), and the other Yellow Pup (he
happens to be black). The system I adopted is a
sort of telegraphy of the tail—a certain number of
shakes for each letter. The intelligence of Dry
and Yellow is wonderful. In less than a fortnight
they were able to read all the old newspapers in
my cave. When one wants to speak to me he
barks twice. Then I look, and his tail does the
rest.
Passing St. Mary’s Cathedral once we met a
strange dog, and Yellow went for him. Before
closing with his enemy he barked twice. I looked
to see what he was saying, but couldn’t under-
stand a word, and told him so. He looked round
and said he had made a mistake, and told me not
to notice his tail any more until business was
over. Then he resumed operations.
It was near 12 o’clock before I went into a pub.
one day, and we had passed several. Yellow
said “What’s rp? Pm hungry.” I said “No
money.” “ Dickm !” was his reply, as he walked
into the nearest pub. While in a bar I let
a little beer fall on Dry’s nose for a trick-.
I’m sorry I did it. He said he liked it,
and asked for more. So did Yellow, and they
have become awful drunkards. They don’t wait
for me to go with them now ; they go out for a
spree on their own, as they call it. However, as
they usually bring me a couple of bottles of beer
and some newspapers, I don’t care much. They
came home tight, the other day, and had a row,
and called one another Dill and George. I don’t
know what they were driving at.
The only thing they can’t do is write. One
tried it with a pencil in his mouth, but it was no
good. There seems one way of getting over the
difficulty. If the Society for the Prevention of
the Heathen will send me money enough to buy a
typewriter all will be well.
O.
“ Asiatic traders own property in Durban
to the; value of £500,000, and nearly half the retail
trade is in their hands.” — East Rand Express.
Prime Minister Watson’s brief definition
of Socialism is about the best yet given. “Roughly
speaking, socialistic legislation, as I understand
it, includes all laws that tend to restrain the
stronger from taking advantage of the weaker
members of society.”
Maoriland supports in some kind of way
6GI lawyers—one to every 1324 of the population,
or one to every 331 male adults. Forty-eight we. e
admitted last year, but a great many ambitious
youths who pass their exams, don’t apply for
admission immediately, preferring to remain with
their employers to get a deeper insight into the
art of having a shortage in the trust funds. So
Maoriland is probably even more lawyered than
appears.
A letter from Townsville (Q.) :
Ex-State Premier Philp “ Jeremiahed” at Townsville
against his successors t’other night, and offered as his
panacea to sell or give the State away to anyone at the
buyer’s own price. Philp left some nice legacies behind
in the historic dredges, the Seaforth estate, the tank
engines, and other evidences of “business” capability,
ltobert of Townsville (where they have spent about
£2,000,000 in trying to scoop up the -bed of the ocean)
objects generally to the Morgan - Kidston policy, pro-
bably because Morgan and Co. are trying to pay as they
go. This causes the ex-Premier to paw the air and sa3'
things. lie was especially aghast at his successors even
thinking of building a railway out of revenue; borrowing
or syndicating—there is no other way, says the discredited
prophet. Syndicates are the thing to save the country,
for evidence whereof look at the Chillagoe Railway Co.
for a shining example. The syndicate microbe and the
borrowing germ seem to have got into the very marrow
of some of the alleged politicians on this island.
Seeing the Northcote barouche drive up to
St. Paul’s Cathedral on Sunday morning one is
impressed for the moment by the reverent
demeanor of the bald-headed coachman on the
box. He takes off his hat and holds it before his
face, as though he were praying inwardly, whilst
the footman (also bare-headed, of course) is open-
ing the door of the carriage for their Nibses. The
coachman’s attitude is so suggestive of official
reverence for the sacred building that one is rather
shocked to see him replace his hat the moment
the G.-G. and his Lady pass through the cathedral
gates. They are the holy institution the coach-
man has been paying his respects to.
About that transient Victorian so jfc of a
year or two back, the Flitterers, “ Jaybell ” writes
from Brisbane:—
A person with a withered eye and a mournful cast of
countenance knocked at my front door, the other day,
and informed me (as clearly as a heavy drape of whisker
would permit) that he was one of the Lost Ten Tribes.
Then he whirled three times on my door-mat and sat down
as suddenly as anything that ever happened. He said
that he was a holy Flitterer, and the last survivor of the
sect, the others having relapsed into sin and cut off then-
long, fervent hair. When he flittered from my humble
home the hairy freak’s innards carried a fairly large
supply of solid refreshment. Two days later he whirled,
sat down, and discussed aliment at a friend’s house in
another street. I await a return flitter, and am armed
with a lathe-turned pie and a smile worth framing.

The Editor’s Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE sat on the front verandah cool on a
summer eve gone by,
The Editor of the Tintarabin Times,
the Editor’s wife, and I ;
The girls and the boys in the room behind made
melody weird and wild.
“ Classical music—Tintarabin style,” the Editor
said, and smiled.
The level rays of the setting sun through the
vine-clad trellis came,
And touched the white and the red wine bright
into topaz and ruby flame.
“ Hermitage both, you may take your oath,” said
the Editor with a grin ;
“I got them in lieu of subscriptions due from
Vigneron Patsy Finn.
The Editor’s face was brown and lean, and his
face was scored and lined,
But his dark grey eyes were merry and keen as
his wife’s were blue and kind.
“A toast!” he cried, and he bowed to her.
“ You'll drink this toast, I know ;
It’s ‘ A midnight ride with a stolen bride a score
of years ago.’ ”
They looked at each other, laughing low at a jest
unknown to me;
As happy a pair, as they sat there, as eyes might
wish to see.
“ Mary, my pride, shall I tell of that ride?” with
a dancing eye in his head,
The Editor asked of his wife ; she smiled, and
“Yes, if you like,” she said.
“In sixty-eight, and God bless the date!” the
Editor then began,
“ I was light and lithe and young and blithe, and
cared not for any man ;
Ah, those were days ! There were no gray hairs
like these you see on my brow,
When spur on heel had a homelier feel than pen
in the hand has now.
“ But though I was proud, with a head unbowed,
some weaknesses I must say
I wasn’t above, so I fell in love with the daughters
of Morgan Shea;
They were but two, but I vow to you that which
of them—Mary or Anne—
I wanted to marry I knew no more than the least
interested man.
“Mary was fair, and Anne was dark, and both
were the country’s boast,
Though now” (with a wink) “that I come to think
it was Anne was admired the most;
And both were kind and both inclined to like me
—but chiefly Anne.”
“ Conceit! ” said his wife, and fondly smiled on
the little grey-bearded man.
“But I was only a printer then, with only a
printer’s pay
To keep myself and a hungry horse,” the Editor
said, “ while they
Were ladies of rank, with coin in the bank, their
father a squatter proud,
When squatters were all of the aristocrats in this
country-side allowed.
“ And Morgan Shea had all the pride of his class,
and a trifle more,
And he gave me to understand one day that if
ever I crossed his door
He’d shoot me as sure as sudden death ; and he
had a quaint grim way
Of keeping his word, however 'absurd, this same
Mister Morgan Shea.
“But Love, they say, has its own fine way of
piercing its own way through.
How much more so with a love like mine that was
ample enough for two ?
And thus, perchance, to my veering mind it
suddenly did occur
That Anne was the girl who was meant for me—
and I settled it so with her. •
“ So it came to pass, on a night like this, when the
moon rose late and large,
That I rode to the homestead of Morgan Shea,
with a swift led horse in charge.
The horse that I rode myself—ah, well, I have
seen some Horseflesh since,
But seldom a breed with the stay and speed of the
breed of old Black Prince.
“That’s always excepting Shea’s own horse—a
terrible great red roan—
And his cream-white mare ; there were none to
compare with the Prince but these alone.
But these there was nothing to catch or match,
nothing to tame or tire :
The wind in their wake on the coldest day was a
breeze that burned like fire.
“ Strong language, this ? Well, perhaps it is; but
remember I tell you—l
There were horses to ride in this country-side a
score of years gone by.
But the roan and the mare, I could safely swear,
were secure in their stable, so
We had nothing to fear on that midnight ride so
long and long ago.
“ Across the bridge at Gulliver’s Creek, and over
the Frenchman’s Hill
We swept, and down to the long grey flats—and
there, in the moonlight still,
Row after row the ring-barked trees rushed by us
on either hand,
Like files of phantoms and sheeted ghosts swift-
flitting across the land.
‘Galloping there I became aware of the sound of
hoofs behind,
And ‘ Anne,’ I cried, ‘we have #dden fast, but
now we must burn the wind.’
For answer I heard a laugh ; I turned—and there
was a sight to see !
As I am a man, instead of Anne it was Mary who
rode with me!
“‘ A printer’s error,’ you’ll say. It was, but I was
an easy man,
And I was in love with Mary, too, though I had
arranged with Anne.
And so.” . . . But his wife interrupted here:
“You see, it was this way. Jim
Had taken so long to make up his mind that we
made it up for him."
The Editor said : “ Having gone so far, you can
very easily see
There was nothing to do but keep going on—a
thought borne in on me
With special force, as over the coarse, dry creek-
bed rubble, the grind
And clatter I heard, and grew cold to hear, of the
galloping hoofs behind.
“ For I knew, without turning head to see, by the
insolent easy way
They were hauling us in, ’twas the roan and the
mare belonging to Morgan Shea.
But the road was clear, and the goal was near,
and I muttered to Mary—‘Ride !
I'll rein up here —and good-bye, my dear —say
‘Jim in his stirrups died.’
“Of course this was bluff, and she laughed
enough, and ‘Die if you must and will,’
Touching my horse with her whip, she said—‘but
first to the church on the hill.’ . . .
And there on the Old Man Hill we were met, full
face by the golden dawn,
And behold, in its light like amber bright, the
tresses of Mary shone.
“And I felt that, in time, I might bear the shock
of the change of sweethearts, when
I heard, like thunder of waves on the rock, the
roan and the mare again !
And, oh, if for Mary and me it had not been a
tragic and dreadful thing,
It would have been joy to have gazed, just then,
on the fierce space-cleaving spring
“ Of that wonderful pair, as, like Crested waves in
the dawn, up the hill they came,
As though a challenge of speed to bear to the
morning’s coursers of flame.
Mary said ‘ Yes—they’re a handsome pair. It
was settled long since. That’s Anne
You see on the mare, and the red roan there is
ridden by Dan M‘Cann.’
“’Twas so. The woman, woman-like, thought of
the human pair ; while I
Thought of the horses—so we are made —but
under the morning sky
Marriages twain took place, and I had for grooms-
man Dan M'Cann,
And Mary for bridesmaid —laughing at me—my
other lost sweetheart Anne !
“ And Morgan Shea ? What had he to say ? He
hid much to say, indeed ;
Too profane and vain to hint in print, but lurid
enough to read
In the dark. However, it all came right—-Dan’s
master now at The Limes
(Shea’s station that was), and I’m on deck of the
old Tintarabin Times.”
The Editor slowly winked his eye, and “A
printer’s error, ” said he,
“It was.” And he fondly smiled on his wife, and
we drank to her health, did we.
And I thought as I gazed at the pair—well,
there, I know all about the leaven
Of error one finds in printing on earth—but this
error was made in Heaven.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
“A Fool There Was-."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE man was old enough to have known
better.
He made his first mistake in wearing
the wrong clothes (heavy boots and a “sympa-
thetic” coat, etc. Nan knew he had better
clothes) and in making a door-mat of himself,
metaphorically speaking.
Phil was the only “possible” man on the out-
back station where the girl was paying a pro-
tracted visit.
She didn’t bother about him at first, except to
wonder why other men thought so much of him.
Gradually they drew together. The b all gathered
as it rolled.
He had studied women, but he didn’t know quite
enough. Her first letter to him ran—“ I miss
you, miss you dreadfully. Do you remember the
night, the roses, the stars, and tile far-off tinkling
bells helped you to tell me how much you cared?”
Phil answered: “As I draft my sheep across
the dust-stricken plains I curse the inevitable that
binds me. Little love, little love, there is only
you, and ever will be, world without end.”
Then the other man came along. Nan thought
him the grand finale.
Eventually he married another woman.
After his advent her next epistle to Phil began :
“The band is playing the ‘Lucia’ music, softly,
exquisitely, under my window. Last night we had
a little harbor tea. Frank”
Then Phil came down :
“ When the apple reddens,
Never pry,
Lest we lose our Edens,
Eve and I.”
Had he waited a little longer things might have
been different. As it was, that’s where he made
part of his mistake. The girl drew comparisons,
and resented the result.
Phil, being a brave man, had dared the leap.
His pride told him he had failed. He made a
hobby of his blighted life.
The other man wants Nan now, because it’s too
late.
Just at present, she’s playing a somewhat tenta-
tive serpent in the other man’s wife’s Eden.
S.M.

Madame Bodda Pyne, better known as Louisa Pyne,
died some six weeks ago, at the age of 76. In her prime
she had a very high soprano voice of beautiful quality.
Somewhere in the ’so’s, in conjunction with William
Harrison, the finest acting-tenor of his day on the Eng-
lish stage, she established the Pyne and Harrison English
Opera Company. Probably no finer co. has ever existed.
It comprised, besides the two principals, Miss Pilling, an
excellent contralto ; George Perrin and Henry Haigh,
tenors ; Santley and Wharton (the latter once well known
in Australia), baritones ; Henry Gorri and Aynsley Cook,
basses ; and George Honey, who as a lyric comedian had
few, if any, equals. At Drury Lane and Govent Garden
they produced for the first time Balfe’s “ Rose of Castile”
and “ Satanella,” Wallace’s “Lurline,” and many adapta-
tions of foreign works, such as Auber’s “ Crown Dia-
monds,” Donizetti’s “ Figlia del Reggimento,” and
Meyerbeer’s “Dinorah.” In this last the singing of
Santley, as Hoel, created a furore.
“Grevillea” : Someone has said that the word “agent”
is derived from the Latin agere, to do, and employment
agents have done their best to prove the truth of the
remark. For instance, a theatrical agent was approached
by a stage-struck youth I know. Youth was told that the
registration fee was 55., and if a “shop” eventuated a
further charge of 2s. fid. would be made. He was then
sent to a “ manager ” who was taking out a co: for a four
days season only, during which four pieces were to be
played. Terms : Board and lodging, ana ss. per night.
The misguided infant was engaged, and turned up to pay
his 2s. 6d., which was refused, and a demand made for £l,
He paid 15s. Wild horses will not drag from me the sex
of the aforesaid agent.
“C”: Maoriland received a series of shocks from
John Snorting-. The gentle John gave Sunday dis-
courses in the cities on the question of Chinese labor
on the Rand. I attended his meeting in Wellington,
which was rushed by thousands. John made no charge
and paid for the whole show himself. This meeting was a
fairly rowdy one, John supplying most of the rowdiness,
and it was, on the whole, a fair compensation for the non-
arrival of Dowie. Occasionally some rash man would
make an interjection, and John would instantaneously
hurl a large and appalling oratorical rotten egg at bin),
which would splash all over his intellect, amidst the wild
enthusiasm of the audience. The crowd kept on making
interjections,’ for the pleasure of hearing John’s forensic
dead cat, and the eloquent cat and verbose rotten egg
arrived every time with great precision and velocity. The
most striking fact was that the crowd, which was mostly
flabbergasted and hypnotised by the unerring precision
with which the gentle M.L.A. shot at it with his poly-
syllables and alliterations, was fascinated into cheers for
Kruger and wild approval of the Boers. For two hours
John stormed and steamed, and punched holes in the
furniture, and plunged into a flaming jungle of oratory,
and ploughed his way through the forest, hurling up
showers of sparks, and emerging triumphantly with
blazing festoons in his hair. Finally the crowd emptied
itself out, and mopped its brow, firmly convinced that it
had heard the greatest orator of the age, and vainly trying
to express its amazement that the dull old dictionary had
contained such a large quantity of first-rate cursing with-
out its ever having known it.
“C.N.” : Be par. suggesting visit of wrestler Hacken-
schmidt to Australia. When I was in London, eight
weeks ago, t sounded Hackey’s people regarding the
possibility of such a trip, and found that this country
could not raise enough money to tempt him for many
years to come. It is the regular salaries drawn from
three or four music halls simultaneously for nightly
exhibitions that form the Russian's solid earnings. The
gates of matches are mere side issues. The big match
with the Turk attracted only 6000 people, and, although
excellently advertised, nobody outside the regular
athletic crowd took the faintest interest in it. A world’s
championship in an Australian capital would shake every-
thing—even the Presbyterian Assembly—to its founda-
tions, and any speculator would insure the attendance of
15,000 at least, if a suitable arena could be found. But
Australians would soon tire of nightly exhibition shows
on a stage, and Hackenschmidt showing at Manly at
eight, Balmain at nine, and the Tivoli at ten would earn
a very poor salary for his trouble.
Wilfred Draycott, the leading man of Williamson’s new
comedy- co., will remind old theatre-goers of Walter
Everard, who played breezy lover parts in the original
Brough and Boucicault Co. long years ago, before
Titberadge joined it. Draycott’s “jammy” decorative
style is very like Everard’s, though his work shows more
finesse.
The late Nellie Farren (Mrs. Soutar),was the mother of
two or three children before she achieved celebrity as the
“ principal boy ”in Gaiety burlesque. She had been the
ward, it was said, of “Bob” Soutar, and was not more
than 16 when he married her. “ Bob,” a bright, humorous
comedian and a man of some literary ability, was more
than old enough to be Nellie’s father, and as soon as his
young wife became the idol of the johnnies, she—er—played
a free hand with the tacit approval of her liege lord.
1 ‘ Bob ” didn’t mind. Nowadays the name of Nellie Farren
is associated with the Fred Leslie period of Gaiety frivoli-
ties, but her first successes were made in extravaganza of
a much earlier type than “ Little Jack Sheppard,” “ Monte
Oristo,” &c. She played in “ Robert Le Diable,” which
was only an afterpiece, lasting less than an hour. Subse-
quently she was the Ganem to Edward Terry’s Ali Baba
in a panto-burlesque version of “ The Forty Thieves.”
Fred Leslje had not been heard of —he was a schoolboy—
at the time when Nellie Farren began to blaze with dia-
monds. Leslie brought a new kind of talent into the
Gaiety burlesque business, and gave a new lease of popu-
larity to the old established “ principal boy,” whose tricks'
of archness and vivacity had begun to pall a little upon
her loyal admirers. Nellie Farren was not an unrivalled
artist of her class in any one particular. Her singing
-voice was hoarse and ragged ; she never frivolled in a par-
ticularly funny way; her clever step-dancing had no dis-
tinctive character. She was bright, alert, mercurial,
magnetic, and in the words of one of her songs, “a jolly
little chap all round.” Off the stage, as well as on it,
Nellie was always a goer. She ate, drank, and was
merry. In Melbourne she got her first serious attack of
gout. A few years later a stroke of paralysis befell the
imprudent “ Queen of Burlesque,” and then her London
subjects gave her a benefit which yielded £lO,OOO.
There is deep anmity between the German and Czech
subjects of Austria. It culminated recently at Lintz in
a brutal attack on Kubelik, the famous Bohemian violinist.
The Germans stormed a concert at which he was playing,
and stoned him. Kubelik manfully continued fiddling for
some time, but was finally compelled to retreat to his
hotel, followed by a howling mob. And yet the Germans
pose as a musical people, which, as a matter of fact, they
are not.
“J.F.D.”: Attended a silver coin show in a suburban
hall a few nights back. Luring the intermission the villain
brought round a basket of peanuts and retailed them
amongst the audienee. I bought a penny bag, and while
waiting for change complimented him on his aeting.
‘Just wait till the next scene,” he replied, “I’m going
to murder that bloomin’ dandy.” He did it, too, and the
corpse went out of the piece and hunted small boys away
from the door during the rest of th# performance.
